if we stay swimming here forever we will never be free

by leftlanden

Summary

Agatha could NEVER forgive Rio for taking her son. …But what if she did? What would that take?

The story starts shortly after Agatha’s canonical death, in a world broken by Rio’s rage. It journeys through the past, in flashbacks and memory spells, to see why the love AND the chasms between Agatha and Rio run so deep. With the help of a team of agents-turned-friends from Shield and a few familiar faces, Agatha and Rio find ways to understand each other like never before and, ultimately, claw their way out from under centuries of conflict, emerging to the possibility of an exciting new beginning.

Canon-compliant with WandaVision, Agatha All Along, and Agents of SHIELD. Diverges a bit from the greater universe of Marvel lore, especially with respect to Rio’s origins and cosmic nature. While characters from AoS appear in the story, you don’t need to be familiar with the show to understand this story or their role in it.

Notes

1. In the 1700's flashbacks and memories, the characters speak like their modern selves. I was more interested in getting their voices right than having era-appropriate dialogue. It would have been less fun for me, and writing fanfiction is for fun. If that's the sort of thing that bothers you, you'll probably be bothered here.

2. Rio temporarily has a penis when Nicky is conceived.

3. There is a little bit of blood/knifeplay. Not that much because I'm a wimp, but it's there.

4. There are some scenes of Rio's reapings with implied suicide and child death.

5. If you want to know more about any of the plot and tags to see whether this is something you want to read, just ask! I'm happy to tell you more.

Chapter 1: Agatha

October 2024

 

The old man was about to die.  

 

The doctors had been preparing the family for this difficult time for months.  In recent weeks, they had done so increasingly urgently.  Five days ago, they had concluded that the moment had arrived when there was nothing more they could do to keep his body alive. They discharged him from the hospital and sent him to spend his remaining days surrounded by the warm familiarity of his home and the love of his family.  

 

At least, that had been the plan. 

 

It had been almost 48 hours since the hospice nurse, Margot, had departed his house for what she, drawing upon her long experience, had assumed would be the last time.  She left the soon-to-be-grieving widow, children, and grandchildren with soft, comforting words on her lips. Words meant to help them accept that it would be only a matter of hours before the man was lost to them.  As she descended their front stairs and trudged through the cold drizzle to her car, she uttered a small prayer for his soul. She had learned never to look back at the house. Tears would come every time.   

 

To everyone’s confusion, what was expected did not occur.  

 

Today, the man still lay clinging to life in a modified hospital bed that had been wheeled into the small library at the back of his house, his favorite room in his home.  It was full of used candles; some were prayer candles sent by his close friends from church.  Some were far less sacred (purchased by his daughters at the Target down the street) but had burned with beloved scents of citrus and spice.  Now, only traces of the wax still remained, the empty glass jars coated with a layer of soot, the burnt wicks standing naked and charred inside.  

 

The record player on the small antique table in the corner sat still and silent.  The dying man, a connoisseur of classical music, had a shelf packed with favorite albums. All the best ones had now been played. Nobody had any energy to make another music choice.   

 

The man’s son and daughter-in-law had dropped everything and taken the first flight from Chicago two nights ago.  One of his grandsons had driven all night from his dorm room in upstate New York.  The man’s younger sister, his only remaining sibling of the five he had grown up with, old and frail herself, had used the phone book to call a taxi to get herself across town to his house.  They, and a dozen others, dozed on chairs and loveseats, or stared, faces blank from sleep deprivation, at the dreary drizzle falling outside.   

 

The dying man’s wife Beth, her rotund, arthritic body aching with activity and grief, refused to do anything but busy herself in the kitchen, cooking batch after batch of homemade potato soup to feed the guests who unexpectedly needed to be sustained for days. 

 

It was an entire, endless day ago now when she had bent down and whispered in her husband’s ear, when no one else was listening.  “It’s okay to go, Teddy,” she’d said.  “I love you.  Don’t stay here and suffer for us.”  

 

But still the old man lay in the bed, breathing shallowly, heart still beating weakly, but evenly.  It was impossible to say what level of awareness he had of the world around him, or what might comprise his internal world.  He seemed peaceful, but it was beginning to weigh on some of his loved ones that by now he hadn’t had food, water, or pain relief in two full days.  

 

Back at the hospice facility, Margot the nurse blinked in surprise when she got a call from the man’s home number.  Surely the time for her help had passed for this family?  But as she spoke with Teddy’s granddaughter on the other end of the line, anxiety bloomed in her chest.  

 

“Theresa,” she said, knocking on the door of the office of the head hospice nurse after putting Teddy’s granddaughter on hold, “Theodore Murphy is still alive.”

 

Theresa, to Margot’s surprise, reacted only with pursed lips.  “That’s unexpected.”  

 

“Did the doctors make a mistake?” Margot asked with a shaky voice.  “Did we?”

 

Theresa shook her head.  “No, I remember his chart.  He had two days, at best.”

 

“Should we - should we arrange transport back to the hospital?” Margot asked.  “I think we need to resume nutrition and hydration.”

 

Theresa shook her head again.  “Margot, I don’t think we can do that.”

 

Margot furrowed her brow.  “I understand it’s unconventional, but --”

 

Theresa held up her hand.  “I mean that we literally can’t.”

 

“But, why?” 

 

“I’ve been told the hospitals are full.  We won’t be able to get him a bed.” 

 

“Full?” Margot shook her head in surprise.  “Has there been a Covid spike?”

 

“No,” Theresa said, swallowing hard.  “It’s my understanding that there are no vacant beds because there’s been no. . . turnover.”

 

“You mean? ”

 

“I mean, according to the ICU nurses I spoke with, it seems that no one has died at Trenton County Memorial for over two weeks now.”  Theresa sniffled and smoothed her skirt.  “We’ll have to manage this on a case-by-case basis.”

 

The anxiety in Margot’s chest threatened to expand into fear.  In her line of work, death was inevitable.  Constant.  Something that happened daily, or more.  Two weeks without any at all?  It felt unimaginable.  Surreal.

 

But what else was there to do but her job?

 

“I’ll take them what they need,” Margot said.  She went to the supply closet to retrieve IV bags and several doses of morphine.  

***

 

The young woman sat nervously in the consultation office of the obstetrician she’d been visiting regularly for the past eight months.  Happy to be fully dressed, warm and dignified again after several hours of being poked and prodded by medical staff, she hummed lightly, soothing both herself and, she hoped, her daughter-to-be.  She rubbed her rounded belly absently, and was reassured by the vigorous kicks that registered against her palms.

 

What was meant to be a routine 8-month checkup had turned into extra bloodwork, an unscheduled ultrasound, and a fetal stress test.  Every time she’d asked, the nurses had repeated some variation of how they “just wanted to make sure everything was going as expected.”

 

Of course, the trouble with that response was, it clearly meant that something was not going as expected.  

 

“Hello, Rena!” The middle-aged doctor greeted her cheerfully as she breezed into the room and sat down at her desk across from the young woman.  “How are you feeling today?”

“I was feeling great when I got here this morning,” Rena said.  “Now, I’m nervous as hell.”

 

“Well I have news that should set your mind at ease,” Dr. Karro said, folding her hands in front of her on her desk.  “All of your blood work came back normal, and baby passed the fetal stress test with flying colors.”

 

“That’s good,” Rena hedged, feeling cautiously optimistic.  “But what was all the extra testing for in the first place?”

 

“So, when we first measured your fundal height at the beginning of your appointment, we noticed that it hadn’t changed since you were here two weeks ago.  That, combined with the fact that you didn’t put on any weight, made us want to be extra sure that baby girl in there is thriving.”

 

“Okay.  And, is she?”

 

“Every test that we have says that she’s healthy.” 

 

“Is there a ‘but’ there?” Rena asked.  Was she imagining it, or was that a rather oblique answer to her question?   

 

“The ultrasound did confirm our suspicions that she hasn’t grown in the past two weeks,” Dr. Karro said, her voice a little less energetic this time.  

 

“Well, I read in the books you recommended that the baby stops growing in the last couple of weeks.  So, isn’t that normal?”

 

“They do stop growing in length during the final month sometimes,” the doctor affirmed.  “But typically, they keep putting on weight.  They spend the final month building those cute little chubby bodies that we love to snuggle when they’re born.”

 

“Am I not eating enough?” Rena asked, tension rising in her voice.  “Should I take more vitamins, what?”

 

“You should continue to eat healthy foods when you’re hungry, and keep taking the prenatal vitamins you’re already taking.  And Rena, I want you to relax.  Even if she doesn’t get any bigger than she is right now, chances are excellent that she’ll come out happy and healthy next month.”

 

“Well, how common is this?  Have you seen it before?”

 

“Yes.  I have seen it before.”  Dr. Karro smiled.  “Stop by the front desk on your way out.  Let’s have you back next week to check in on her, okay?”

 

“All right,” Rena said, nursing a persistent uneasiness in her stomach.  “Thank you, doctor.”

 

When Rena closed the doctor’s door behind her, Dr. Karro’s manufactured smile quickly faded.  She removed her glasses and rubbed her graying temples.  

 

What she hadn’t mentioned to Rena  -- because really, there was no reason to frighten a woman who almost certainly would have a healthy baby in a few weeks -- was that every case she had seen of this halted fetal growth that she’d seen before?  It had happened in the past three weeks.  

 

***

 

It was 7AM on a Saturday morning, and the Westview Farmers’ Market was springing up in the town square, just as it did every weekend from May through October.  Neighbors pulled up their trucks and SUVs loaded up with backyard veggies and homegrown honey, canned fruits and hand-crafted bouquets.  Farmers from the outskirts of town sometimes even made the early morning trek to offer surplus crops, fresh meats, or eggs.  

 

“Hey neighbors!”

 

John Collins uttered his trademark greeting as Sarah and Harold Proctor and their daughter Maura pulled their minivan up next to his booth.  They waved as they clambored out of the vehicle.  

 

“Need help unloading?” John asked, peering over their shoulders looking for their usual baskets of fresh tomatoes and zucchini in the back of the van.  

 

“Not much to unload today, I’m afraid” Harold said.  “Seems my last harvest of the season doesn’t want to grow up.  Must be that cold snap we had last week.”

 

“Huh,” John said.

 

“We’re just here to offer out the rest of Mrs. Davis’s azalea starts,” Harold continued.  “We know she’d have wanted folks to have them.  What about you, anything from those blueberry bushes today?” 

 

“Nah,” John said, “I got the same problem. Not too much grew since last week.  To be honest, I’m only here today hoping to get some eggs from the folks from past the county line.  Supermarket shelves have been pretty cleaned out the past couple of weeks.”

 

“Supply chain issues, I imagine,” Harold said.  

 

“Must be,” John said.  The three exchanged uneasy glances.  

 

“I’m sure it had nothing to do with the, um,” Sarah said, gesturing vaguely toward the sky.

 

“Naww, no, definitely not,” John said, while Harold simultaneously reassured them all, “Oh yeah no, it’s just a cold snap for sure.”

 

They fell silent again for a beat.

 

“I mean, the sky was only green for like fifteen minutes,” Harold added.  

 

“Definitely something minor,” John said.  “Probably unrelated.”  

 

“And, um. Nobody has seen Agnes, still?” Sarah asked.

 

“Agatha,” John corrected her.  “And no.  Not since that night.”  He paused and shrugged.   “Seems like we might finally be witch-free around here.”

 

The three of them looked around in silence at the mostly-empty town square.

 

*********

 

Zephyr 18 uncloaked at 800ft and began its vertical descent directly over 2804 Sherwood Drive in Westview, New Jersey.  

 

During her descent, the pilot watched the scene below with mild curiosity.  To most small town residents, the arrival of a bona fide spacecraft in their streets would be a noteworthy event, evoking at least some pointing and shouting, and possibly some gathering in the streets. The citizens of Westview, if they noticed at all, merely glanced up and frowned in annoyance. Kind of like they were primarily concerned with whether whatever-this-was would be done in time for them to carry out their dinner plans. The man doing yard work at 2806 Sherwood Drive put up his arm to block the craft’s exhaust from his face as it landed, but didn’t bother to put down his pruning shears.  

 

When the craft was safely parked in front of the house, Monica Rambeau emerged from inside through the spot where a front door seemed like it should be, and extended her hand to the pilot and two other agents as they exited down the ramp.  

 

“Melinda May,” the pilot said by way of introduction, removing her sunglasses and shaking Monica’s outstretched hand.

 

“Oh, I know,” Monica said with a wide, warm smile.  “Who else could land a multi-billion dollar spacecraft in a suburban driveway and not damage a single chrysanthemum?  It’s great to meet you, Agent May.”

 

She turned her attention and her outstretched palm to the other two agents, who introduced themselves.

 

“Agent Daisy Johnson, SWORD Galactic.”

 

“Doctor Jemma Simmons, formerly of SHIELD Biotechnology.”

 

“Thanks so much for coming, Agents. I’m Captain Monica Rambeau of SWORD Operations.   How was your flight?”

 

“It was actually so fun,” Daisy said.  “The three of us haven’t been on the same mission in years.  It was kind of like a college reunion up there.”

 

Jemma laughed in agreement, and elbowed Daisy playfully.  Agent May remained stone-faced, and Daisy could feel the “ be professional ” emanating from her wordlessly.  

 

“She’s the stern but loveable professor,” Daisy said to Monica, jerking her thumb towards May. “She’s more fun than she lets on. Anyway, so this is the Westview of the Westview Anomaly, huh?  Where Wanda Maximoff magicked up an entire city?” Agent Johnson held her hands up in front of her and rolled her wrists like she was casting a spell.  

 

“More like she rewrote an existing one,” Monica said.  “But yep. This is it.”

 

“I read your file,” Agent May said to Captain Rambeau.  “You were instrumental in peacefully resolving that standoff.  And since then you’ve spent time in the field with Captain Marvel?”

 

“Oh, that’s awesome,” Daisy said.  “I’ve always wanted to work with Cap. The other Cap.  We’ve never crossed paths, even though we both work in space.  It’s big up there..  You’ll have to tell me how you got that assignment.”

 

“With Captain Marvel? Well, she’s kind of my mom, so,” Monica said. 

 

“Wait, what?” Daisy asked.

 

“Long story.”

 

“My point was,” Agent May continued, “Whatever brought you back here after such high profile missions and reconvened our team must be a significant threat.  What are we dealing with, Captain?”

 

Monica sighed.  “It does have global implications, I’m afraid.  Why don’t we head inside, and I’ll brief you?”

 

While the outside of 2804 Sherwood Drive looked like a typical yet charming suburban two-story home (sans the missing front door), the inside was anything but.  A pile of debris, comprised mostly of splintered wood and broken glass, had been swept haphazardly to one side of the kitchen.  The doors to both the backyard and the basement were barricaded and marked with yellow crime scene tape, and through the back windows the agents could see a yard that looked like it had been decimated by a small, precise tornado.  Beyond that stood a greenhouse with a shattered roof.  

 

The living room furniture had been pushed aside to make room for a small operations and communications center where several SWORD agents buzzed about.  Among them quietly sat a tall, slender, solemn-looking boy of 15 or 16 with dark curly hair. He raised his gaze and nodded at Captain Rambeau as she led the team of newcomers inside.  

 

Monica, Melinda, Daisy, and Jemma settled in at a small, makeshift conference table in the corner of the living room/temporary SWORD base.  

 

“I know you’re all wondering why you’re here,” Monica began.  “Your official team was disbanded years ago, and you’ve all begun new chapters of your careers.  But we needed the three of you specifically.  And that’s because about a decade ago, your SHIELD team was the first one, at least that we know of, to encounter a poorly-understood force that is unfortunately highly relevant to our current situation.”

 

“What force is that?” May asked.  

 

“Well,” Monica said, shifting uncomfortably in her chair, “For lack of a better term: dark magic.”

 

“Dark magic?” Daisy repeated.  “Oh, you mean like vengeance demons that can literally drag people to hell?” 

 

“Like a magical book that can send people into parallel spirit dimensions and imbue androids with godlike powers of creation?” Jemma clarified.

 

“Like literal ghost possessions?” May asked.  

 

“Yes,” Monica said, grimacing slightly.  “Things exactly like that.”

 

The Agents agreed in unison.  

 

“Yeah, I remember that.”

 

“Yup, we got you.”

 

“That’s us, yes.”

 

“All right,” Monica said.  “So, then next let me tell you what we’re up against.  As you may know, starting about three weeks ago, the worldwide death rate plummeted. It’s been in the news for a few days now. But what you might not have been told yet is that we’re not talking about some remarkable-but-harmless statistical blip here.  We’re talking, no human being on Earth, as far as our data can tell, has died in the past two weeks.”  

 

Daisy and May stared at Monica in stunned silence. 

 

“That’s statistically impossible,” Jemma said.  

 

“And yet.”  Monica said, and then continued, “Hospitals are overflowing because people are still getting sick, but morgues and funeral homes are sitting empty.  We’ve tried our best to keep the extent of it out of the press until we could look into it, but it’s affecting millions of people at this point, and we have no idea how to explain it.”

 

“That can’t happen at random.  Something must be causing it,” Jemma said in a near-whisper.   

 

“Is it some kind of anti-Snap event?  Something to do with the Blip?” May asked.  

 

“Oh, yeah,” Daisy agreed.  “Maybe some kind of universal course-correction after so much death happened all at once?”

 

“Our physicists looked into that,” Monica said. “We thought it could be some kind of reverberation in spacetime affecting the cycle of life and death.  But when they looked for signs of a disturbance like that, they found nothing. Not to mention, this is affecting everyone on Earth, those who got blipped and those who didn’t alike.  And technically that wasn’t a mass death, per se.  The people who got blipped by Thanos, like myself, didn’t die; for those few years we never existed except in people’s memories.  But here’s the kicker: we know now that this is only happening on Earth.  No other planet of sentient beings that we’re in contact with is experiencing the same issues.  And they all endured the Blip, too.”  

 

“So the life force on Earth, and only on Earth, has been somehow disrupted?” Jemma clarified.

 

“Exactly.  And it’s funny you should say life force, Dr. Simmons,” Monica said, “Because there’s another part to this story.  Growth is slowing down, too.  Globally.”

 

“Growth,” Jemma repeated.  “What kind of growth?”

 

“Everything,” Monica said.  “Crops have stagnated in the fields.  Flowers have stopped blooming.”

 

She swallowed hard and her voice strained as she spoke.

 

“Babies in the womb have stopped developing.  Yet the birth rate has stayed the same. In other words, they’re still being born.  So far it’s been mostly okay because it’s only been a couple of weeks, but. . .” her voice trailed off.  

 

The three Agents lost several shades of color from their faces.

 

“As you can imagine, if we don’t resolve this soon, the scale of human suffering will be virtually indescribable.”

 

They sat in silence for a moment, digesting this information.  

 

“So we’re talking, ultimately, about an extinction-level event, then,” Daisy said.  “Nothing dies, but nothing grows either, including our food. And our offspring.”

 

“For humans, at least, yes. Our analysts are working on global projections for the impacts to the biosphere as a whole.”  

 

Daisy sat back in her chair, and the three former SHIELD agents exchanged looks.  

 

“Well, it’s been a while since we’ve saved the world together,” Jemma said. “So Captain Rambeau, you said the source of the problem, it has to do with magic?  Dark magic?”

 

“Sword thinks so.  That’s why they brought me in, due to my familiarity with the Westview situation.  Besides the Maximoff Event you all know about, there’s been as another, smaller anomaly here in Westview. 

“According to eyewitness accounts,” she said, distributing file folders around the table, “About three weeks ago there was what can only be described as another, well, magical conflict.  People reported weather and electromagnetic disturbances, some property destruction.  As well as those tell-tale pretty colored lightning bolts coming out of people’s hands.”

 

“That could be inhumans or aliens,” May said, flipping open the incident folder.  “I know the location’s history is suggestive, but it doesn’t have to be magic, does it?”

 

“We also have an eyewitness who’s come forward with his story.  Someone who was there and says he knows the parties who were involved.  His testimony is in your folders, but he’s also here to speak with you personally.”

 

Monica stood and motioned for the quiet, curly-haired boy to come and join them at the table.  

 

“Agents Johnson, Simmons, and May, this is Billy Maximoff.”

 

“I’m sorry, did you say Maximoff?” May asked.  

 

“I’m Wanda’s son.  Sort of.”

 

“Wow, y’all got some famous parents around here,” Daisy said.  

 

“And you were here when this recent anomaly occurred?” Jemma asked him.  “Do you agree that the zero death rate and the declining primary productivity of the biosphere are magical in nature?”

 

Billy shook his head uncertainly.  “I’m not sure, entirely, if you’d call it magic. It’s not a hex the same way Wanda’s town was. It’s -- hold on, let me just -- ” Billy interrupted himself mid-sentence.  He seemed unable to keep his eyes from darting to Agent May as he spoke. He turned to face her. 

 

“Wanda and Vision created my twin brother and me during the week of the original anomaly.  We were born and aged to ten years old during the course of that week.  When Wanda folded the anomaly, I reincarnated into this body where I’ve been living for the past three years.”

 

Everyone stared at Billy in silence.

 

“Did you just read my mind?” Agent May asked.

 

“I could hear your skepticism,” Billy replied.  “I’m sorry, I didn’t mean to be invasive.  You’re quiet, but your mind is the loudest one in here.”

 

“Well, that’s unnerving,” May muttered.  

 

“If you wouldn’t call it magic,” Jemma asked, eager to get back to the task at hand, “What would you call it?”

 

“It’s Death,” Billy said, turning back to Jemma.  “She stopped doing her job.”

 

“She?  Are you saying Death is a person?” Daisy asked.  

 

Billy nodded.  “I don’t know exactly what she is, like, cosmically.  But I knew her in the form of a woman named Rio.  She was a witch who specializes in green craft. You know, growth and decay.”

 

“Okay, so.  Why did this 'Rio' stop doing her job?” Daisy asked.  

 

Billy looked at Monica, who nodded.

 

“Based on what I saw, I think it was either rage or grief, maybe both.  She lost the woman she’d loved for centuries.  Her name was Agatha Harkness.”

 

“Agatha Harkness,” Daisy repeated.  “Why do I know that name?”

 

“She was present for the Westview Anomaly,” Monica said. “Her magic was a complicating factor the entire time, but we didn’t know it until she tried to take Wanda’s powers by force right as we were trying to orchestrate a peaceful resolution.”

 

“Her magic?” Jemma asked.  “So, she’s a witch?”

 

Monica sighed. “Straight outta Salem.”




*****

 

Salem, 1683

 

“Agatha!  Agatha, come back here!”

 

Agatha, tearing through the forest near her home as fast as her little legs would carry her, tried to stifle her giggles as she ran.  She realized it was probably slowing her down to be laughing so much, but she was just too tickled with herself to stop.

 

Her small hands were full of treasures. Ten minutes ago they had belonged to the other children she’d been playing with, but they were hers now.  (Though, admittedly, there was some disagreement on that front; hence the chasing and yelling.)  

 

She scampered down a small, rocky embankment, and there it was!  Her tree. Checking over her shoulder to make sure she was out of her friends’ sightlines, she ducked inside the small cubby hole that was hollowed out of its trunk. 

She’d found this particular tree a couple of months ago, on one of her first walks outside in the brand new spring of 1683. By some good fortune of Agatha’s, this tree had grown up with a hollow space inside, providing just enough room for an eight-year-old girl to hide -- along with a few of her most prized possessions. She had cleared out the dead bark and leaves, laid down a blanket, and hung furs and a small lantern inside to make it into a cozy hiding space.  For whenever she needed that sort of thing.  

 

Once safely inside, Agatha dropped her new possessions into her lap, murmured a few select words -- she didn’t know what they were, but she knew what they would do -- and moved her little hand just so, the way the older ladies of the coven always did when they were casting a spell to hide something.  Obediently, a magical barrier appeared, glimmering just outside of her tree, obscuring her from outside eyes.  Now she could thoroughly evaluate her new treasures while her playmates called her name fruitlessly outside, their running footsteps passing back and forth mere feet from where she sat.  (A fact which tickled her endlessly.)

 

Let’s see.  There was a red ribbon -- like new, she noted with approval, except for a bit of fray at the ends.  There was a polished stone about the size of an acorn, smooth and sparkling with an alluring bluish tint when she held it in the ray of sunlight that squeezed in through the cracks between the furs that made her door.  There was a tarnished silver coin, and a small wooden whistle, smooth from being gripped by small hands for years and years. And most excitingly, what was undoubtedly her best acquisition yet: a small, bound book of children’s poems. Not simply more boring psalms or hymns, but playful, rhyming stories about things like horses and stars and naughty children and winter walks on leaves and ice! She tucked the other objects into a small crevice behind one of the hanging furs inside for safe-keeping and opened the poetry book.    

 

So charmed by the rhyming lines was young Agatha that she only heard the grown-ups’ footsteps with a few heartbeats to spare.  Quick as a flash, she stashed the treasured book beneath a handful of leaves kept inside just for this purpose.  She reached up, her small hand feeling around to locate the wooden whistle by touch.  

 

In that instant, she felt the spark of her obscuration spell go out, like a cold prickle in the skin of her fingertips and forearms.  Like a fire quenched.  

 

“Agatha!” 

 

Her mother’s voice bellowed from a few feet outside her tree, and Agatha’s heart sank.  It wasn’t just any grown-up.  Her mother, of all people, had found her perfect hiding spot.    

 

Agatha, her options limited in this moment, stepped out into the open.  But she held her chin high and her fists clenched, one of them wrapped tight around her new wooden whistle. Besides her mother, standing there was Goody Adams (though Agatha had always found that title dubious for pretty much everyone who used it), who was a member of her mother’s coven (which was, of course, a secret), and several of the friends she had played rolling hoops with that afternoon. The children stood still, clearly chastened by adults’ opinions on this situation, and staring at the ground to avoid meeting Agatha’s gaze.  

 

Agatha knew her mother couldn’t reprimand her for using a spell in front of the non-coven children, but “stealing” other children’s things was fair game for yelling no matter who their mothers were.  

 

“Agatha, did you take Margaret’s ribbon?  And Joseph’s stone?  And Harriet’s coin?”

 

Agatha pressed her lips together.  She would make her mother ask at least once more.  

 

“Agatha, answer me!”

 

“I also took Samuel’s whistle,” she announced as her answer, doing her best to make her small, young voice match her mother’s commanding tone.  She held the whistle in full view in her outstretched palm.  “But I didn’t take them, Mother, I won them.  We were playing a game.”

 

“Quiet,” Evanora barked.  “Give Samuel his whistle back and then go inside that filthy tree and get the other objects to return.”

 

“No,” Agatha said.  In her mind she added, “ I won’t, ”  but the words didn’t make it to her lips.  

 

“It’s okay, Miss Harkness,” little Samuel said softly.  “She won it fair and square.  Agatha always wins, but she usually gives our stuff back after a couple of days.”

 

“Yes, she gives almost everything back,” Harriet agreed. “It’s part of the game.”

 

“She will give it back NOW,” Evanora said.

 

Agatha dropped the whistle on the ground in front of her and stomped on it as hard as she could, shattering it into splinters.  

 

“Sorry, Samuel,” she called over her shoulder as she passed him in a full sprint, running madly, deeper into the forest and away from her mother.  Agatha knew her mother couldn’t use magic to stop her in view of these children.  Her goal was to get Evanora as far away from that tree as possible in the meantime.  

 

Agatha was young, fast, and full of energy, but her mother’s legs were twice as long.  And Agatha was almost-but-not-quite too big for her mother to lift her off the ground and carry her, and so she soon found herself elevated above the forest floor, held fast against her mother’s hip.  

 

After a struggle, she kicked herself free and led her mother in circles through the forest. She knew all the most treacherous, rocky, rooted, and muddy terrain. During this distraction, when she was sure her mother couldn’t hear, she used an incantation to move Her Tree a short ways deeper into the forest.  She also spun it around so the cubby hole wasn’t visible from the direction of the village.  

 

It should now look like any other tree.  Her mother couldn’t search them all.  

 

Her hiding place with all its treasures safely protected, Agatha stopped running.  Time to surrender before things got any worse for her than they already would be.  

 

With the other children now dispersed, Evanora bound Agatha’s hands together and her lips closed, and shoved her roughly in the direction of home.  

 

As they walked, Evanora berated Agatha on Agatha’s various evils - theft, mischief, disrespect, and worst of all, impermissible magic. Not impervious to her mother’s mean-spiritedness just yet, Agatha felt the tears fall down her cheeks.  

 

It’s okay , she said again and again in her head.  My tree is safe. I have a book to read tomorrow.  

 

It’s okay, it’s okay, it’s okay.   



***

Salem, 1688

 

The tired old Puritan ladies that led the daily lessons knew that Agatha Harkness, age 13, was always reading something when she was supposed to be listening.  And whatever it was, it was always irrelevant to what she was supposed to be learning. 

 

They let her.  That perpetual book on her knees, partially obscured by her desk, kept her occupied --  and therefore quiet.  

 

Besides, if they never looked at the cover, they could pretend they thought it was the Bible.  (It wasn’t.)  

 

Whatever it actually was today, Agatha must have finished it or become bored by it, because she had apparently decided to participate in class. 

 

“I have a question,” she said when there was a lull in the lesson.

 

The five other girls in the room turned to look at her.  Agatha was slumped at her desk in the back left corner of the room, cheek resting heavily in her hand, as though this experience was barely keeping her awake. Which was, of course, more or less true.

 

“Go ahead, Agatha,” one of the teachers sighed, because she couldn’t think fast enough of a reason to deny her.  

 

Agatha waited until all the eyes in the room had settled on her.  “If God is omniscient,” she said,  “Why do we have to pray?  Shouldn’t he know what we want and just give it to us?”

 

It wasn’t the most original question the teachers had ever been asked in this classroom, and they had cultivated a certain patience for answering ones just like it.  But there was something about the bored confidence dripping from the young Miss Harkness’s voice that made any answer they could give feel like it had already fallen flat.  

 

The two teachers exchanged glances, and then the older of them spoke.  “God learns what’s in our hearts by hearing the things we choose to ask for, Agatha.”

 

“Doesn’t he know that already? What’s in our hearts?” She enunciated the last word so strongly it left no doubt she was mocking the very question.  

 

“He does,” the teacher sighed.  

 

Agatha shrugged her shoulders dismissively and held up one hand, palm up, in front of her.   “Seems inefficient, then.”

 

Agatha heard a couple of the other girls in class titter.  Her posture straightened a little.  

 

To Agatha’s own surprise, no one stopped her from talking at this point.  So stop she did not.  

 

“If he knows we’re going to ask for a good harvest every year, why doesn’t he just plow the fields for us.  Then the rest of us can do something more interesting.”

 

More laughter, poorly stifled from behind her classmates hands across their mouths.  

 

“God knows hard work is good for us.”

 

“But if I’m the one doing the work to push a plow in the field,” Agatha continued, “Why doesn’t anyone pray to me?”  

 

The laughter in the room got a little bolder.  

 

“Saint Agatha,” one of the girls muttered, inspiring even more laughter at the already-recognizable irony.  

 

“That’s enough,” the teacher said. (Though it seemed to Agatha that this attempt to end the conversation was half-hearted at best; Agatha began preparing her next outrageous remark.)

 

“Agatha should be sent home,” said Emily, interrupting Agatha’s performance.  This girl was perhaps the most annoying person Agatha had encountered thus far in her young life.  

 

“Maybe if you pray real hard you can make me disappear,” Agatha shot back.  

 

“Send her home,” Emily said, turning to the teachers.  “Or do you allow blasphemers in your classroom now?”

 

The classroom fell silent.  Well, that was that.  Agatha knew it, and rolled her eyes aggressively.  Emily, whose father was a reverend, had said one of those “special bad words” that no adult ever seemed to have any capacity to defend against.  Blasphemy . They all turned into sad, pathetic, cowards at any hint of that word sharing air with them, as though the sound of it would burn their ears with imaginary hellfire.

 

It was a dark magical incantation if Agatha had ever heard one.  

 

Witch , Agatha mouthed at Emily as she gathered her things and walked out of the classroom before she could be ordered to do so, her shoulders squared.  



Agatha’s posture was less proud as she trudged home.  As mind-numbing as it was, the schoolroom was generally a more pleasant place to spend the afternoon than her mother’s house.  And now she would have to explain why she was home early.  There was no use lying about it; her mother could read it directly from her mind if she wanted to.  

 

Like god reading a prayer , she thought glumly. 

 

Her mother’s initial admonitions were predictable, which made them more tedious than frightening. That meant Agatha could probably get in a few return barbs before she was bound, or silenced, or blasted.  This was at least slightly more interesting an experience.

 

“You need to hold your tongue in front of the other children,” Evanora spat at her.  “You put us all in danger.”

 

“Why do we care what these Puritans think?” Agatha asked, for the hundredth time at least.  

 

The answer was always the same.  

 

“Because we need them to survive.”

 

“Why? We’re more powerful than they are.  I’ve never seen a miracle at their hands, yet I have hundreds in waiting, right here.”  Agatha held up her left hand and purple wisps, manifestations of her illicit magic, danced between her fingers.  She smirked as she watched her mother’s temperature rise. 

 

“Why do you let them rule you when they hate what you are?”   

 

“Put that away!” Evanora yelled, and then quenched it before Agatha had a chance to do it herself.  “And use the correct hand.  I’ve told you, Agatha.  Individually, we may have power.  As a coven, we may have power.  But it is their society.  They could banish us, deprive us, even kill us.”

 

“Let them try.”

 

“And with that arrogance, they will succeed.  They have done it before.” 

 

“Better than participating in this pointless society of pointless men,” Agatha taunted.  “And the pointless women who could put an end to it, but instead play by these meaningless rules.”

 

“Poisonous child,” Evanora spat at Agatha.  Agatha found herself being whisked up the short staircase to her lofted bedroom (via magic - her mother couldn’t lift her any more). Her hands and ankles, as usual, were bound.  She landed roughly on her knees on the wooden floorboards at the foot of her bed.  

 

“Good night, mom,” she smiled wryly as her bedroom door closed and locked behind her.  

 

Used to maneuvering her body in this condition by now, she got to her feet and then flopped onto her bed.  She would wait here until she heard her mother leave or become distracted, then she’d magick herself some food and one or two of the forbidden spell books her mother kept under her floorboards, so she could do some reading before bed.  

 

As she waited there for an opportune moment, she ruminated on the day.  It certainly hadn’t been all bad.  The girls in her class -- not Emily, but the ones who had laughed at her calculated blasphemy -- it’s possible they weren’t lost causes like she had assumed.  Agatha hadn’t been allowed to spend time with the other children of her mother’s coven for several years now.  What did you call a coven that didn’t have magic?  Hmm.  Agatha thought maybe she would find out.  




***

Salem, 1691

 

Trying to have friends who didn’t have witchcraft, it turned out, was an exercise in frustration.  By the summer of her 16th year, Agatha had reached the limits of her ability to humor anyone whose greatest joy was to host afternoon tea and gossip in a sewing circle about their prospects with the sons of virtuous families.  

 

Her growing magical powers had no outlet, and neither did she. 

 

The oppressive boredom begat a powerful restlessness.  She had few options, but she did have youth and motivation.  She could walk, and so walk she did -- for hours at a time.  The surrounding farms and scattered small villages of the outskirts were not unlike Salem proper in any meaningful way, but at least they housed people she hadn’t yet become disappointed in (and who were not yet wary of her).  

 

She would leave at night, after her mother had gone to bed.  She’d pick a direction -- inland, south, or toward the coast -- and walk for a while, sleeping a bit in the forest, hidden from human and animal predators alike by her spells.  By day, she would explore any new settlements she came upon. A teenage girl traveling alone was unusual, but it tended to inspire curiosity and pity rather than suspicion --   especially if she used the words “lost” or “hungry.”  

 

Her mother, by this point, was a thorn but not much more, at least in practical matters. Usually Agatha would depart her visiting spot in time to make it back home by dinner time to avoid the hassle of Evanora’s abrasive complaints; occasionally, if she was particularly enjoying a place, she accepted an offer to spend the night in someone’s spare room or barn, and dealt with the consequences of her mother’s ire when she returned.   

 

The coven families, if there were any in a given village or camp, were easily recognizable to Agatha by their insularity and telling ratio of women to men.  Agatha would make conversation with young girls in the market or while happening to casually pass by a family home while a teenaged girl was sweeping the front steps.  She’d reveal her own magic to anyone who seemed promising, and the girl would invariably reveal hers back.  

 

By the end of the summer, the radius of a four hour walk had yielded her four other teenagers who were defiant, bored, or curious enough to reliably do what Agatha told them to.  Which was, of course, Agatha’s primary criterion.  The necessary obedience included sometimes following her into the woods in the middle of the night so they could cement their “coven” by learning and casting group spells.  

 

Truth be told, Agatha was not sure she was ready to use the c-word for this bunch just yet.  She was still evaluating their qualifications.  

 

There was Cora Bartlett, who was 15 and the third youngest daughter of a farmer who had twelve of them.  She’d won Agatha over by harvesting corn via magic in broad daylight while she sat under a willow tree daydreaming aloud to Agatha about moving to Boston and living in her own room where no one “from here” would ever find her.  

 

Next was Sokanon Sachim, a Wampanoag girl of 16 who had managed to track Agatha in the forest near her tribe’s settlement, after noticing her magicking up dinner in the forest one night.  Sokanon could perform many of the same magical feats as Agatha, but did so by using completely different incantations and movements.  This delighted Agatha, who had never learned of such a thing.  In the beginning, Sokanon knew only a few English words and Agatha knew no Wampanoag ones -- but they were working on it.  

 

The youngest member of Agatha’s not-quite-coven was Gwenhwyfer Keyler.  She was thirteen, and already a talented healer, but like Sokanon she used methods Agatha had never seen before.  Gwenny, as she called herself, along with her mother and her grandmother had recently escaped slavery (a practice that no one would ever need to think of twice if magic were more widely accepted, Agatha believed).  The family had walked for weeks, protected and guided North by Gwenny’s grandmother’s magic. A family magic that, if Gwenny was to be believed, stretched back generations, long before they had been uprooted and their names altered to flow more easily from their captors tongues.  Now, Gwenny and her mother and grandmother found themselves trying to fit into a small Massachusetts community of Puritans.  How bad must their old life have been for this to feel like freedom, Agatha wondered with a shiver.    

 

The final member of Agatha’s potential coven was Constance Lane, who was 15, and from the outskirts of Salem.  Agatha chose her because she hated Puritans and liked letting Agatha kiss her in the woods, which was a newly discovered activity that Agatha found to be extremely helpful in alleviating her day-to-day boredom.    

 

On a warm, moonless night in early September, Agatha led these girls through the woods, illuminating the way with the glowing purple emanating from her palms.  The girls chatted nervously with each other as they followed Agatha, keenly aware that they had no way to predict what she was up to this time, but assured that whatever it was would be completely and totally Not Allowed.  

 

“I hear water,” Sokanon said, and the girls fell silent and listened.  Sure enough, they heard the gentle lapping of waves on the beach.  Agatha had brought them East, to the ocean coast. 

 

“We’re here,” she said, and used her purple flames to light a couple of torches stuck into the sand, illuminating the beach around them.  

 

“A ghost forest,” Cora breathed, as they looked around at the dried out, dead trees that rose dramatically from the swampy coastal area just down the beach.  

 

“What’s that?” Gwenny asked. 

 

“Saltwater invades the soil and kills the trees so they just stand there, barren and dead,” Cora explained.  

 

“Oh, kind of like you all,” Agatha said, gesturing at the girls.

 

“Very funny, Agatha,” Cora replied, as Gwenny and Sokanon groaned.

 

“Not what you thought this afternoon,” Constance added, and Agatha smirked at her approvingly.

 

“Shall we?” she said, and began leading them towards the dead trees.  When she came to one with a low hanging branch she pulled at it with all her weight, and it detached from the trunk with a satisfying crack.    

 

The other girls followed her, exchanging looks.  “Agatha, what are we doing?” Constance asked.

 

“We’re breaking big branches off of dead trees, Connie, it shouldn’t be that hard to figure out.”

 

“It’s a hexenbesen,” Gwenny said.  “She wants us to fly.”

 

“Don’t you want you to fly?” Agatha shot back.  “Let’s go, let’s go!”

 

The girls stood awkwardly, glancing at each other but not at Agatha.

 

“What’s the problem?” Agatha asked, agitation rising in her chest.  

 

“This is really, really not allowed, Agatha,” Cora said. 

 

“Yeah, and someone will definitely see us,” Gwenny said.  

 

“They won’t,” Agatha said matter-of-factly. “Why do you think I picked a moonless night?”

 

“How did you even learn how to do this?” Constance asked.  “This is a crazy protected spell.”

 

“I’ve known how to do this since I was eleven,” Agatha sighed.  “But unfortunately, you need a coven to make it work.  Covens fly together or not at all.”

 

The girls still didn’t move to break their own brooms from the tree branches.  

 

“Are we a coven or not?” Agatha demanded.  

 

“Well, we want to be, Agatha,” Constance said.  “But we’re not even supposed to be in a coven at our ages, let alone fly as one.  It’s not allowed.”

 

“Neither is stealing you food,” Agatha said.  “Or money.  Or blinding your mothers to your whereabouts.  All things I’ve done for all of you, all summer.  Is it only fine when I’m the only one taking the risk?”

 

“Agatha, this is different.  This is public, it’s risky,” Constance said.

 

“Agatha… they’ll burn us for this,” Cora said.  

 

Agatha threw her would-be broom down in the sand.  “They’d have to find us first.  They’d have to catch us. That’s the whole point of the brooms.  Aren’t you tired of walking for hours just to get to the next village?  Don’t you want to be able to go far, farther than you can even imagine going on your feet?  This is about actual freedom.  Something we will never have if we stay here and do nothing.”

 

“I can’t do it, Agatha.  I’m sorry,” Cora said.  

 

“Me neither,” Constance said.  “I’m not risking my life for this.”

 

“I do not go up there,” Sokanon said, pointing at the sky.

 

Agatha blinked back hot tears.  “What about you, Gwenny?” she asked.  “Come on.  A small coven is still a coven.”

 

Gwenny looked at Agatha sadly, noting the desperation in her eyes not quite hidden by the simmering rage.  But she shook her head.

 

Agatha curled her lips derisively.  “I would have thought you of all people would see the value in taking our freedom into our own hands,” she said in a low growl.

 

“Don’t you presume to know what I want,” Gwenny shot back.  “You may want to escape this place, but you have no idea what my mother and grandmother and me went through to get here, to a safe place.  And you want me to risk getting caught doing something that is punishable by banishment or death?  Are you a coven leader, Agatha, or are you a coven killer?” 

 

Agatha’s eyes flashed purple, and the electrical storms flared brightly in her palms.  

 

“Not sure,” she said.  “Should we find out?”  She levitated a foot above the ground and drifted toward the girls, who turned and fled from her, the pink glow of Gwenny’s magic lighting the way for them.  

 

Agatha dropped back down to the sand.  She picked up her branch and stared at it in disbelief, letting tears fall, mourning the freedom she had thought she’d finally wrangled from this life.  

 

“Witches, afraid to fly,” she muttered.  “Pathetic.”

 

She took her not-broom and with a scream of rage, smashed it to bits against the trunk of the nearest tree.



***

Salem, 1692

In the spring of 1692, 18-year-old Victoria Miller moved with her family to the outskirts of Salem Village from one of the more northern settlements. Agatha, always interested in newcomers and lately very interested in pretty girls about her age, was one of the first to welcome her to Salem.  Within a few weeks, Victoria became an unprecedented phenomenon in the short history of Agatha Harkness: a peer that she actually spent time with regularly and voluntarily, and not for nefarious purposes.   

 

There wasn’t any one specific reason Agatha kept her around.  Besides the novelty, she wasn’t anything that special.  She was nice enough to look at, with medium-length medium brown hair, green eyes, freckles on her nose and forehead, and a slender frame.  And she was easy enough to entertain, with a ready laugh and the ability to converse about a couple of topics Agatha cared about. 

 

She was a Puritan by obligation but displayed no particular motivation to defend her religion from Agatha’s free-flowing criticisms. She was willing to listen to Agatha go on about her plans to move to Boston or Providence or New Amsterdam without interjecting to tell her she was unrealistic, or skeptically ask what her future husband would have to say about all that.  From the outside, it looked for all practical purposes like an honest-to-goodness, actual, genuine friendship. Agatha would have scoffed at those characterizations as far too hasty. 

 

Nonetheless, in return for being mostly tolerable and not too stupid most days, Victoria was rewarded by occasionally getting to do some of the talking.  Today, on this warm and sunny day in early June she visited Agatha’s house after lunch, as she often did.  She followed Agatha, chattering, into the barn where Evanora and some other members of the coven kept a few goats and sheep. It was Agatha’s job to replenish the animals’ food and water, which she was currently doing via magic, whilst sitting on a bale of hay with her feet propped up on a taller bale of hay. (Agatha had lost the patience to keep her magic abilities closeted in front of Victoria almost immediately.  Victoria, after an appropriate amount of fawning and delight upon learning Agatha’s secret, had helpfully assured Agatha that she wouldn’t purposely do anything to get her executed.)  

 

Ironically, the topic of conversation on everyone’s lips this summer, including Victoria’s, was the Witch Trials.  

 

The witches of Agatha’s coven knew, of course, that not one of the women currently on trial was actually a witch -- so fundamental was these people’s misunderstanding of witchcraft that their accuracy rate at identifying it was literally zero.  Agatha had felt a pang of pity when it became clear these fanatics really were going to start killing people for these conjured crimes; then she figured these Puritans had, after all, helped make the bed they were currently lying in.  She elected not to think of it much beyond that.    

 

Victoria, when she spoke of it, didn’t do any moral hang-wringing for either side, adding to her tolerability in Agatha’s eyes.  But she was absolutely concerned with the spectacle of it.  Each time they met, she made sure Agatha knew all the details that she had heard since they last lazed around the barn together on a summer afternoon.  

 

Today, though, Victoria’s updates on the Witch Trials, and everything else she typically went on about for that matter, were conspicuously short.  The silence that fell over the barn after ten minutes was enough of an anomaly that Agatha opened one eye and peered at Victoria from her spot in the hay.   

 

“Cat got your tongue?”

 

Victoria sat on a second bale of hay across the narrow central aisle of the barn from Agatha, her usual chatting spot.  Far from lazing, though, she was sitting up straight with her hands over her mouth. Beneath her long black dress, her knees were bouncing nervously.

 

Agatha narrowed her eyes, and waited.  

 

“I’m a little nervous, I think,” Victoria finally said, removing her hands to her cheeks from her lips, “To tell you about something that happened to me.”

 

“Why?” Agatha asked, curiosity piqued.  “Is it something embarrassing?  Did you belch in the middle of church and get kicked out?”

 

‘No.”

 

“Did you get a word wrong in your prayers and get sent to the stocks in the square?”

 

“No, Agatha.”

 

“Did you pick a carrot too hard from the garden and fall on your ass in the mud?”

 

Victoria threw her head back and laughed at Agatha.  “Agatha, you’re so frivolous!”

 

“I’m just trying to think of all the exciting things you do in life to help me come up with a good guess, but all I can think of is church and chores.”

 

“Well,” Victoria said, the tone and volume of her voice both lowering as she spoke, “You remember how I told you I might have a. . . a suitor?”

 

Victoria's pale cheeks flushed red as soon as the sentence was uttered.  She gazed at her feet rather than meeting Agatha’s eyes.  

 

Oh, this might be actually interesting, Agatha thought.  

 

“I remember,” Agatha said.  “His name was . . . Jesus or Judas or something like that.”

 

Victoria tittered, “Those are two very different names, Agatha.”

 

“If you say so.”

 

“It’s Josiah, actually.  Anyway, I saw him yesterday.  We went for a walk.  Together….alone.”  

 

Agatha watched Victoria fidget with her hands in her lap.   

 

“Scandalous,” Agatha teased.  “Well, you’ll never get into heaven now.”  She sat up straighter on her bale of hay.  

 

“That’s not all,” Victoria added breathlessly.  

 

“Oh?” Agatha prompted, a smile creeping onto her lips.  “Did something interesting happen on this walk?”

 

“No, don’t laugh!” Victoria giggled.  

 

“I’m not laughing. I’m smiling in anticipation,” Agatha assured her. “Go ahead, you can tell me.” 

 

“I let him kiss me!”  Victoria spat the words out in one big jumble and then buried her head in her hands to hide her face from Agatha.  Against the bale of hay, she kicked her feet.

 

“Wooo-ooo-ow,” Agatha exaggerated her enthusiasm.  “That’s a big step.  So was that your first kiss?”

 

“With Josiah, or with anyone?  I suppose the answer is the same either way - yes. I can hardly believe it happened,” Victoria said.    

 

Agatha cocked her head to the side.  “Why were you nervous to tell me that?”

 

“I’m not sure,” Victoria said, smiling sheepishly.  Her cheeks flushed a shade darker, if Agatha wasn’t mistaken.  “I guess it’s because you find all the young men in town to be so foolish, I feel shy about liking one of them.”

 

“Well, if you like him, then what do you care what I think?”

This only seemed to fluster Victoria further.  She wrung her hands for a moment. 

 

“I don’t know, I want my friend to be excited for me, I guess?  I was afraid you wouldn’t be.”

 

“Hmm,” Agatha said.  “Well, I could be excited for you.  But it depends on how the kiss was, doesn’t it?  I’m not going to be excited for you if it was a bad kiss.”

 

“Oh,” Victoria said with a small shrug.   “Well, it was my only one.  I don’t have anything to compare it to.”

 

“But you can tell if it felt good, right?” Agatha said.  “Kisses are supposed to feel good.  At least, that’s what I’ve heard.”

 

“Feel good, like.. how?”

 

“I dunno,” Agatha said and shrugged.  “But it must, or why would people do it?” 

 

“I guess I don’t know.” 

 

Two things were becoming clear to Agatha: One, Victoria cared much more about talking about having a kiss than the kiss itself, and two, it had never occurred to her to evaluate how she actually felt about it.

 

Agatha stood up from her bale of hay and crossed the central aisle of the barn to where Victoria was sitting, and leaned against the wall next to her.  Victoria looked up at Agatha and shifted nervously in her seat.  

 

“All right, we can figure this out,” Agatha said.  “Process of elimination. Did it make you feel. . . sleepy?”

 

“No.”

 

“Did you feel .. .  hungry?”

 

“Hungry? No.”

 

“Sad?”

 

“No, of course not!”

 

“Bored?”

 

“Definitely not.”

 

“Did it make you feel like laughing?”

 

“No. Kissing isn’t really funny, Agatha,” Victoria said, shaking her head and chuckling a little.

 

“Hey, I don’t know how it feels, so I’m just taking guesses,” Agatha said, holding her palms out, face up.  

 

“Oh, I know,” Victoria said quickly.  “It’s okay, you can keep guessing.  It’s fun.”

 

In response, Agatha gazed out the barn window, lips pursed as she made clear she was deep in thought.

 

“Okay,” she said finally, returning her gaze to Victoria. “I’ve got a good one.  This is a good guess, are you ready?”

 

“Ready.”

 

“Did it make you feel. . .” Agatha said, trailing off as though she were searching for the right words, “Kind of warm?  Warm everywhere?”

 

Agatha gestured up and down the length of her torso with her index finger as she asked.  Victoria’s eyes followed Agatha’s hand up and down her body.  

 

Huh , Agatha thought to herself as she watched Victoria’s expression.  Somehow, it had never occurred to her before this moment that there could be a reason more interesting than idle conversation that Victoria followed her around all the time.

 

Time to potentially ruin this nascent friendship? Yes, indeed.  

 

“Did it make you want him to touch you?” Agatha asked softly. She held her index finger to her lips as if to say “shhh” and looked over her shoulders exaggeratedly, making sure no one was listening to them before adding, in a dramatic whisper, “Like, under your dress?”

 

Victoria opened and closed her mouth without making any words.  

 

“You’re naughty, Agatha,” she said finally, allowing herself a small smile.

 

“Mhmm,” Agatha nodded.  She sat down next to Victoria, squeezing herself between the other girl and the wall.  There was so little room that Agatha’s left hip and thigh pressed against Victoria’s right. 

 

“Were his lips soft?” Agatha asked, brushing her fingertips over her own lips to accentuate the question.  

 

“Yeah, I. . . I think so,” Victoria said to Agatha’s fingertips.

 

Agatha turned her upper body toward Victoria, and leaned forward just a little.  “Was he gentle, Victoria?”

 

She paused a few beats, took a breath before adding, “Or did he kiss you hard?”

 

“I’m. . . not sure,” Victoria said, her voice barely above a whisper.    

 

“Maybe he started off gentle, and then got a little rougher by the end?”
