“What? Is what true?” Agatha said, squinting through the bright blue beams of magic radiating up from the floor.  

 

“Is it true that you were. . . holding. . . Hannah against her will?”

 

Agatha felt a chill run through her.  They would accuse her of that ?  

 

“How dare you?” she said icily.  “Hannah invited me into her home. Her hand touched me first!!”

 

“No, it was a trick!” Hannah said to the coven. “She- she sparked my desire to steal my power.  She used her illicit magic!”

 

“If by magic,” Agatha said, eyeing Hannah coldly, “You mean my uncovered skin.  My untamed hair.  And my warm breath.”

 

“Enough!” Evanora roared.  

 

Hannah’s cheeks flushed.  “Succubus,” she hissed.  

 

“And yet you still stand. You still spark,” Agatha said, forcing her chin high.  “I controlled it, even though I didn’t know I had that power.”

 

Agatha shifted her gaze to her mother. 

 

“Did you know?”

 

Evanora averted her eyes from her daughter.  

 

“Keep her here,” Evanora said to the coven.  “We’ll discuss what to do about her.”

 

Agatha, confined to that small corner of the kitchen by a magical barrier, slumped to the floor as the coven filed out the door of Hannah’s house.  

 

“Liar!!” she screamed, as loud as she could, hoping the coven was still close enough to hear her.  

 

She plunged her fingers into her hair and ripped the woven strands of her braid apart as quickly as she could.




*************************



Chapter 2: Agatha and Rio

Chapter Summary

It wasn't the plan, but this turned out to be 30,000 words of Agatha and Rio falling in love.

Author's notes:

#1:  It was a hilarious trick of life that while I was deep in the throes of planning a fic about dealing with grief and loss, my dad passed suddenly in early February.  It's not an exaggeration to say that writing this story is what got me through three of the hardest months of my life.  I think it's no surprise that I lingered in 1696 for all this time, getting lost in the world of these two witches falling in love.  I never would have told my dad that this story even existed (given the subject material), but he would have LOVED knowing that I spent so much time doing something that made me so happy.

#2: Relatedly, this chapter is dedicated to my wife, who gave me endless time, space, support, encouragement, and patience to retreat to my writing corner whenever I needed it.  Like Agatha and Rio's garden, you may not have created it, but this story wouldn't exist without you.  Thank you.  Also, when I asked her what I should tag this chapter she said "hot sex" and "emotional Spanish". I'm not going to do that, but fwiw.

 

Chapter II -- Agatha and Rio

 

1696, Boston

 

The young woman lay in a crumpled heap on the floor of the storage closet at the back of the tavern.  Her dress was undone, bunched up from both the top and bottom around her waist; her shoes, stockings, and undergarments were strewn haphazardly across the floor, leaving most of her body exposed.  The room reeked of alcohol.  Bottles of beer and spirits had crashed to the floor and shattered during whatever struggle had taken place here, or perhaps during the acts of passion that had clearly preceded the struggle. 

 

The woman had been about 20, brunette, light skinned, and slender.  Now she was withered, desiccated, her face sunken in and her skin a sickly greenish-gray.  

 

Yes, Death noted to herself with mild amusement as she surveyed the scene, this young city of Boston certainly had a serial incubus at work within its streets.  This was the fifth? Perhaps sixth? Young female soul she had had to reap in similar circumstances in the past few months.  

 

“That’s -- I’m dead?” the girl’s spirit asked incredulously.  

 

Death, fully shrouded in black from head to toe, appearing ethereal and translucent, usually did not make it known that she could speak to humans.  And the girl knew very well the answer to that question, anyway.  

 

But the girl turned to her, eyes desperate for answers even from this nightmarish figure beside her.  “Did God strike me down because I sinned? Because I was sinning?”

 

If Death had eyes in this form, she would have rolled them.  Among all the ridiculous stories humans had been telling each other over the millenia, she particularly disliked this narrative that certain populations had invented to darken the joyous cycle of creation.  What a betrayal of the sacred balance, she always thought, to punish with fear and shame those who wanted to participate in acts imbued with pleasure to encourage the creation of new life.   

 

“No,” Death snapped, her voice low, gruff, and echoey through her semi-corporeal form.  “That’s not real.”

 

“Then why did I --”

 

“The human you were having sex with drained your life force,” Death answered.  “He was an incubus.”

 

The girl hung her head.  “Agnes,” she said.  “She killed me.”

 

Death experienced a pang of something akin to the human feeling of surprise.  

 

“A succubus,” she corrected herself.  

 

“She told me we’d start a coven together,” the girl said.  “But now. . . I think it was a trick.”

 

“You’re a witch?” Death asked.

 

“Not really,” the girl said quietly.  “Agnes said I had a spark, though.  I wanted to learn from her how to be one.”

 

So.

 

There was a serial killing succubus witch preying on young women with nascent magical powers operating in Puritan Massachusetts?  

 

Well, Death thought.  Sometimes humanity produced something that was actually interesting.  

 

“Am I going to meet God?” the girl asked, eyeing the doorway and the bridge beyond it that had appeared over Death’s shoulder.  

 

Death did not answer.  The girl would learn the truth of the afterlife soon enough.  

 

***

Death had no cause to involve herself in human affairs. She didn’t choose when humans’ lives would begin or end or the circumstances of either event; she merely maintained the flow and balance of the process.  In her cosmic form, she had no influence over how the humans lived those lives.  Typically, she observed both individuals and societies with a distant disinterest only occasionally punctuated by moments of mild curiosity. The woman who would soon change her very relationship to humanity loved to call her “the ultimate tourist” in the realm of the living .  (At the beginning she would deliver it with a playful smirk; decades later it would be spat at her with bitter, devastated vitriol.)

 

And yet, by the very nature of Death’s job, human affairs consumed her every moment.  In an average era, scores of people were waking up next to their own dead bodies every minute, needing to know what to do next.  During a busy time, like a major war or invasion, a natural disaster, a pandemic, or a genocide, it could be hundreds or even thousands at a time. And as Earth’s human population grew exponentially, so did the magnitude of Death’s job.  All souls, no matter what culture or faith they were departing from, waited for her, the force of Death, to help them complete their journeys out of life. It was a lot for one entity to do, but this was not a concern; Death’s journeys across the bridge operated outside the stream of human time.  

 

By 1696, the year (according to her people’s calendar) that Death met Agatha Harkness, Death had already been operating this way on Earth for tens of thousands of years.  Her job began when humans first understood that the very fact of being alive meant that they would someday die.  Throughout the universe, this was the way it had been done for tens of millions of years; once a species became sentient, or developed “souls” as some humans liked to call it, they gained access to post-life dimensions.  But that didn’t mean they’d be keen to enter those new dimensions when the time came.  If there was one near-universal law it was that living things tended to want to stay alive. And so, the force of Death evolved sentience, too: a pool of primordial cosmic energy turned to matter turned sometimes to flesh, born alongside intelligent species to keep the sacred cycle running smoothly.  

 

Thus, in a very practical way, on this planet known as Earth, the incarnation of Death was humanity’s most constant companion.  There was nothing she hadn’t witnessed, from the epic to the mundane.  The births of babies, the lover’s quarrels, the inventions, the revolutions, the civilizations that rose and fell.  Tourist or not, there was no more experienced anthropologist in the Universe.  

 

From a professional standpoint, it was critical that Death could take a multitude of forms.  Her default state, once she had become sentient, was a humanoid shape made of black matter and the absence of it, a void surrounded by swirling black energy that evoked flowing robes to human witnesses.  This form was something visually halfway between her dark energetic origins and the human she could appear to be.  Sometimes a humanoid void was appropriate for the souls she ferried.  

 

But sometimes a soul needed to see what they expected they’d see when they died.  Minor deities, angels or saints or prophets, distant ancestors, recently departed loved ones --  Death had played them all.  Sometimes a soul simply needed to see a human face, one that matched them in physical and cultural appearance.  Death could do that, too.  

 

As is often the case in the course of evolution, Death's emergent traits had more than one use in her life as a living organism.  

 

Yes, Death had no cause to involve herself in human affairs.

 

…But she could .  

 

Tens of thousands of years was a long time.  Sometimes a disinterested cosmic entity who had seen it all. . . well, she saw something new.  

 

And like many sentient beings do, she got restless.  Curious.  

 

Perhaps, she got involved .

 

It had been three hundred years since it had happened last.  It might be time again.  

 

Humans could not see Death’s void-form while they were still alive, so Death stayed in this way while she lurked about Boston’s streets.  

 

She was gathering information about this human woman, this wielder of dark magic, this serial killer who had piqued her curiosity.  

 

It didn’t take her long to find the succubus.  

 

The locations of all of the victims triangulated her location within a five-mile radius or so of Central Boston. The rest of the puzzle was solved by the simple fact that there weren’t many young women in Boston openly pursuing other women. 

 

The Venn diagram overlap of these two groups contained only one person.

 

Her name was Agatha, but Death was one of only a few minds to know that -- this witch seemed to have no family, no coven. Those who knew her true identity included the bartender in her favorite tavern who unwittingly fed and wined her most nights, the landlord who was always certain she had just paid him yesterday, and the older woman who rented the room next to hers in a large boarding house in the city. This woman was the only person, as far as Death could tell, who Agatha may have revealed the truth to out of a sense of friendship rather than manipulation. 

 

She went by “Agnes” to everyone else, including strangers, casual acquaintances, conquests, and victims.  

 

One could never assume the true age of a witch, but she appeared to be in her early 20’s. She was fair-skinned, almost certainly descended from European settlers. She wore the expected attire of a Puritan colonist, with the exception of a cap. Her long, dark hair was always free, and always perhaps just one or two ticks of the dial above “unkempt.”  Death also noted that she preferred her left hand, using it for utensils, signing her name, writing notations in the books she was never without.  Death knew that many of those born with that proclivity were trained out of it as children -- one of those puzzling, arbitrary human quirks -- but somehow this witch had escaped that fate. 

 

Defiance seemed intrinsic to her nature.   

 

Death watched her work a few times.  She had a natural ease in talking to women who sparked her interest, managing to cultivate an air of mystery and suggestiveness using few words.  Once her target had agreed to follow her to a second location, usually with the unspoken understanding that intimacy would follow, the girl’s fate was sealed.  Death noted with some interest that the primary objective seemed to be sex, not siphoning --  Agatha didn’t kill all of her conquests.  Whether this was based on a random mood or some internal logic was as yet unclear.  

 

Once Agatha, or “Agnes,” was found, the next question was whether to approach her in human form.  This was a much bigger step for a cosmic being than simply observing.  Oftentimes, after a closer look at a human, Death’s curiosity would fizzle, and she would withdraw, nursing a vague disappointment in the ordinariness of it all.  

 

That was not the case now.  Agatha, in contrast to evoking boredom, made Death feel a stirring, a pull.  A vague sense that something, somewhere was incomplete.  A small voice in her consciousness nagged her that there would be something missing until she got closer, knew more.   

 

A direct translation was impossible, but in human terms this sensation might be described as longing .  

 

Death did not like the feeling of possibilities left unsatisfactorily unexplored. 

 

So now there were a few decisions to make regarding the nature of the human form that she would take.  Death could look like anything or anyone, but the bodies of Agatha’s victims were always women, usually within a few years of age of Agatha herself, though occasionally a little older.  In terms of physical traits, the young witch seemed to prefer dark hair and a slender frame, though of course, there wasn’t a large pool of prospects for Agatha to choose from in a small and repressed community.  

 

Death desired that her chosen form would intrigue, above all else. The books the witch was always reading were a clue: it wasn’t Bible verses but spells, stories, and information of all kinds, often from faraway people and places. Death suspected that traits that proved unexpected or challenging would serve her well.  

 

Once the physical body was chosen, Death clothed it in the typical women’s attire of the time, even as she bristled against it, imagining, given what she knew, that Agatha likely did the same. 

But while she dressed to blend in, she had no intention of letting Agatha mistake her for some run-of-the-mill Puritan.  She donned a darker skin tone than most of these immigrants from Northwestern Europe, and manifested piercing almond-shaped eyes and a sly, mysterious smile.  The name she chose for herself would be foreign to Agatha -- both a puzzle and a clue.

 

Rio Vidal, the human incarnation of the cosmic force of sentient Death, could not kill humans and she could not die. She could step outside of human spacetime or revert to her other forms at will. She could conjure frightening magic unmatched by almost any Earthly witch or sorcerer. 

 

She also had cells that ran on chemicals, and nerves that ran on electricity, and emotions born of both of those, just like any human. She couldn’t starve or dehydrate, but she could feel hunger and thirst, as well as the innate hedonistic pleasure of quenching them.  

 

The warm and heavy savory sensations of the venison and barley stew in her mouth and the cold, sour bite of the ale she sipped between mouthfuls shocked her out-of-practice senses. Her eyes and ears took some time to adjust to the assault, the immediacy, of the sights and sounds of a busy tavern at supper time.  

 

She could feel the pull of gravity at her muscles, the brush of the fabric she wore against her skin, the change in temperature when she walked in from the crisp, cool evening to the warm, fire-lit tavern.  She could smell rain and burning wood and the unwashed clothes of the people she passed on the street.  Pangs and itches, sweat and shivers, annoyance and delight, all free with the purchase of a human body.  

 

When Agatha Harkness swept into the tavern and took her seat at her usual supper table in the corner (which happened to be right next to the one where Rio had chosen to enjoy her first meal in hundreds of years), Rio remembered one more human sensation. It came to her acutely, and felt like catching fire in some places and breaking out into chills in others, like all of her senses were heightened and craving satisfaction at once.  It was a decidedly more corporeal version of the longing, the sense of possibility she had been feeling before.   

 

Rio remembered lust .   

 

She’d been observing Agatha from afar for a couple of days now, but never from within this human body.  She’d grown familiar with the way the young witch carried herself, with squared shoulders and a chin held high, hands and fingers always in motion even when they weren’t conducting any magical music.  Her eyes were a radiant, translucent blue, wide and searching as they took in the view of the entire tavern that she had from her corner near the bar.  Her cheekbones, her jaw, and her nose were sharp and striking.  Her lips were always quirking or curling in accord with her inner thoughts, or the tongue behind them was moving about.  Her long hair fell in waves over slender shoulders and soft curves.  

 

Beautiful , Rio thought, delighting at the trill of butterflies in her stomach. They were meant to be beautiful to each other, these humans , she ruminated, meant to inspire love and lust and creation .  

 

She was one of them now.   

 

Rio shook herself from this reverie. She pulled her eyes away from Agatha, who would certainly soon notice the staring. These were not the thoughts, she chastised herself, that one human spoke to another upon a first meeting.  There was a dance to do.  There was a game to play.  

 

Agatha was about to finish her meal.  

 

Rio stood, glass in hand, and approached the empty chair at Agatha’s table. 

 

“Can I join you?” she asked.  They were the first words she had spoken with human vocal cords in centuries.  

 

Agatha looked up from her meal and blinked in surprise at the abrupt appearance of this stranger who suddenly took up her whole view. She looked Rio up and down, and then back up again for good measure. Wordlessly, she used her foot to push the chair across from her back from the table, and its wooden legs scraped loudly against the floorboards.  

 

Rio sat in the offered chair. She set her drink on the table and folded her hands in front of her.  

 

“I thought you could use some company,” she said.  “I noticed all these louts in here are leaving you alone.” She gestured around the room at all the men, most of whom inhabited various stages of drunkenness.   

 

“Hmm,” Agatha agreed with a subtle nod.  “They know better.  Makes them smarter than you.”

 

“Oh, I don’t think so,” Rio said, taking a sip of her drink, holding Agatha’s gaze above the glass. “I’m pretty smart.”

 

“That so?”

 

“Mmhmm.  Smart enough to figure out if they’re leaving a pretty girl alone it’s probably because she’s already hurt their feelings a bunch of times,” Rio said with an approving smile.  

 

“And yet here you are.”

 

“Here I am,” Rio said, nodding.  “The difference between me and them is,” she said, taking a swallow of her drink, “I’m your type.” 

 

Agatha raised her eyebrows but said nothing.  

 

“What’s your name?”  

 

“Agnes.”

 

“Are you sure?” Rio asked, even as the name was being spoken.  

 

Confusion clouded Agatha’s eyes for a moment before she swept it back under the rug of her surly expression.  

 

“Am I sure I know my own name?”

 

“You hesitated for a second, is all,” Rio said. 

 

Agatha furrowed her brow.  No, she hadn’t.      

 

She didn’t have time to protest, though, because just then the bartender appeared at their table carrying two fresh glasses of ale, full to the brim.  He set one in front of each of them.  

 

“You ordered me a drink,” Rio said, smiling.  “Thanks, Agnes.”

 

“I. . . no, I didn’t.  When would I have--”

 

“I’m just kidding.  It was me.”

 

Agatha narrowed her eyes at Rio.  “I didn’t see you order anything.”

 

Rio smiled at Agatha and tapped her temple with her index finger.  The mug slid a few inches closer to Agatha without Rio laying a finger on it.  

 

The understanding washed over Agatha’s face, softening her eyes a shade, and the left corner of her mouth curled upwards.  It wasn’t a smile, exactly, but an acknowledgement.  

 

Agatha lifted the glass to her lips and, in a few gulps, drained half its contents.  

 

“So, who are you?” she asked as she set it back down in front of her.

 

Rio knew contained within that question was the less polite version, what are you? Which really meant, of course, what type of sorcery am I dealing with? 

 

She answered only, “My name is Rio.”

 

“What kind of a name is that?”

 

“It means ‘river’.”

 

“If you say so.”  

 

Something about the haughty, dismissive expression as Agatha assessed the meaning of her name hit the pit of Rio’s stomach like a fist.  A memory swept through her newly-human mind like a flash flood down a mountainside.  

 

“Oh, I remember you,” she said.

 

“From where?” Agatha demanded.

 

Shit .  Rio was out of practice with controlling inner voice vs. outer voice in human form.  

 

“Uh. From this tavern.  I was here the other night.”  

 

Agatha narrowed her eyes, clearly sensing a lie.  

 

And yes, it was a lie.  Rio had just realized that she remembered Agatha Harkness from somewhere else entirely.  It had been Salem, she believed, perhaps three or four human years ago. An impending death had drawn her there; she had sensed it was magical in nature and joined the humans’ time stream a little early.  Human witches were exceedingly prone to drama. When they killed people, it was either incredibly deserved or completely unfounded --  and either way it was usually entertaining.    

 

On that night, Rio recalled, a coven had gathered, binding and attacking one of their own, a young witch barely over the threshold into womanhood. It was memorable mainly because the would-be killers included the girl’s own mother.  Rio’s human body shivered as she remembered it.  It had stood out to her as a perversion of the natural behavior of the human species, a mother intending to take life from her child.  

 

But of course, that was not how it had played out. That same young witch was sitting across from her now.      

 

Of course , Rio thought. It made perfect sense. This serial succubus was the same witch who had saved herself with her siphoning powers that night years ago. And who had then, with her coven and her mother lying dead on the ground, magicked herself away without any detectable trace of guilt or pity.  And she was right for that -- Rio had thought so even back then. Those miserable traitors didn’t deserve either one.  

 

Rio recalled with satisfaction that she had not been kind to them as she had taken them across her bridge.  (That is, the ones who hadn’t refused to go.)  

 

For Agatha’s part, she had apparently decided to roll with Rio’s present day lie.  

 

“So, you’re saying you’ve noticed me before.” 

 

“I have,” Rio said. Turned out that part was even more true than Rio had initially thought.

 

Agatha digested this news, and regarded, chin in hand, this somewhat unsettling stranger in silence for a moment.  

 

Rio watched her thrum her fingertips against her lips as the wheels turned in her head. 

 

“I get the feeling,” Agatha finally said, “That you’re not here to talk.  Rio.”

 

“We can talk if that’s what you want to do,” Rio countered with a smile.

 

“Mhmm,” Agatha said.  “Then, where are you from?”

 

“Oh. Nowhere you’ve heard of,” Rio answered with a wave of her hand.

 

Agatha scoffed.  “You’re condescending.”

 

“I’ve earned it.”

 

“Not with me, you haven’t.”

 

Rio shrugged.  “Give me a few hours.”

 

Agatha’s eyes widened slightly in response, and she seemed to mutter something to herself under her breath. She picked up her glass and drank the second half of her ale in long gulps, which was clearly as much to buy time to figure out her next words as it was to have a bit more alcohol in her blood when she said them.  

 

Rio locked eyes with Agatha over the top of their glasses and finished her drink as well.  

 

When they set their glasses back down, Rio smiled at Agatha expectantly.  

 

“All right,” Agatha said, pushing herself back from the table.  “Let’s take a walk, shall we?”

 

Without waiting for an answer and without looking back to see if Rio was following her, Agatha left the table and wound her way through the obstacle course of raucous suppertime patrons to the tavern’s side door.  She opened it and stepped out onto a well worn dirt road that was lined by residences and small shops. She did not hold it open behind her and, once she was outside, set off at a brisk pace.  She turned left toward the outskirts of town rather than toward the center, where Rio knew she lived.   

 

In contrast to the crowded, noisy scene inside, the narrow, rocky street was dark, lit only dimly by the outdoor lanterns of a few modest homes, and mostly deserted for the suppertime hour.  It was chilly, as evening in late April often was in Boston, but clear and moonlit. The dirt beneath her feet was soft from a rainy afternoon.   

 

After only a few steps, barely past the glow from the tavern windows, Agatha felt Rio fall in lockstep next to her. There was just enough light in the fading dusk that Rio was able to see the dimples puncture the sides of Agatha’s cheeks as she smiled - a sign of satisfaction, Rio suspected, at Rio having done what she instructed.  

 

“So where are you taking me, my lady?” Rio asked as they passed the first few buildings, bending the grass along the road beneath their feet to avoid tripping over rocks in the road in the fading light.  

 

“Who said I was taking you somewhere?” Agatha replied.  “I just said let’s take a walk.”

 

“Well, a walk has to end somewhere,” Rio countered.  “Even if it ends right back where you started.  We could walk around the whole world, I guess.  Except, I think there’s an ocean in our way just a few miles in this direction.”

 

“It’s that way,” Agatha said, gesturing about thirty degrees south of where Rio had been pointing.

 

“So we’re not going to the beach, then.”

 

“We could,” Agatha said, “Takes a long time to get there, though.”  

 

“Oh, are we in a hurry?” Rio asked.  “We have no destination, but we need to get there fast?  I might like the sound of that.”

 

Agatha smirked, but didn’t otherwise respond, and they walked in silence as the buildings became fewer and farther between.  The light from the setting sun and from the ever-sparser houses began to dim.  

 

She was impatient about this thus-far destinationless walk, but Rio felt a giddy overwhelm rising in her the longer she was in such close proximity to this girl.  Her out-of-practice senses savored everything: the soft swish of her dress, the padding of her footsteps in the grass, the swing of her arms at her sides and her hair across her back, the incessant movements of her fingers.  

 

How would a little boldness go over?  Rio extended her left hand out to her side to brush her pinky against Agatha’s right hand; she felt fingers extend toward hers in response. 

 

A first touch.  Rio felt warm from it, and from the ale.  

 

Encouraged, she ran her fingertips up the length of Agatha’s, and partially up her forearm, too, and then back down. Then she grasped Agatha’s hand.  When Agatha didn’t pull it away, she stroked her wrist with her thumb.  

 

This was when Rio became extremely aware of not just the skin against her palm but also the heartbeat in her chest.   

 

Agatha glanced sideways at Rio, then looked furtively over her shoulder, checking behind them.

 

“There’s no one around,” Rio reassured her.  She intertwined their fingers and wondered if it felt as intoxicating to Agatha or if it was just her recent inexperience.  

 

Rio tilted her head back and looked at the stars, drawing in a deep breath of the cool, humid night air.  It felt good, and yet she felt unsatisfied.  She had been prepared for a lot of things when she finally approached Agatha, but a long stretch of silence wasn’t one of them.  She craved more, now.  What was Agatha’s plan?  

 

She lengthened her stride to pass Agatha, then spun around to walk backwards in front of her.  It forced Agatha to slow her pace.

 

“Are we there yet?” Rio asked, smiling.

 

“Now who’s in a hurry?” Agatha observed.  

 

“Well, I’m the leader now,” Rio said, “You’re following me, see? So maybe you should tell me where we’re going so I know where to lead us.” 

 

Rio stopped suddenly, forcing Agatha to unintentionally step into her personal space.  Agatha stood with her body just inches from Rio’s.  

 

“How about somewhere dark,” Agatha replied, “And deserted?”   No longer snarky and taunting, Agatha’s voice now held stillness, authority.  Rio’s skin prickled with electricity.  She felt her cheeks flushing despite the crisp night air.  

 

“Sounds dangerous,” she said.  

 

“Oh,” Agatha said, “Do you not want to go somewhere like that? Too scared?”

 

Rio lifted the hand that she still held in her own, brought Agatha’s fingers to her lips, and kissed them gently.  Agatha shook her head to remove errant strands of hair from her face, and squared her shoulders with Rio.  

 

“That depends,” Rio said softly. “Why would we go someplace like that, Agnes?”  She tilted her head to one side and fixed her gaze on Agatha’s lips.  

 

“Mmm,” Agatha hummed, and that was the only syllable Rio got in response.  

 

Agatha lifted her left hand and ran her thumb along Rio’s right jaw, the one Rio had helpfully bared by tilting her head to the side.  She scraped her fingernails lightly down the side of Rio’s neck, traced her index finger down the center of Rio’s chest and belly, and let it fall away just below her belly button.  

 

Rio smiled and closed her eyes, and breathed hot, shallow breaths of anticipation.  Her blood rushed, fiery, through her veins.   

 

“Agnes. . . how much farther?” she whispered.  

 

“Here’s good,” Agatha said, the only split-second of a warning before Rio found herself being roughly guided backwards. Agatha’s right arm encircled Rio’s waist and her left hand grasped the collar of her dress, and in a flash Agatha had disappeared them from the street into a narrow cranny between two nearby wooden buildings. 

 

Rio laughed in surprise at the sudden change of scenery, and was rewarded with a devious smile -- the first genuine one Agatha ever bestowed on her.  Her eyes flashed so brightly that the mere moonlight let Rio see it. 

 

She let Agatha wrangle her into a recessed entryway about halfway down the length of the house on her right --a small porch hidden from view at most angles from the street, Rio noted. It was a location much too serendipitous for Agatha to have not already known it was there.  

 

They disappeared into the darkened corner of the tiny porch of what seemed to be an unoccupied house, accidentally, breathlessly crashing into each other and the wall in the blackness.

Rio swept to her side.  There were many, many reasons she hoped that Agatha was okay, but not least among them was the strong desire to NOT have to try to wrangle this woman’s soul in this current moment of instability.

 

To her relief, Agatha was fine and already taking out her predicament on the branch that had had the misfortune of becoming her first broomstick.  It had shattered into three separate pieces under the force of Agatha’s left foot by the time Rio arrived at her side.

 

She pointed her index finger in Rio’s face.

 

“This is your fault.”

 

“Um, okay. How, exactly?”

 

“Why did you tell me that??!”

 

“About how I think of you?  Well, it kind of seemed like you were asking about my feelings.”

 

“Did you put a spell on my broom to make me crash?”

 

“Why would I do that??”

 

“I DON’T KNOW.”

 

And then, despite her admission that Rio probably wasn’t the immediate cause of her arboreal incident, she raised her hands in front of her and emitted two bolts of concentrated purple magic that hit Rio square in the chest.   

 

Rio’s magical but nonetheless human body reacted as any human body would, and went careening about ten feet backwards before coming to an abrupt stop against the trunk of a maple tree.  

 

“OW!” she exclaimed, and sent bolts of magic right back at Agatha who suffered, then, a second tumble through the woods in as many minutes.

 

It went on this way for several minutes.

 

“Are you done yet?” Rio asked wearily and warily, doubled over and panting after her eleventh or twelfth double bolt to the chest.  

 

“I’M NOT SURE!” Agatha yelled -- or, tried to yell, since she was quite literally currently knocked on her ass on the forest floor.  

 

Rio approached cautiously.  “I’m available to help you up now.  If you promise not to try to kill me.”

 

“You can’t die.”

 

“I said ‘try’.”

 

Rio helped Agatha to her feet.  

 

Agatha yelled at her one more time.  It wasn’t even words this time, it was just . . a bellow.

 

“AUUURGGH!”

 

“Do you feel better?” Rio asked when it was over.  

 

“YES!”

 

“Do you want me to make you another broom?”

 

“YES, PLEASE!”

 

“Do you want me to take back what I said about that word?”

 

“NO, I LOVE IT!”

 

Rio shook her head and stifled a laugh.  “Can you stop yelling, then?”

 

“FINE!”

 

Agatha flounced back down on the forest floor and hugged her knees to her chest while Rio made the second broomstick she had made for su novia Agatha Harkness in the past twenty minutes.  This time, despite the magic burns and the bruises, she couldn’t stop smiling while she did it.   



*

 

Now that their new terminology had been sufficiently digested, Agatha’s second attempt at a flight went quite a bit more smoothly.  She stole glances at Rio as they soared through the air, and mimicked her confident, nonchalant posture on her broom.  Once they rose above the treetops (and thus beyond the threat of entangling branches), she felt the apprehension turn into exhilaration;  whether it was the physical sensations of flying tickling the pit of her stomach or the realization of the freedom she’d just attained, she couldn’t say.  

 

At first she felt like this flight could go on for eternity and she’d be perfectly content with the course of the rest of her days.  After the first hour, she was forced to agree with Rio that taking regular breaks on the ground was a necessary concession to the practicalities of broomsticks.  Having to travel with your ass resting on a tree branch, squeezing it between your upper thighs so you didn’t fall to your death, wasn’t the most physically comfortable way to spend time.  Some delicate areas of skin began to chafe.   

 

“I’ll kiss it better later,” Rio offered helpfully when Agatha complained.  

 

Discomfort was forgotten when they passed over New York at twilight.  They had to ride high enough that they probably wouldn’t be noticed and wouldn’t be recognized as witches on brooms if they were. From that height, the city appeared as a small, irregular grid of dim yellow lights -- torches, lanterns, bonfires.  Some of the dark patches were woods and some were water, the latter of which could be discerned by the last bit of sparkling sunlight setting over the horizon.  

 

They banked left just south of the city and headed out over the long island that jutted out into the Atlantic. Smatterings of punctate lights indicated that small settlements were springing up rather far from the city already.  They flew until those lights were few and far between, landing in a place that a wooden sign on a dirt road helpfully proclaimed to be called Massapequa Village.  It seemed like a reasonable place to settle; a few colonists wandered about, but it was mostly Native structures for now.  The terrain was wild and rugged.  Thick, concealing groves in the woods gave way to sandy paths down to the beaches.  It was far from the city, but this wasn’t a concern when you could fly to the shops, services, and taverns any time.   

 

As they walked the woods in the early night getting the lay of the land, Agatha stole some fish that had been salted and laid out to dry in the backyard of some unsuspecting colonists.  She and Rio had a hearty dinner and a good night’s sleep by a campfire on the nearby beach.  

 

The next day, they settled on the location for the small house they would build -- a tiny clearing in the trees set far back from the foot paths that ran parallel to the beach and concealed by a row of willows lining a freshwater stream.  

 

The actual construction of the building, Rio had assumed, would be accomplished by magic.  Agatha had other ideas.  

 

“You have to know what you don’t know,” she declared, and Rio realized that she was right.  They could use their magic to break trees into logs and stack stones into walls, but they hadn’t the knowledge to put them together into a cohesive structure they could trust to not fall in on their heads.  And so briefly, Agatha and Rio became “sisters” -- new settlers in the area whose poor, dear father had been recently, grievously injured.  And so you see, they just so desperately needed help from strong young colonists in the area to construct them a sturdy, warm place to live.  

 

Their powers of flirtation were strong enough that they barely had to use their mind control ones, though the latter certainly did come in handy to make the team of handsome young colonist cousins of Massapequa forget they had ever met “Agnes and Ruth” or knew the location of their log cabin with the strong stone foundation and the sturdy thatched roof.

 

Now that Agatha knew the location of her new home, it could be targeted in translocation spells. Over the next few days she gradually moved in her furniture and then her possessions.  She took her time, preferring to do all of the arranging and decorating by hand rather than by magic, finding every book and seashell its own perfect place.  Rio, when she was around and not off working, spent her time outdoors.  She relocated wild shrubs and flowers to the grass around the cabin, planted herbs, berry bushes, and edible mushrooms in easy foraging distance, and set fruit seeds to germinate unnaturally quickly in the nearby woods.  She turned the trickle of a stream that ran along the left side of their yard into a set of small waterfalls with rock barriers, and used the same technique to collect a tiny natural pool for bathing or washing clothes.  She even brought just a bit of the beach up to Agatha’s front yard, arranging a small patch of sand along the water -- just enough for two people to sit and stretch their legs across, should they so desire.  

 

Rio waited until the most golden hour of the summer evening and then brought Agatha out to see her handiwork.  Agatha stood in front of the willow trees and gazed at her new patch of land, covered in color and life.  She clasped her hands together and held them in front of her lips.

 

“What do you think?” Rio asked, beaming at her.

 

Agatha shook her head in awe.  “You create so easily,” she said softly.  

 

“Life from death,” Rio said.  “It’s kind of my thing.”

 

“I wish. . . “ Agatha said, and trailed off.  “My powers only take.  Or at best, change things that already exist,” she said, glancing sadly at Rio.  

 

“No,” Rio said.  She nudged Agatha to stand in front of her and wrapped her arms around her waist, and spoke softly into her shoulder.  

 

“You’re looking at this all wrong, Agatha.  Maybe you didn’t grow these flowers or build these waterfalls, but you were just as much a part of their creation as I was.  It wouldn’t exist without you. I made it for you.” 

 

Rio felt Agatha’s belly expand in a deep sigh against her arms.  She laid her hands on Rio’s forearms.  

 

Rio’s chest ached at the deep sadness that still radiated from Agatha even amidst the beauty and excitement of their new home.  She should have known; it was never going to be enough to merely give Agatha beautiful things.  She didn’t want to be the inspiration for them, she wanted to be the source.  This was partially jealousy, yes, and a craving for all manner of power, including Rio’s. And it was certainly also a deep, pervading competitiveness that was always itching for a fight.  

 

But on the lighter side of those darker impulses were the most human of truths. The restless discontent that was her driving force -- it was there because Agatha needed to make her own world.  She was full of a drive for autonomy, control, and understanding that had been fighting against being buried alive for most of her young life. 

 

Those desires, both sides of them, were what made her so alive -- the most alive of human beings Rio had ever known.  

 

“Hey, I know what you need,” Rio said, arms still wrapped around a brooding Agatha.  

 

“What’s that?”

 

“I’m going to take you somewhere right now, do you trust me?”

 

“Tell me where and then I’ll let you know.”

 

“That’s not really how trust works, Agatha.”

 

“Fine,” Agatha conceded, and Rio’s dark green magic enveloped them.



When the smoke cleared, the first thing that Agatha noticed was that the sun was both hotter and brighter than she had ever experienced in her entire life.  A July day at the beach in Boston seemed like twilight by comparison.  

 

“Rio, what the f--” she started to say, before the sights around her stole her words.  

 

She turned in a slow circle; she could see so far into the distance in every direction that it was dizzying.  The open, flat landscape, layer upon distant layer of brown, gray, and off-white seemed to go on forever.  There were hills in the distance in one direction, blue-grey and rolling.  They looked almost soft, like someone had laid a blanket over a jagged pile of rocks.  Closer to her were actual piles of rocks, orangish brown boulders strewn across the landscape in piles and stacks, some looking precarious, like they would fall over with a strong breath against them.  Beneath her moccasins, the ground was cracked and dry, with a fine layer of dust that rose into the air when she moved.  The grass -- was it grass? -- grew in funny looking tufts with brown dust and dirt between them.  Its shade of green was different too -  a muted, desaturated version of what she was used to.

 

“Why does the sun feel like this?” she finally asked Rio, who was looking at Agatha in amusement while Agatha was looking around in bewilderment.  

 

“We’re in the desert,” Rio told her.  “That’s how the weather is here.”

 

“It feels. . . mean,” Agatha assessed, holding a hand above her browline to block some of the brightness from her eyes.  

 

“Ohh, it is,” Rio agreed.  “That’s why all the plants are all sharp and spiky.  Recognize this?”

 

Rio pointed with her foot to a small, round cactus with whirls of spikes.

 

“This is where that weird plant I stole came from?” Agatha asked in surprise.

 

“Unless that witch you killed had some super high level Green Magic, then yeah.”

 

“Hmm.  She definitely didn’t,” Agatha decided.  “Why are we here?”

 

“Because I could tell you were bummed that you didn’t get to help with the garden,” Rio said.  “I thought I’d bring you somewhere cool and you could pick something to bring home for it. You like your cactus, and I love the desert.  It’s my favorite kind of weather, actually.  Look, I even brought you a bottle so you could bring home some dirt.”

 

“How far from home are we?” Agatha asked, snatching the bottle out of Rio’s hand and bending to sprinkle some tiny pebbles and sand into it.  

 

“Oh,” Rio said, exhaling sharply, “It would take days and days to fly here.  From here if you go a couple of days in that direction,” she added, pointing south, “You’d get to my absolute favorite place. It has deserts, jungles, mountains, beaches.”

 

“Well, why didn’t we go there?”

 

“Not every place on Earth is safe to just pop into.”

 

“What is that thing?” Agatha asked, walking up to a tree growing out of the dry ground a few yards away.  It was fairly small for a tree, not even double Agatha’s height, with serpentine branches growing at all angles from the trunk, making it nearly as wide as it was tall.  At the ends of the branches were more tufts, these ones made of tough, dark green spike-shaped leaves. As Agatha’s eyes adjusted to the bright sunlight, she could see that there were dozens of ones just like it scattered across the vast desert landscape.

 

“It’s a native tree.  One name for it is ‘humwichawa’,” Rio said.  

 

“I want one for our garden,” Agatha decided.

 

“You want a whole tree?” Rio said in surprised amusement.  “I thought you’d pick another cute little spiky succulent with a pretty flower.”

 

“I want that too.  I want one of everything,” Agatha clarified.  “Who knows what kind of spells you could use these in?”

 

“You know you can come back here with translocation now that you've been here.”

 

“Can you keep them happy in our garden even without the mean sun?” Agatha asked, ignoring her point about translocation.  

 

“Of course,” Rio admitted reluctantly.  

 

“Well, then?” Agatha asked, raising her eyebrows at Rio.    

 

And that was how Agatha and Rio wound up with a little patch of the Mojave Desert in their backyard in the Mid-Atlantic.



The next day, Agatha asked Rio where else she could take her for new plants.

 

That was how they wound up with a slice of the Siberian permafrost, complete with mosses, lichens, and a blanket of tiny summer wildflowers nestled among the oaks and apple trees that lined their creek. 

 

And a snow covered fir tree from the Canadian taiga.  And an olive tree. A lemon tree.  A coconut palm. Plumerias, orchids, tulips, gingko, and cherry blossoms.  Hot peppers, vanilla, cacao, and cinnamon.  

 

To Agatha’s disappointment, Rio couldn’t or wouldn’t take her to all of these places with her; there were many warnings about unfamiliar diseases and violent people.  So sometimes she had to settle for picking a place and a plant out of one of her books and setting Rio to task bringing it back for her.  On the plus side, Rio, beginning to get a fuller understanding of how to keep Agatha content, would usually bring back not just a plant and some dirt but also a spell book from a local culture that lived wherever she’d been.  

 

“You know I can grow all of these things from scratch, right?” Rio asked with a sigh after returning home with a particularly unwieldy African fern.  

 

Agatha pouted her lower lip.  “I want the real thing.”

 

“You don’t think I can make the real thing?” Rio asked indignantly.  

 

“I just like ones that grew up in their native places,” Agatha explained.  She threw down her newest spell book in frustration.  

 

“Señorita, I’m going to need you to go get a language book from wherever you got this.  I don’t have anything to help me with these words.”  

 

Rio looked at Agatha incredulously.  “Right now?  Can’t I just get one next time I’m in the neighborhood?”

 

“Well, I can’t read my brand new book unless I can do the translation, now can I?  And don’t you need to be in every neighborhood at all times, anyway?”

 

Rio stood up, and sighed heavily.  

 

“Fine.  But you better be naked when I get home.”

 

Agatha bit her lip and smiled as Rio disappeared in a dark green puff of smoke.  










**

 

Death had no cause to involve herself in human affairs.  Her most natural state was ethereal and unconcerned.  For 99.99% of her existence, humanity had been mostly a job, or at times a mild curiosity.  Her “happiness,” if it could be called that, rested on one thing, and one thing only:  maintaining the cycle. 

 

Once every few hundred years, the pleasures of human flesh, blood, and biochemistry intrigued her enough to partake until she tired of it.  Death was a stone skipping across the deep waters of humanity, alighting for an instant but barely getting wet, then sailing into the air once again.

 

Rio Vidal was sinking. 

 

Sometimes she would ask herself, when she was in her working form, why she couldn’t resist the pull.  It was the call of a different self, a call that could only be described as bodily memory seeping into her cosmic mind, her human form demanding her return.  It should be -- no, it was , she insisted to herself -- easy to lose the sense of human time in this form.  If she could stay, if she could only just stay long enough that those memories would lose their grip. . . maybe she could still save herself.  And that girl, that girl, that girl…. the one who made the feelings that kept doing the calling. . . she would eventually forget, too.  She would move on.  To someone more suitable.  More ordinary.  It would be for the better…  For the best…  

 

Someday, someday soon, Rio would find the strength to resist the call.  She had to. Didn’t she? How could it possibly be anything else?

 

The futility of that hope hit her like a falling desert boulder when she found herself standing in an alley behind a New York tavern with the spirit of a young woman whose body had been siphoned by a succubus and left to be found by an unlucky bartender.

 

…And by Rio Vidal.

 

She snarled at the young woman’s questions about the afterlife and sent her scampering in fear across the bridge.  Comforting her was the job of whatever angels she believed in on the other side.

 

When she went home to Agatha afterwards, she brought a literal storm with her.  It was one of the peripheral aspects of her Green Magic, to have some control over the abiotic factors that life depended on, like precipitation and temperature.  Well, now Agatha would see that this power wasn’t just about keeping her special little plants happy in their cute little patches of her backyard. Dark green clouds spiraled overhead and unleashed sheets of rain as Rio strode through the grove of willows and into their yard.

 

Agatha was waiting outside for her, hair whipping around in the wind, soaked to the bone already.  

 

Rio let the lightning illuminate her deathly form, skull and ribcage laid bare, before letting her human body take over.  Her angry heart surged with satisfaction as Agatha visibly flinched at the sight of her.

 

“Get inside, Agatha!” Rio yelled.  “I can’t control where the lightning hits.”

 

“Let it,” Agatha spat back.  “Sounds fun.”  She stood her ground as Rio entered her personal space.  

 

Rio blasted her backwards across ten feet of vegetation and sent her crashing at high velocity into her front door.  Agatha picked herself up and glared at Rio from the depths of her soul, but opened the door and went inside instead of fighting back.  She slammed it behind her as Rio reached for the doorknob.  

 

Rio translocated through the door.  “Welcome home, then?” she asked, taking off her soaking wet top layer of robes and throwing it angrily on the floor.  

 

“Oh, is this your home?” Agatha asked.  “That’s news to me.” 

 

“What the hell is that supposed to mean, Agatha?”

 

Agatha stood in the center of her room, small and soaking wet, but nonetheless intimidating as she burst with rage. She strode toward Rio, hurling angry words at her face. 

 

“It means, do you still live here?  Did you ever live here? Or is this just some place you visit because it’s where my bed is?”

 

“What are you -- how can you even ask me that?”

 

“I don’t know.  How can Death even have a home?”

 

“When I’m a human I’m Rio, maybe you remember me.  We’ve met a bunch of times.”

 

“And where else do you live?  With who else?  How would I even know?”

 

“Agatha, haven’t we been through this? I told you how I feel; in fact, I’m the only one of us who’s done that so far.  You want to be jealous right now?  That’s rich, coming from you today.”

 

A smile spread across Agatha’s lips and she stared at Rio tauntingly.  “So you found her.”

 

“She was dead, of course I ‘found’ her, Agatha.  Just like you intended.  And don’t try to tell me you didn’t fuck her before you killed her.”

 

Agatha shrugged.  “It got your attention.”

 

“You don’t need to cheat on me and murder people to get my attention!” Rio screamed.  

 

“Do you even know that you were gone for a WEEK?” Agatha screamed back.  

 

Rio’s intended next words caught in her throat.  The storm outside turned to a steady, windless rain.  

 

“I knew you didn’t,” Agatha concluded derisively.  “Unbelievable,” she scoffed.  “You know, you may be a tourist in this world, Rio, but some of us are stuck here.  We can’t cross the interdimensional bridge and fuck off to the underworld or whatever it is you do.  We get one reality, and when I’m in this one and you’re in that one, the only way to communicate with you is with dead bodies!” 

 

“I was gone a week?” Rio repeated.  

 

“Not even a flower on my porch,” Agatha said bitterly.  

 

Rio wracked her brain.  How had she stayed away that long?  She hadn’t meant to.. Had she?

 

“I don’t know what’s worse,” Agatha continued, her voice now low and tired, “If you forgot about me because of another woman, or because of nothing at all.”

 

Rio put her head in her hands.  “I didn’t forget about you, Agatha.”

 

“Then you stayed away on purpose?” Agatha blinked, and tears shot down her cheeks like tiny bullets.  Her nose darkened into a shade of red.  “That supposed to make me feel better?”

 

“No,” Rio said, feeling her own eyes fill with hot tears.  “I can’t explain it, I -- time doesn’t work the same way when I’m working.”

 

“How am I supposed to believe you,” Agatha said, her voice trembling, “When you can go anywhere, and I’d never know?”

 

“Okay, you’re panicking,” Rio said.  “It’s my fault, I know. I wasn’t careful.”

 

“Or maybe I’m just really stupid,” Agatha said.  “This fucking ridiculous situation I’m in,” she muttered.  

 

Rio put her arms on Agatha’s upper arms.  “No, listen.  Agatha, there is no one else.  There’s never been someone else like this, Agatha.  That’s why I’m so fucking bad at this, okay?”

 

“How can I trust you when there’s so much I don’t know?”

 

“That’s all any two people can do!  Nobody can ever know everything! And by the way, you think it’s easy to trust YOU?  I’m not the one who had sex with someone else YESTERDAY.”

 

Agatha’s gaze cast downward, but her words were still defiant.  “You technically never said I couldn’t.”

 

“Well, you can’t,” Rio snapped.  “‘Siphon them, kill them, I don’t care.  But not that.”

 

The rain outside calmed into a gentle shower.  Agatha nodded almost imperceptibly.  

 

“And what about you?” she asked.  

 

“ Novia ,” Rio said, slightly exasperated, “You are already so much work.  You think I have time for other girlfriends?”

 

“Hilarious,” Agatha said, rolling her eyes, spilling more tears.

 

Rio sighed.  “There are no other girls. I live here. Only here.”

 

Agatha stared into Rio’s dark eyes, studying her, searching for clues as though if she could just look hard enough she would find proof, she would find truth.  

 

Rio could only try to give it with words.  

 

“Agatha,” she said, “Listen, I didn’t suggest you move away from Boston just because of your reputation.  Okay?  I suggested it because I wanted to build a place with you.  I wanted us to start over, together.”

 

Agatha’s eyes widened at this revelation, then narrowed in confusion.  “Is that true?  Why didn’t you tell me that?”

 

Rio hung her head, and rubbed her eyes with her fingertips.  “My love, do you think you were ready to hear that from me? You crashed into a tree when I called you my girlfriend.”

 

“Did you just call me -- “

 

“Yeah, oh.  By the way, I love you,” Rio said.  

 

“Can you even do that?”

 

“Oh my god.  YES.  I’ve told you, Agatha.  As Rio, I’m as human as you are. I can feel the same things as any of you can.  It’s never happened before, but it’s happened now.”

 

Agatha stared at her like she’d been hit in the face.  Then her face crumpled, and she was crying.

 

“God, you idiot,” Rio said, letting her own tears fall and pulling Agatha’s head into her chest.  “Can you really not tell?”

 

Agatha grasped Rio’s sleeves and held tight.  

 

“I don’t care if you’re bad at it, Rio,” Agatha said.  “I just want to know you’re coming back.”

 

“Can I put you in dry clothes, please, Agatha?  You’re cold.”

 

Agatha shivered, and nodded.  Rio kept her girl in her arms while she magicked away the rain-soaked clothes and replaced them with a warm set of Wampanoag garments, a full body robe-like covering. Rio wrapped it around her to warm her up, but didn’t fasten it closed. There was something she wanted to do.

 

She guided Agatha backwards until her back was against the wall beside her bed. And then she took out the knife that she always kept in her waistband. 

 

“Agatha,” she said, holding the tip of the blade just below Agatha’s collarbone.  

 

Agatha glanced down at the knife and then back up at Rio’s face, her eyes shining with recently shed tears.  She was quietly puzzled.  But not afraid.   

 

“Are you mine?” Rio asked.

 

Agatha took two deep breaths before answering.  

 

“Yes,” she said, quietly. 

 

Rio pressed the blade into Agatha’s skin, just barely drawing blood, and dragged it downward. Agatha exhaled at the pain of her skin being broken, but made no moves to stop her.  Rio made two more cuts, a semicircle and a diagonal line, shorter than the first. 

 

An “R.”

 

Rio dabbed at the small trickles of blood on Agatha’s chest with her sleeve.  She flipped the knife around in her hand and held it, handle first, out to Agatha.

 

Agatha took it in her left hand.

 

“Do it,” Rio instructed.  

 

Agatha carved a small, shallow “A” into Rio’s chest, just below the collarbone.  

 

She handed the knife back to Rio. 

 

“Aren’t you going to ask?” Rio prompted her.  

 

Agatha shook her head.  “I’m afraid to,” she whispered.  

 

Rio took the knife and held it to Agatha’s neck.  But this time, instead of puncturing skin, she cut off the long leather cord meant to tie Agatha’s garment in place around her shoulders.  Once it was removed, she tossed the knife on the bed.

 

“Give me your hand,” she ordered Agatha.  

 

Puzzlement, then understanding, then disbelief flashed across Agatha’s face.  

 

She placed her right palm against Rio’s left, and their fingers naturally, involuntarily intertwined.  They worked together wordlessly -- Agatha used her left hand to help Rio wrap the cord around their hands and tie a knot at their wrists.  

 

Rio closed her eyes and uttered an incantation that Agatha didn’t know.  The cord binding their hands turned a shimmering green.  Agatha wasn’t sure if there was something she was supposed to do now, so she did what felt right.  She closed her eyes and let her magic rise from the veins in her right wrist.  Purple wisps mingled with the green encircling their hands and wrists.  

 

“Ask me,” Rio said.

 

Agatha closed her eyes.  “Are you mine?” she asked, her voice cracking on the last syllable.  

 

Rio’s stone hit the bottom of the lake, and became embedded forever in the silt beneath the water.

 

She grasped Agatha’s bound hand roughly, and lifted it to her mouth.  She pressed the back of Agatha’s hand to her lips, closed her eyes, and kissed it gently.  

 

“I’m yours.”

 

Rio kissed her then, with a fingertip under her chin to tilt it upwards, tasting her lips, salty from the tear tracks that had ended there.  Agatha’s free hand pressed into the small of Rio’s back, fingers curved to sink nails into the fabric of her dress and hold fast.

 

Their bound hands squeezed each other tight as Rio cradled the back of Agatha’s neck in her free hand, pulling her into deeper kisses.  She waved her clothes away, not simply for the usual reason -- to be naked with Agatha -- but this time also not wanting to press cold, rain-soaked clothes into Agatha’s bare skin when she was just getting warm after the storm Rio had wrought.   

 

“Come here,” Rio whispered after a moment, and guided Agatha onto her back on the bed.  Agatha’s soft robe lay under her but, untied as it was at the front, it fell open, exposing her fully.  

 

Agatha, usually so kinetic and decisive, even when Rio was ostensibly in the lead, lay still and expectant, cowed by her emotions.  She looked up at Rio, eyes wide and soft. Waiting.  

 

Rio knelt over her, bending down to kiss her lips again. “Are you mine?” she asked for a second time, this time with a gentle smile.  

 

This time Agatha nodded, and without hesitation, whispered, “Yes.”

 

Rio felt a sickness, a churning in her gut, a fiery open channel burning through the middle of her chest. The euphoric power of this feeling mixed with the terrifying knowledge that she could have, she almost let this go. . .
