She headed down the dirt road in the opposite direction, toward a row of even more modest homes.  She knocked on the door of one she knew to be occupied by a frail older woman (Agatha knew her neighbors much better than they knew her).  

 

Upon Agatha’s knock, the occupant pushed aside the curtains and took in the sight of the pale, disheveled woman and tiny, fragile baby.  She murmured “Oh no” and “Oh goodness” as she fiddled with her locks and began to open the door.  

 

Agatha stepped inside and flashed purple in her eyes and palms.  The woman, instantly terrified, sparked her own magic.  Agatha took it, leaving the woman a gray husk on the floor of her own kitchen.  

 

She bathed herself and Nicky in the washtub, and made a meal of the meager contents of the woman’s cupboards.  After Nicky had eaten, he cooed contentedly and drifted into sleep.  Agatha made him a nest of blankets and pillows next to the couch where she curled up to sleep.  

 

As Nicky sighed contentedly and babbled in his sleep, Agatha let the sobs rise from deep within her chest.   

 

*******

 

Westview, Fall 2024 (Present day)

 

Back in the basement of Agatha’s stolen Westview house, Agatha and Rio continued to hurl accusations and magic around the room.  

 

“For the millionth time, I didn’t TAKE him,” Rio was screaming at Agatha.  “I take souls!  You’re the only one of us who TAKES LIVES!” She punctuated her words with a shockwave that knocked Agatha backwards -- again.  

 

“I am so SICK of you clinging to these technicalities!”  Agatha growled back, shooting Rio with purple fire.  “Just admit what you did!”

 

“I did my JOB!!  God, I wish you still had a body so I could STAB YOU.”

 

“Oh please, stop embarrassing yourself.  Everyone knows that’s a euphemism!!”

 

“Two things can be true!!”



Though the bodily injuries to each other and the destruction to their surroundings was accumulating, Agatha and Rio’s violent focus on each other did have a positive side: it gave the agents in the room some cover to discuss their current options.  They crept quietly into the stairwell to discuss their ideas in hushed tones.

 

“So, I’m starting to think that they’re not going to work this out on their own,” Daisy said.

 

“Yeah, and the damage isn’t going to stay contained to this basement for much longer,” Monica added.

 

“Agree.  We need to talk neutralization,” May said.  “What are our options for these two?”  

 

“Rio is technically human in this form, correct?” Jemma asked, pulling a device from her holster that looked like a futuristic handgun crossed with a water pistol.  “That means she’s susceptible to human weapons.”

 

“You want to use the night-night gun on Death?” Daisy asked, amused.  

 

“Sure do,” Jemma said.  “If she has a human nervous system, the dendrotoxin will work its magic on her.  And if I’m not mistaken, it would mean she can also be quaked.”

 

“And punched,” May added.  “Now, what about ghosty over there?”

 

“I think I can use my powers to neutralize her,” Monica offered.  “Kind of like a Faraday cage for magic?”

 

“Brilliant,” Jemma said, “And, might I add, fascinating.  You’re implying the magical powers are part of the electromagnetic spectrum?”

 

“Based on my experience, yes.  Let’s hope I’m right,” Monica said.  “Billy, can you put up a barrier between them long enough for us to get this done?”

 

Billy nodded, sparking up blue wisps in his palms.  

 

“Okay, but hey,” Monica said, stopping them just as they were about to mobilize.  “Remember, team, these women are not our enemy. They’re not evil, they’re in pain.  They’re--”

 

“Grieving mothers,” Jemma finished for her.

 

“Exactly,” Monica said.  “Okay, let’s move out.”



Billy led the charge, firing a blue magic barrier into the space between Agatha and Rio.  The agents separated into teams behind him.  Monica’s eyes and body glowed as she built an electrical grid around Agatha from the floor to the ceiling, quite literally pinning her into a corner.  Agatha, of course, attacked it with her own magic, but Monica’s cage absorbed the energy and dissipated it harmlessly throughout the grid around her.  

 

“Oh, I see we learned some tricks, did we?  Some scarlet ones?” Agatha taunted Monica once she realized she couldn’t stop her from pinning her in.  “Fair warning, I’ve gotten out of a lot of cages in my day, honey.”

 

On the other side of Billy’s blue wall, Daisy’s earthquake powers pinned Rio against a concrete pillar, allowing Jemma to fire two shots from the night-night gun directly into her chest.  Though she slumped weakly to the ground, she was still stronger than the average human and remained conscious, though subdued.  It gave May the opportunity to move in; she pinned Rio to the ground on her belly and sat on her legs as she attempted to get her wrists into cuffs.

 

“Handcuffs, huh?” Rio asked her, looking over her shoulder with drugged, lidded eyes.  “That’s cool, I’m into it.”

 

“Can I get your number?” she asked May as the cuffs clicked into place.  

 

Rio sat up and promptly pulled her hands apart, snapping the chain between them, so Jemma fired another round into her chest.

 

“Seriousssssly, which apps. .  you on?” Rio asked May as she finally slipped into unconsciousness.

 

Agatha watched these proceedings with delight.  “Where has that thing been all my life?” she asked, indicating toward Jemma’s toxin gun.  “Can I have one?  I’ll give you a million dollars to be able to shut her up like that.”

 

May shook her head as Monica constructed a second power-absorbing grid around Rio.  “I’ve flirted with death a lot in my career, but I think that’s the first time Death has ever flirted with me.”

 

“The effects on Rio probably won’t last long,” Jemma said, re-holstering her gun, “Though human, she’s clearly enhanced.  But it looks like you were right about the EMF cages.” 

 

On the other side of the barrier, Agatha tried to pass through in her ghostly form, only to be denied.  As she howled in frustration, the Agents re-convened in the stairwell.  

 

“So we have a containment strategy,” Monica affirmed.  “Now we can talk solutions.  

 

Daisy asked, “Should we still go with the bridge work-around now that Rio is here?”

 

“I think that would only make things worse,” May said. “You saw how enraged she got when Agatha opened the portal. Now that she’s here, we have to focus on getting her back to work.”

 

“Her singular focus on her personal life right now seems to make that unlikely,” Jemma said.  “But linked as it is to her professional role, perhaps we’d have some success if we could get Agatha to concede some of the points of contention.”  

 

“I think you’re right Jemma, but I’m just going to say, I do not see that happening,” Daisy said.

 

Monica blew out a long breath from between pursed lips. “Well.  Agents, it appears our operations based mission has evolved into a negotiation exercise.”

 

“So I might have been wrong about that ‘not being marriage counselors’ thing,” Daisy said. 

 

“Let’s go find out the demands,” Monica replied.  

 

**

 

“All right, Agatha, we just want to talk,” Monica said as the team approached her.  “But rule number one is that you have got to stop antagonizing her.”  Monica pointed to Rio, who still slumped unconscious against the pillar where she had fallen.  

 

“Hey, you’re the ones who had me build that bridge,” Agatha pointed out.  “Not that it matters what I do.  My existence antagonizes her.”

 

“Can you help us figure out what she wants?” Daisy asked. “This crisis all started because of the fight between you two, right?” 

 

“I already gave her what she wanted.  I died.”

 

“Clearly that isn’t what she wanted or she wouldn’t be ‘on strike’,” May said.  

 

“Don’t play dumb, Agatha.  You died but you still wouldn’t go with her,” Billy pointed out.  “Your existence antagonizes her because you’re a ghost.  It’s like taunting her.”

 

“She’s going to have to get over that,” Agatha said, smoothing her translucent robes.  “I’ll cross that bridge when I’m good and ready.  And when I am, now I know how to get there all by myself.”

 

“This is like negotiating with terrorists,” May muttered under her breath.  To the team she said, “I don’t buy it.   These two have been fighting for centuries, and clearly Agatha being a ghost doesn’t stop them from doing that.  There was something else about Agatha’s death that enraged Rio.”

 

“Or devastated her,” Daisy said.  “Maybe it wasn’t so much of a rage quit as it was. . . a mental breakdown.  A psychotic break from reality, Death’s Version.”

 

“Rio still loves her,” Billy said.  “That’s always been clear.”

 

“So she never really wanted you dead,” Monica said.  “She wanted you back.”

 

“Mmm,” Agatha said, leaning against the bars of her electric cage.  “You’re on the right track,” she nodded.  “But I assure you, she most definitely wanted me dead.  Because she couldn’t have what she wanted even more.”

 

They all turned toward Agatha, who held her hands out, palms up.

 

“Absolution.”

 

“Forgiveness for taking your son,” Daisy said.  

 

“Is that something you can do?” May asked.  “For the sake of saving the world?”

 

“First of all, no,” Agatha said.  “Second of all, she wouldn’t believe me if I offered it, not after all this time.  Third of all, I didn’t say forgiveness.  Rio would say there’s nothing to forgive -- what she did was a moral imperative, all in a day’s work.” Agatha brushed her palms together to indicate a finished job.  “What she wants is for me to stop being mad about it.  To accept what she did and move on.“

 

“I understand this is a delicate topic, so please forgive me, Agatha, but -- doesn’t she have a point?” Jemma asked.  “She didn’t take Nicky’s life, she simply guided him to the spiritual dimension after he did.”  

 

“Yeah, not to derail,” Daisy added, “But that’s been confusing me.  If Rio doesn’t kill people, and she doesn’t create life per se,  why do death and creation stop when she stops ferrying souls?  A flood of ghosts would make sense to me, but. . . why no more circle of life?

 

Agatha closed her eyes, and shrugged.  “She calls it the Sacred Balance.”

 

“Hmm,” Jemma said thoughtfully.  “It makes me think of cell biology.  The survival of our cells depends on biochemical cycles -- hundreds of them, all intricately linked, interdependent.  Because the completion of each one depends on the success of the prior, if one enzyme fails, the whole process may grind to a halt.  Perhaps Rio is like that: the rate-limiting step in the flow of Earth’s life force.”  

 

“Well, it’s a good analogy for today’s problem,” Agatha said in a tone that wasn’t as complimentary as her words.  “An enzyme quitting its job, failing over and over to do what the cell needs. Clearly that leads to disaster.  But what if. . . what if that enzyme fails just once?  Maybe it skips a reaction -- whoops, didn’t see you there.  Then it catches the next molecule that’s exactly the same and, poof, everything goes back to normal.  Peace and harmony and sacred balance in the cell.”

 

Jemma smiled at Agatha bittersweetly.  “I do see your point, Agatha.  And the answer is, almost certainly nothing bad would happen in this hypothetical situation.  Possibly 99.99% of the time.  But what if the cell was already vulnerable?  What if the molecule four steps down one connected pathway and seven steps down another have effects that combine and lead to a bioenergetic crash?  What if they seed a toxic aggregation in a neuron?  What if they have a million imperceptible little effects that harm the cell over days or weeks or months?”

 

“A biochemical butterfly effect,” Monica said.   

 

“Exactly.  The point is that we can’t know.  I imagine that’s why the rules are as stringent as they are.”

 

Agatha pursed her lips, clearly unconvinced.  “Rio is not an enzyme.  And Nicky was not a toxin.  She had a choice.”

 

“And a responsibility,” May countered.  “And we can’t know what that feels like for her.”

 

Agatha scoffed.  “Do any of you have children?”

 

“Not yet,” said Monica.  “I want to.”

 

“Never worked out for me,” May answered.

 

“Fitz and I have a daughter, Aliya.”

 

“Daniel and I have twin boys.”

 

Agatha focused her gaze on Jemma and Daisy.  

 

“What if they were going to die?  Your daughter, your sons.” She pointed to Jemma, then Daisy.   “And what if Fritz, or Darnell, could make a choice to save them.  And yeah, the consequences might hurt their reputation or go against their principles.  But what if with that choice they could have bought you more time, a longer chance to figure out how to save your children? But instead they just. . .made a different one.  They threw up their hands and said, ‘Time’s up!  Sorry, but it’s meant to be!’”

 

Daisy and Jemma shared an uncomfortable glance.

 

“Well,” Jemma started, “I suppose it would depend--”

 

“I would never forgive him,” Daisy said.  

 

“Nor I,” Jemma sighed.  

 

Silence descended in the room for several long moments, broken by a simple question from Monica.

 

“How long did you get with him?”

 

“Six years, four months, and seventeen days.”

 

No one had noticed, but across the room, Rio had regained consciousness.    

 

“Is anyone going to ask how long I got with him?” she asked with a voice tired, subdued.  “What was it Agatha, about thirty seconds before you took him away?”

 

“He’s been in your afterlife for two hundred and seventy five years!” Agatha bellowed, her quiet pensiveness destroyed.  She shot beams at the walls of her enclosure in anger.

 

“That’s not the same, and I’ve told you that five million times!” Rio screamed back.  “You don’t LISTEN!”

 

“Damn it,” May said to Monica.  “It felt like we were getting somewhere.”

 

“Agreed,” Monica said.  “Clearly the two of them ‘talking’ to each other is the problem -- we need to divide and conquer.”  She thought for a moment.  “Okay. I have a history with Agatha, so I’ll work with her.  Daisy, you’re with me.  May, Rio clearly likes you.  Jemma, see if you can find out more about the science of what it is she does.  And Billy, you know Rio, you go with them.”

 

“Ooooh, she hates me though,” Billy said.

 

“You’ll be fine,” May said.  “Let’s go.”  


**

Monica approached Agatha’s EMF barrier, waving her arms above her head to cause distraction enough to pause the shouting.  

 

“I was hoping we could continue our conversation!”  She spat out the words as quickly as she could, not convinced she could hold Agatha’s attention for longer than approximately a nanosecond.  

 

Agatha glanced from Monica back to the other side of the basement where May, Jemma, and Billy were approaching Rio.  Agatha watched with bated breath, clearly hoping they would shoot Rio again, or otherwise take her down.  But if they did, Agatha was denied the pleasure of witnessing it, because Billy put up a curtain of blue magic behind himself and his group.   Agatha and Rio could no longer see each other.  

 

“Oh.  I see we’re being separated like misbehaving children,” Agatha said by way of reply to Monica.

 

“Do you blame us, Agatha?” Monica shot back. “It’s hard to problem-solve the end of the world in the middle of a domestic dispute.”  

 

“She started it.”

 

Monica ignored the petulance and took a seat on the basement floor just outside the EMF barrier, then motioned for Daisy to join her.  

 

“Will you sit with us?” she asked Agatha, who replied that she was fine where she was. She restlessly paced the small space between the barrier and the wall, her mind clearly searching for a way to take down the electric field that held her there.

 

Monica decided to open the conversation with something likely to stop Agatha in her tracks.  .

 

“So, losing Nicky,” she said,  “Is that why you tried to take Wanda’s power?  Because you saw that she brought Vision back?”

 

Agatha groaned in annoyance and rolled her eyes skyward.  

 

“This is relevant to nothing, but let’s get one thing straight,” she said.  “Wanda didn’t bring anyone back.  She made a replica of her dead boyfriend so she could play fake house in a fake reality. She’s no miracle worker.”

 

“Hmm, defensive,” Monica said. “But you didn’t know that at the time.  It must have been intriguing to see her capabilities given what you’d lost.”

 

“She was a glorified 3D printer, Mary Lou.”

 

Monica turned down the corners of her mouth and shook her head.  “Nahh, I don’t buy that.  You were obsessed with figuring out how she did what she did.  I should know,” Monica said.  “I was locked up in the attic of this very house while she tried,” she added to Daisy.

 

Agatha scoffed and waved her hand dismissively.  “When I came here I had no idea who or what Wanda was.  I took her power because I take everyone’s power who’s dumb enough to blast me with it.”

 

“Wait, so you’re a siphon?” Daisy asked.  “So was my mom.”

 

“Mine too,” Agatha replied.  

 

“Mine tried to kill me, actually.”

 

“Same.  How’d you stop her -- little ‘earthquake’?” Agatha asked, holding up her hands to mimic Daisy’s quaking stance.

 

“Oh, no, I was mostly dead when my dad found us.  And then. . . he, uh. . .  he killed her.”

 

“Lucky you,” Agatha said.  “I had to do it myself.”

 

“Yeah, totally,” Daisy said.  “I feel really. . .lucky. .  about that whole situation.”

 

“So then you’re a blood witch?” Agatha asked.  

 

“I don’t, uh, really know what that means,” Daisy said, glancing uncertainly between Monica and Agatha.  “We’re Inhumans.  We get our powers from terrigenesis, so, it’s a different kind of siphon powers, I guess?  Although, maybe they could be related.”  She furrowed her brow.  “We don’t really know how magic works, do we?” 

 

Monica shook her head.

 

“Great, well, as captivating as the girl talk is,” Agatha said, “Can we get back to the matter at hand?  I’m getting tired of humoring this fence situation.” 

 

“Sure, Agatha, I’d be happy to get down to business,” Monica said, the frustration bleeding through her words.  “I would love to hear what choices you think we have left.  You won’t cross Rio’s bridge, your existence angers her, and you can’t forgive her.  Do you think there’s anything you can do to actually improve this situation?”

 

“What do you want from me?  I can’t control her,” Agatha said, and Monica and Daisy registered a new quality to her voice -- tiredness.  Resignation, maybe?  “If I could, we wouldn’t be in this mess.”

 

“There’s middle ground between controlling someone and trying to kill them, you know.  Whole other continents of territory to explore beyond that, actually,” Monica said.  

 

“Do you still love her?” Daisy asked quietly.

 

Agatha’s eyes narrowed and darkened, and she waved her hand dismissively.  A mask of tiredness spread across her face.  

 

“That’s irrelevant,” she said.  




**


On the other side of the room, when May, Jemma, and Billy approached Rio, she leaned against the electric bars and hissed at them like a cat.

 

“Knock it off,” May said tiredly, “We need to have an adult conversation.”

 

Rio smiled approvingly at the rebuke.  “Sure thing,” she said.  “Send these yahoos away and let me out of here, and we can be as adult as you want.”

 

Billy curled his lips in mild disgust.  “God, you really do like them mean,” he muttered under his breath.

 

“Hey, May’s not mean,” Jemma interjected.  “She’s. . . determined.”

 

“Oooh, determined to do what?” Rio asked.  

 

“Drop the act,” May said.  “Obviously we’re here to get you back to work.  Ideally before our planet dies.”

 

The remnants of the smile fell from Rio’s face.   

 

“That bad, huh?” she said.  Her haughty demeanor wilted a bit.   “How-- how long have I been gone?” she asked.  

 

“You don’t know?” Jemma asked in surprise.  

 

“Time passes differently on the bridge,” Rio said, “And it’s meaningless in the Void.”  She closed her eyes and rolled her neck and shoulders, wincing. “I can tell it’s been a while.”

 

“Weeks,” Jemma affirmed.

 

“You feel it, don’t you?” May said, understanding dawning on her.  “Infringement on the Sacred Balance hurts you physically.”

 

“Even in human form,” Rio confirmed.  

 

“So if you rectify the damage, does that mean you’ll feel better?” Jemma asked, sensing a foothold.

 

“It does,” Rio said.  “But why do all that work? I could go back to the Void and never have to worry about the freaking Balance again.  Feels like a no-brainer to me.”

 

“If you do that, we’ll be forced to use the Darkhold to bring the bridge back.  And you-know-who would take over your job,” May said, indicating to Agatha’s side of the room.  

 

Rio scoffed.  “Let her try,” she said.  “No human could withstand my job for long.  No matter how much of a sociopath they are.”

 

“Is that what you want?” May asked.  “Recognition for your abilities?”

 

“Oh, we could do that,” Jemma added quickly.  “We could have Sword introduce you to the world as a superhero who rescued humanity from the current doomsday scenario -- “

 

Rio cut her off.  “Humans have hated me for tens of thousands of years, and they’ll stay that ignorant for as long as I keep their world alive.  Humans are beneath me.  I don’t give a shit what they think.”

 

“With one exception,” May said quietly.  

 

Rio didn’t refute it. Their side of the room fell silent.   

 

In that silence they could hear the muffled conversation drifting over from Agatha’s side of the room. 

 

“How could love be irrelevant?” Daisy was asking, her voice raised, tinged with desperation.  “If there’s still love, there still. . .possibility. Right?!  There’s still hope!”

 

“There’s no hope,” came Agatha’s voice next.

 

“She’s right,” Rio said.

 

“That’s the first thing they’ve agreed on,” Jemma noted, her previous optimism gone from her voice.  

 

Both sides of the room fell silent, the gravity of Agatha and Rio’s words wrapping around them like weighted blankets.  

 

“It’s been poisoned,” May said, after a moment.  Her voice was even and emotionless, but her eyes shone with unshed tears.  “The love, I mean.  Grief and guilt are poisons.”  She sat down on the concrete floor outside of Rio’s cell.  “I should know, I lost my marriage to it.”  

 

Jemma sat down and  put her hand over May’s, and signaled to Billy to take down the blue magic wall that separated Agatha and Rio’s sides of the basement.  

 

Agatha and Rio glared and glowered at each other across the room as May spoke.

 

“I was just doing my job,” May continued.  “Which was to protect civilians and other Agents by neutralizing threats, no matter their nature.  There’s no room for moral qualms in my old line of work: you make the tough call.  You don’t get to choose, you don’t get to say no.  You ARE the job.  

 

And then all of a sudden one day, the threat I had to neutralize wasn’t an alien, or a terrorist, or Hydra Nazi.  It was a child -- a twelve year old girl.  She was an Inhuman with mind control powers and violent tendencies.  She had already killed the first wave of agents, and she was about to kill a lot more, and then escape into the world unchecked.  So I did the job.” May paused and wiped her right eye with her wrist.    

 

“My coworkers celebrated me.  My husband Andrew, he. . . he was a psychologist.  A good one.  But even he couldn’t save me from myself.  And he couldn’t save our marriage.  

 

“It was poisoned,” Monica said.

 

May nodded.  “We had been trying for a child, and I couldn’t bear it anymore.  I couldn’t live with the idea that I would get to raise a child after taking the life of one.  I divorced Andrew, retired from field work for a few years.  You could say I went to my own version of the Void,” she said, glancing at Rio.   

 

“It wasn’t my child that died, but part of me did.  Along with my vision of the future with my husband, and my chance at having my own children.  I loved Andrew before the grief, and I loved him after it, and I loved him until the day he died.  It didn’t matter that eventually I internalized that I had made the right choice that day.  We still couldn’t survive the poison.”

 

“I’m so sorry,” Monica said.  

 

“I’m also no stranger to the conflicts between motherhood and the line of duty,” Jemma said, squeezing May’s hand. “When my daughter was three, I had a neural implant placed in my brain to make me forget that she existed.  The security was so stringent that it came with a kill directive should anyone try to remove it and allow me to remember her.  I left her somewhere safe where she wouldn’t know I’d been gone for any length of time, but the job would take years for me and could have ended the world if I had been blackmailed under threat of her life. Oh, I should mention the implant made me forget Fitz, too. For at least that length of time, I couldn’t have both my family and my job.” 

 

Jemma looked sympathetically at Rio.  

 

“You guys have been through a lot,” Agatha said.  Her eyes moved from Monica to Daisy to May to Jemma.  “You’ve lost people, made sacrifices.  I can’t argue with that.  But none of you has lost your own child.  Isn’t that right?”

 

The room remained silent for a moment before Monica said, “Tell us.”  

 

Agatha ran her fingers through her ghostly hair and drew in a long, heavy breath.  

 

“I always wanted children, always,” she began. “I didn’t know how or when or with who, but having them was a certainty.  Growing up, my future children were as real to me as my magic.  I felt like Nicky was with me my whole life, long before we made him or my body built him, or I played with him, or took care of him.

 

When he was a newborn, those first few weeks, I remember feeling. . . strange.  Surprised.  Because I didn’t love him.”  Agatha shook her head, eyes closed.  “I didn’t love him.

 

I didn’t love him, because love wasn’t the right word.  It wasn’t big enough.  I’d felt love -- as a child, fleetingly, for my mother, before I knew better.   For a few childhood friends, maybe, back when I had those.  For my pets, goats and horses I’d raised as a teenager, then my rabbits -- ‘fur babies’ I think you’d call them these days.  And. . . “ Agatha’s eyes opened and she gazed at Rio.  

 

“But what I felt for Nicky wasn’t like any of that.  I didn’t believe in souls, but I knew he had mine now.  It wasn’t ‘I love you’ I felt, it was devotion. To the point of violence. I knew I would be everything he needed; he was mine to protect.   It was the truest thing I had ever felt.  I don’t feel a lot of connection to this world, but being his mother felt. . .cosmic. 

 

I had him my whole life.  I didn’t know who he would be, but I knew that he would be.

 

And then . . . he was gone.  It was over.”

 

Agatha gripped the EMF bars and spat her next words at Rio.     

 

“And I will never.  Understand. How his MOTHER could let that happen.”

 

Rio braced herself against the basement wall, swaying unsteadily.

 

“Because if you felt what I felt,” Agatha continued, “It wouldn’t matter what else you were.  You were his mother first.  You say you don’t have a choice, but you do: you can wait.  If you turn your back, the spirit might go back to the body.  You’ve told me that.  Maybe the world suffers, but how much?  How much, spread across the whole world?  One child’s soul refused -- Oh, what? Did an apple blossom wilt somewhere?  Did a baby fail to conceive until the next month, the parents blissfully unaware?  How badly did the world suffer compared to how much I suffered?  How badly did it suffer compared to now?  You say you don’t have a choice, but it seems to me you made a choice three weeks ago that’s a whole lot worse for the Sacred fucking Balance than saving one little boy.”

 

Agatha didn’t let tears fall, but her eyes shone with them, and her voice was coated with a layer of gravel.  Rio doubled over, bracing her forearms against her thighs.  

 

“All you ever talk about is the extra time you gave me before you went ahead and took him anyway.  Did you even grieve for him?  For what he could have been?  What he could be right NOW if you hadn’t--”

 

“Agatha -- “ Monica started to say.

 

“Please calm down,” Jemma murmured.

 

“You’re upsetting her,” Daisy said.

 

“Everyone be quiet,” Rio said.  She used her deep, echoing unearthly voice.  Except, quietly this time.  To the agents, it was more unsettling than when she shook the walls with it.  

 

“I can speak for myself,” she continued in her human voice.  She crossed the space to the wall nearest Agatha and stared at her, fire in her eyes.  

 

“You want to know if I grieved,” Rio repeated.  Her voice simmered, low with rage.  “Two hundred seventy-five years later, and she finally asks me if I grieved.

 

I WAS HIS MOTHER!” she shouted, voice tearing at the edges.  “AND his father.  Maybe he was always with you, Agatha, but I gave you the other half of him.  You remember that, right?”

 

Rio let go of the fence and paced to the wall and back.  “Did I grieve, did I grieve.”  She stopped and faced Agatha.  “I spent months feeling him kick my hand through your flesh while I held you both at night.  I felt him get stronger and stronger and more alive every day in the body of the woman I loved.  

 

Did I grieve?  How would you even know?   I got thirty seconds to look into his eyes and understand what you were about to take away from me.  Agatha, I begged you to come home, and instead you ran away.  I was a mother and a father, but I never got the chance to be a parent, because YOU decided it.  YOU shut me out.  

 

But hey, that’s no big deal, right?  Rio’s just a tourist.  She doesn’t really care what happens in the human world to her human family because she can just fuck off to the afterlife and chill.  

 

That’s what you think, right?  That’s why you’d get so mad if I was gone a little later?  Why you never truly trusted me to come home?  Well, guess what?  I NEEDED that time, Agatha.  I can’t do my job as a human -- no one could.  I can’t have the mind of someone with a wife, and a child on the way, while I light the path for the souls of people who are wracked with the deepest versions of anger, shock, regret, fear.  Not to mention the living they leave behind.  The spouses, the siblings, the children, and, yes -- the parents.  The fathers doubled over, throwing up, the mothers wailing with their hearts torn out and shattered.  I see all of that, Agatha, and more. I saw it long before you wanted a child. And the longer I spent with you, the longer it would take me each time to force space between that part of myself and the part that could come home and love you.

 

And instead of understanding, that made you mad.  

 

Maybe I made a stupid choice to love a human, but you made a choice, too.  You knew what I was.  And at least I tried to understand from your perspective.  But not you, not Agatha Harkness.  It’s allllllll about YOU.  Your pain and your rage and what you lost.  It’s all about how you could never forgive me, but guess what?  I can never forgive you, either.  The difference is I’m not too much of a coward to admit that I still love you anyway.   

 

I kept this form, the one I picked out for you, in hopes that every time I found you, you’d be a little closer to acceptance.  And every few decades, just for a night or two, I’d think we still had a chance. That was the cruelest part of all.”

 

Rio scoffed.

 

“And you ask me if I grieve.  

 

I have been grieving since the day I MET YOU!  Do you know that the amount of time you had with Nicky is an ETERNITY compared to the percent of my life that I would have had with you -- with both of you? If we had gotten five hundred years together in the living world, that would have been the blink of an eye compared to the length of my life I’ll spend without you afterwards.  I knew it from the beginning.  And I lived with it.  I chose to love you for the time I had.  

 

Did I grieve.  I’ve done nothing BUT grieve, for BOTH OF YOU.  I grieved, and I grieved, and I grieved, until there was nothing left to do but want you dead.  So I could finally be free of you.  

 

And you can’t even give me that much.”

 

Rio turned her back on the room.  

 

Her shoulders slumped and she buried her face in her hands.  She let out one long, mournful sob.  

 

Agatha knelt on the floor of her cell, looking smaller than they had ever seen her, her ghostly hair falling around her.  

 

Through the silence came the sound of a few smaller, quieter sobs.  They all turned to see Billy, who was sitting on the bottom step of the basement stairs with his knees drawn up to his chest.  Tears streamed down his cheeks, and his eyes glowed bright, magical blue.   A translucent crown rested, glowing, on his forehead.  He was rubbing his temples and grimacing as though in pain.  

 

“Rio,” he sniffled, getting slowly to his feet.  He took down Monica’s electric bars with a wave of his hand and approached her.  She turned to face him.  

 

“Rio, you have to show her,” he said.  

 

Rio and Billy shared a long gaze, and then Rio, understanding something unspoken, nodded.

 

Billy reached up and pulled a strand of hair from Rio’s head.  He strode towards Agatha and waved away her bars as well.  She looked up at him through tears and the hair falling in her face.  

 

“You have to do it,” he said, and held out Rio’s hair for her to take.  

 

Agatha got to her feet and reached out her hand, taking the strand of Rio’s hair between her thumb and forefinger.  Billy nodded at her, and then took a step back.  

 

Agatha turned to face Rio.  

 

“Repete memoria,” she said, their eyes locked on each other.

 

The strand of Rio’s hair glowed brilliant green, and then disappeared.  

 

On the wall behind Rio, a door appeared -- it was old, made of stone, and partially overgrown with moss and ivy.  

 

Rio turned to it and reached for the handle, and it shuddered open with a heavy groan. She stepped across the threshold.  Agatha followed her.  




Chapter 4: Rio

Chapter Summary

Rio takes Agatha on a tour through her memories, with a lot of surprising revelations in both the past and the present.

Chapter Notes

Content warning specifically for this chapter: Rio is Death. Not all of her memories are nice, to say the least.

Please be aware that the first third of this chapter includes people being quite upset by their own deaths.

This includes a suicide victim.

This also includes the aftermath of a school shooting.

Both scenes are brief and non-graphic, but they are sad. I will clarify if you ask me.

 

 

Location:  Under a memory spell

Date:  unknown



Agatha and Rio passed through the conjured door, which vanished behind them, and stepped into a world lush with greenery -- pines and oaks and maples towering above them, brush and brambles around their legs, and bright, soft moss growing vibrantly on bark and rock.  The angle of the sun in the cloudless sky placed the time at midday, but the trees grew so thick that their leaves high above cooled the forest floor.  The trees themselves proclaimed this place’s claim to thousands of years of uninterrupted growth; their bark was a feast of colors and textures, the trunks sometimes skinny and bending under the weight of their own branches, sometimes old and scarred by encounters with long-departed living things.  Some were so broad that human arms couldn’t hope to embrace even half of their circumference.  The untouched ground beneath their feet was invisible beneath roots erupting from the soil, unkempt brush, and long-fallen branches.  Birdsong and the rush of a nearby creek were the only sounds.  

 

Powerful nostalgia drew a sharp exhale from Agatha.  She could never forget the untouched New England forests of her youth.   

But they were in Rio’s memory, and Agatha was certain they weren’t there to stroll peacefully down memory lane.  

“Why are we here?” she asked.

Rio didn’t answer, and instead walked silently away from Agatha, leading her towards the sound of the stream.  Agatha, without much choice but to go where Rio’s memory led, followed a few paces behind her.  

Though it had been hundreds of years and she had seen thousands of acres of forest in her days, Agatha quickly, and with a heavy heart, placed their specific location.  They were passing by familiar landmarks -- a pair of boulders, waist-high, one cracked; a fallen log dotted with patches of bright orange fungus; a small, scraggly apple tree that made Agatha’s mouth pucker when she thought of its fruit.  When the realization hit her, she stopped in her tracks -- partially out of defiance, and partially because her legs had gone wobbly, and weak.

“I don’t want to be here,” Agatha said with urgency.  “Whatever this is, I don’t want to see it.”

“Be quiet, Agatha,” Rio said.  “You know what this spell does.”

“It uses memories to answer a question.  This is the way to his grave.  What question did I --”

“You asked me if I grieve,” Rio said, cutting her off.  

Upon Rio’s words, the scene shifted around them.  Now they stood a few yards away from NIcky’s burial site, a mound of flat rocks on a small, protected bank next to the running water.  Agatha stepped toward it and when she reached it, laid her ghostly hand flat on one of the sun-warmed rocks.  She closed her eyes as the flame of dormant grief reignited.  

“I didn’t have a shovel, so I stole one from a barn in the village,” she said quietly.  

“And then you carried the rocks that protected him,” Rio said, standing next to her.  “Covered him so he could rest in peace.  Just like humans have been doing for tens of thousands of years.”

“So you were stalking me, even then?  That’s how you knew this was here?”

“Agatha.  Of course I knew where you buried our son.”

As Rio spoke, a figure clad in black robes emerged from the brush across the pile of rocks.  Startled, Agatha stepped backwards and rolled her wrist as though to conjure a magic bolt.  

“It’s a memory, Agatha,” Rio reminded her.  “She can’t see us.” 

As the figure pulled the hood from her head and crouched beside the pile of rocks, Agatha realized that the “she” was Rio.  A remembered version of her that had visited Nicky’s grave in 1762.  

And Memory Rio was crying.  “I’ll miss you,” she murmured. 

She caught a falling tear in her hand and pressed it into the soil.  When she lifted it, a tuft of small, green sprouts pushed upwards and immediately flowered into a rainbow of small blossoms.   

Agatha shook her head.  “I should have known you put those there.”  After a pensive pause, she added, “They came back every year.”

Present day Rio looked at Agatha pointedly.  

“The town expanded and took over the land eventually,” she said.

“1768,” Agatha said.  

“I brought him flowers every year until then.”  

 

Agatha turned her back on the emotional scene.  

“Okay, so, you visited his grave.  So what?” she asked. “Is this supposed to make me feel sorry for you?”

“For a grieving mother?  Of course not, why would you?” Rio said, rolling her eyes skyward.  

“Because you’re the one who TOOK him!”

“For the ten millionth time, I didn’t KILL HIM, but you make me wish that I could JOIN HIM!”

“So do it!!” Agatha shouted.  “Just go there and stay there!”

“Well I would, but I think your world is finding out first hand right now just how much it needs me to do my job.”

“It’s such a stupid system,” Agatha grumbled.  “We could find our way across the bridge on our own.  Why is your JOB even necessary?  You’re nothing but a glorified crossing guard.”

 

As present-day Agatha and Rio shouted at each other in front of the memory of their son’s grave, memory-Rio blew a kiss, put her hood up over her head, and disappeared back into the brush.  

A weathered wooden door appeared in her place.  The memory had concluded and they were being ushered to the next one.  Their shouting silenced for now, Agatha and Rio opened the new door, and stepped through it.  



On the other side was a barn loft.  It was full of bales of hay and wooden crates, and a small area of floor space where a pile of blankets seemed to be serving as a temporary bed.  

As they passed through the door and onto the wooden planks, the scene solidified around them. Agatha heard anguished screaming that started off as muffled and muted but gradually became louder, clearer, and closer.  Panic hit the pit of her belly -- why was Rio screaming like that?  Wait -- no, that wasn’t Rio.  

Agatha noticed a girl she hadn’t seen before; she was the one doing the screaming.  She was young, probably barely twenty, and had curly hair and freckles.  She was dressed in the clothing of Agatha’s youth.

She was doubled over, screaming the word, “NO!”  Agatha winced at the volume, and the distraught tone.  

It was then that she noticed the unmoving figure on the floor.  The screaming girl was a spirit, and her lifeless body lay beside her.  

Rio appeared in the memory then, a shadowy apparition in the corner, clothed in her reaping robes, her skeletal face obscured by black cloth.  As she stepped out of the shadows, the girl’s spirit turned to face her.      

“Please! Please send me back, please!” she begged.  

Rio only stood still and held up one finger to point at the bridge that appeared behind her.             

“No! NO.  My mother…” the girl sobbed.  “Please, it’s not fair.  It’s not FAIR.  She promised she wouldn’t hurt me, but she did! They tricked me!  She was a witch, she --  stole my power and then -- and then my life.”

Rio started to usher the girl toward the bridge.  The girl refused to budge.

“No! I won’t go!  I’m going to find her and kill her.  I’ll kill Agnes like she killed me!”  

Upon hearing her murderous pseudonym, Agatha realized why this girl and the setting looked familiar.  She and Rio had seduced this girl - and then taken her life.  

“Joan,” she whispered, and looked at Rio in a panic.  Rio did not look back.  She stood calmly, watching the scene unfold.  

Deathly Memory Rio was similarly serene.  “No,” she said, “It’s time to go.”

“No!  No, wait!  What -- what if they were right?  The godly?   I don’t want to go to hell!”

Joan tried to lash out physically at Rio, but their non-corporeal forms passed right through each other.  Rio stood behind the girl’s spirit and placed her hands in front of Joan’s face.  Tendrils of black death magic rose from her fingertips. Joan breathed in, and the magic entered her nose and mouth.  

The change was immediate.  Joan's body relaxed, and her voice quieted.  She turned toward the bridge with a look of peaceful wonder on her face.  

“Ohh,” she said in a half-whisper.  “Do I go through that tunnel?” she asked. 

Rio didn’t answer, but Joan stepped onto the bridge.  

“I see my Gramma!” she exclaimed.  “Ohhh.  She loves me.”

Joan crossed the bridge without any further prompting. She disappeared on the other side of it, followed closely by Rio. Then the bridge itself was gone, too.

 

The barn loft was empty now, except for the unclothed body of the deceased young woman.  Into this sad and lonely scene, Memory Rio reappeared.  This time, she was in human form, wearing not her deathly robes but plain, colonial-era clothing.  She crouched next to Joan’s naked body.  She closed the girl’s eyes with her hand, and then with a wave, put Joan’s clothes, which had been strewn about the loft, back onto her body.  Rio laid a blanket across her, sighed deeply, and then magicked herself away.  

There was no time to react to the memory before the next door appeared before them.

 

This time it was the sliding doors of a modern day hospital.  Rio stepped through them into a chaotic hospital lobby.  Agatha followed her down corridors and up several flights of stairs.  They entered the Oncology wing, and then a single room inside it.  The light was gray, cold.  Lifeless.  

The soul of a departed woman stood next to Death, and a bridge was visible behind them.  The woman was tall, or at least her confident posture made her seem so.  She had radiant dark skin, and black hair cropped at her chin.  She wore a decorated US Air Force uniform.  Her spirit was a far cry from the ashen, frail, chemical and cancer-depleted body in the bed next to them, hooked up to a wall of silent and still life support machines and monitors.                  

She turned to Death and asked quietly, “It’s over?”

“It’s over,” Rio said.  

“I wish I could have fought harder.”

“You fought as hard as you could.  Time to go.”

Maria gazed past Rio at the bridge.

Her voice broke as she asked, “Is my daughter in there?”

Rio shook her head.  

Maria hung her head.  “Because she didn’t die.”

“She didn’t die,” Rio affirmed.

“The people who were blipped aren’t in the Afterlife because they never existed.”  Tears fell, leaving tracks on her cheeks.  “Well, I don’t accept that.  Monica did exist, and I need to see her again.”

“She’s not here,” Rio said quietly.

“She’ll be back, I know she will,” Maria said, anguished.  “Sometimes people come back when you never had any hope that they would.  Please, I can’t go yet.  I need to stay until my daughter comes back.”

“I can’t let you do that,” Rio said calmly.  “If she comes back, she’ll join you here some day.”

“That’s not good enough.  I need to see her again.  Please, she’s my baby.”

Rio held out her arm, pointing towards the bridge.

“I promise I won’t bother anybody,” Maria said, in a last-ditch effort.  “I won’t interfere with the living.  I’ll go somewhere quiet, and I’ll wait.  When she comes back and I see her again, I’ll go.”

Rio dropped her hand, and stared into Maria’s eyes.  “You promise these things?” she said sternly.             

“I do.  Please.  We were cheated.”

“I know you were.”  

Death Rio turned her back to Maria’s spirit.

“Thank you,” Maria said through tears.  She became translucent and then enveloped in shimmering smoke-like mist.  And then she was gone.

Death Rio grimaced, grabbed her head with both hands and growled in pain.  

Agatha turned to present day Rio.  “You let Monica’s mother go.”

“The Blip was a perversion of the Sacred Balance.”

“So you can bend the rules when you want to.  It hurts, but you can do it.”

“Most people don’t ask to become ghosts.  Most people want to live.”

 

A stone archway appeared, revealing the next memory, and they stepped through it into a dramatic stone atrium.  The ceiling arched above them, so tall that Agatha couldn’t see the top for the bright sunlight streaming in from the skylights.  A cluster of pink and purple flowering trees grew out of a fountain bed in the center; weeds sprouted between every pair of stones and moss coated the statues along the perimeter.  Ivy climbed the walls so high it got lost in the sunlight above.

Around the perimeter of the circular space were stone archways. She couldn’t see inside of them, but they each emitted light of a particular brightness and hue, suggest that each one led to somewhere else.  Or, some-when else - a new memory.  

Curiosity drew Agatha toward the one nearest to them in the clockwise direction.  When she peeked beyond its threshold, it was night. As Agatha crossed to the inside of it, she could hear the sounds of traffic.  The faint wail of a siren kicked up in the distance, then another joined it out of sync.  As she stood just inside the doorway, the scene materialized around her.  She and present-day Rio stood on the sidewalk by the railing of a four-lane bridge.  Agatha could hear the rush of a river below. Several cars were pulled over to the nearby shoulder, and a few people stood beside their open doors, speaking in hushed tones.  

A young man in a t-shirt and ripped jeans stood with his head in his hands, talking to Deathly Rio. 

“I shouldn’t have done it!” he wailed in anguish.  “As soon as I let go of the railing, I knew it was a mistake.  I regretted it before I even started to fall. I regret it!” he repeated.  The cacophony of the sirens grew closer, louder.  “Please, let me try again,” he said.   “I’ll call the number this time instead -- my mother -- she’ll never recover --”

Agatha recoiled from the heartbreaking scene before her, and rushed back across the threshold into the atrium.  But already, the next door, only a few paces away, was drawing her in.  She floated toward it, full of dread, but unable to resist its pull.  

Flashing red and blue lights emanated from within.  Inside, a chaotic golden hour scene of police and plainclothes people shouted to each other, and groups of people stood by barricades and clung to one another, crying.  Helicopters hovered above, their searchlights sweeping the scene as far as the eye could see.  On the hill a short distance away stood a large, low brick building barricaded by police officers and yellow hazard tape.  When Agatha saw the group of small children surrounding Deathly Rio, the horror bloomed in the pit of her stomach.  There were twelve, maybe more, and none could have been older than 7 or 8.   They wore backpacks and buttoned up winter coats, hats with built-in earmuffs or tassels on the top, light up sneakers or brightly colored winter boots.  They each had one hand on the shoulder of the child in front of them, as they always did when they walked with a teacher outside.

Death Rio was urging them to begin their journey over the bridge.  

"Oh, no, no,” Agatha murmured.  

“I want my mommy,” one of the children was crying. 

“Me too!” another cried out.   

“You’ll be safe in there,” Rio said to them.  “You won’t be scared anymore.”

“Are my mommy and daddy in there?”

“Not yet, but they will be.”

One child looked up at Rio as he passed by her on the way to the bridge.

“Are you an angel?”

Immediately, Rio’s robes changed from black to white, the cape across her back lifted behind her, evoking wings.  

“Do I look like one?”

The child nodded.  Rio placed a reassuring hand on his head as she passed.

The last soul to cross the bridge was not a child, but a young woman.  Agatha hadn’t noticed her before.  

As she passed Rio, she stopped, looked over at her and asked, “Do you know if I saved any?”

“Three,” Rio said.  

The woman let out a small sob, and nodded her head.  Rio nodded back at her, and she turned to cross the bridge.

 

Agatha pushed past present-day Rio and practically threw herself through the stone archway back into the atrium.  Desperately, she looked around.  She had to get out of this torture chamber.  

The entrance they had come through was gone now; all that remained were the archways, each one, Agatha was now sure, containing a vision of some human tragedy that Death had borne witness to.  Craning her head upwards, she realized that there were many stories above, stretching up as far as she could see, each one studded by its own perimeter of archways.  Layer upon layer, like the circles of hell.  

She tried to dematerialize, to no avail, and Agatha learned then that one cannot escape a spell that way, even as a ghost.  

She propelled herself through the atrium, avoiding looking inside any of the doorways, lest they draw her in.  There were no exits that she could discern, no stairwells or doors to open.  The only possible way out seemed to be up.  Well, that was one advantage to her non-corporeal form.  She floated upwards towards the domed skylight above.

As the rose higher, the diameter of the room shrank.  Closing in on her -- maybe this was a mistake.  She tried to focus on the light above her, but eventually, as the doorways drew closer she couldn’t avoid exposure to the scenes on the other side of them.  

Scenes of car accidents, swimming pools, houses still smoldering in flames, dark alleyways, battlefields, gallows and guillotines and electric chairs, buildings flattened into dust - whether from earthquakes or missile strikes, it was impossible to tell.  And in all of them, Death’s robes flowing around her while humans stood nearby, shoulders hunched, bodies doubled over or down on their knees.  A cacophony of screams, and sobs, and wails filled her ears.

“Please!”

“Send me back!”

“Let me try again!”

“My brother needs me!”

“My kids still need me!”

“My wife won’t know what happened to me!”

“Who will feed my cat?  She’ll die!”

“I need more time!”

“I need more time!”

“I need more time!”

 

The last doorway Agatha passed before she reached the top of the atrium held a simple, modern hospital room.  A woman, belly still round from pregnancy, lay in the bed.  A man draped himself around her from a chair pulled close, holding her as they cried.  A hospital chaplain bowed his head at the foot of the bed.  

In the doorway, Rio stood with a baby in her arms.  She gazed at the mourning parents for a moment before she turned and walked with the child over the bridge.  

Agatha rose through the top of the atrium, towards bright sunlight that suddenly went black as she passed through.

She was surrounded by nothingness.  Silence.  

She had never felt so relieved.  She could see nothing, hear nothing, and feel nothing, except the buzzing of Rio’s presence nearby. 

She fought back tears of outrage.  

“Is this your void?” she tried to bellow, but it was as though her voice was swallowed by the darkness.

“It is,” came the answer, distorted like they were underwater.

“Isn’t this against the rules?  Bringing a human here?”  

“You’re a ghost, and this is a memory.”  

“So, revenge by psychological torture, huh?  You’re cruel,” Agatha spat into the nothingness.    

“You needed to see,” Rio’s voice came calmly. “Still think I’m just a crossing guard?”   

  “I don’t feel sorry for you,” Agatha said, declining to answer the question.  “I’m not an idiot, I know it doesn’t affect you the same way it affects m-- humans.  Your job is to get people over the bridge by any means necessary.  You didn’t. . .you don’t . . .feel all of that.” 

“Let me remind you that we are in my memories, Agatha. I may not feel those things as Death but I can still remember them all as a human.  All of those rooms are things that I carry with me, do you understand that?  And I could fill thousands of places like that one.”

Agatha could feel Rio’s rage radiating through the void.  It was satisfying, but terrifying.  It might not be wise to antagonize her in her own memory spell, but temperance had never been her strong suit.  

“What does that have to do with me?  It’s not my fault that’s your job!”

“You should have cared, Agatha.  You should have cared about what it’s like for me. I let you get away with being completely self-centered for SO long in the name of protecting you.”

“Protecting me from what?”

“From the things I can’t unsee!”    

 

Agatha felt herself being pulled -- no, dragged -- away from the stillness of the void.  She was wrenched out of the darkness and into bright light.

Compared to the horrors she had just witnessed, the memory Agatha found herself in this time around came as a massive relief.  As her eyes adjusted she took in the scenery: palm trees, dramatic white clouds, a beach with the ocean lapping in the distance, a beach bungalow a short distance across the sand.  

She recognized this place.  

“This is where we decided to have Nicky,” she said in startled awe.  Then she snapped, “Why are we here?”

“Because you asked what this had to do with YOU, Agatha!”

As Rio spoke the words, the memory versions of the two of them appeared in the scene.  They were sitting cross-legged on the floor of the bungalow, hands clasped together, deep in an undecipherable conversation.  

“God, we were so young,” Agatha murmured.  

Suddenly, memory Agatha threw Rio’s hands at her, got up, and stormed down the beach, disappearing into the palm trees.

As Agatha strode away, Memory Rio put her head in her hands and shouted, “Fuck!”  She stood, and started pacing the beach in front of the bungalow.  She was muttering to herself, her hands gesturing wildly as though engaged in some unseen conversation.

“What are you saying?” Agatha asked, and stepped closer to the furiously animated memory version of Rio.

“Tell her, you coward.  Just tell her!” Memory Rio hissed to herself.  

“Tell me what?” Agatha asked current Rio.

Memory Rio continued pacing.  “You can’t tell her.  She’ll think you’re sabotaging it.  Again.  But she needs to understand.  Just tell her!”

Agatha watched Memory Rio’s inner turmoil in fascination.  She had never seen her so agitated unless it was in a fight with her.

Rio stopped pacing only after wearing a deep path in the sand.  She had clearly come to some decision.  She faced the beach and ran her hands through her hair, sighing deeply.  She walked to the corner of the bungalow where Agatha’s satchel lay, and rummaged until she found a hairbrush.  She stood up straight, pulled her shoulders square, and walked down the beach to find Memory Agatha.  

Current Agatha turned to Rio.  “What didn’t you tell me?”

Current Rio crossed her arms over her chest.  “That I had just carried sixteen newborn babies across the bridge.  And held the hands of a hundred children, and eased the transition for thousands of boys who had never yet grown a hair from their face.  Just that morning.”

Agatha was quiet.

“That’s what you were afraid of,” she said.  “It wasn’t change or responsibility.  It was death.”

“Of course that’s what I was afraid of!”

“But I know -- I knew that people die, Rio.  Babies, children.  Every parent knows that going in.”

“You’d never seen what it was like for those parents.  I see it ALL the time.”

“Are you saying you didn’t really want a baby?  You wanted to make the decision based on the fear that they’ll die?”

“I am not just any parent, Agatha!  Who was always going to have to carry his soul across the bridge when he died? And I know you.  I knew if he died before you did, it would be the end of us, too.”

“Why didn’t you tell me the truth?  What you were really scared of?”

“Because, Agatha, you wanted children so badly. If I had said no to kids, would you have stayed with me?  And I didn’t want to say no, I wanted a family with you!  I just couldn’t figure out a way to give you my perspective without also giving you my. . .my darkness. It didn’t feel like the right way to start a family.” 

“Neither is lying about how you really feel.”

“I was trying to make you happy, Agatha.  And you’re one to talk.  Biggest liar about feelings there is.”

“What are you talking about?  When did I lie to you?!”

 

As the question left Agatha’s mouth, a new door appeared in the middle of the beach.  It was plain and white with a wooden doorframe and a silver doorknob and keyhole.  There was a number at eye level, 17B.  

A hint of a memory stirred, but Agatha couldn’t place it, not yet.
