Agatha pointed at her own chest. “Me?” She looked at Rio in confusion.  

“God, she’s been dead for weeks and she still has a big mouth,” Rio muttered.  

“It’s time to show her, Rio,” Lilia said.  “And then stop punishing her for your choice.”  

“What is she talking about?” Agatha asked.  “Wait, how can she even see us?”

Lilia’s spirit offered no answers.  She turned back to Memory Rio and said,  “I’m ready now.”   And with that, she turned and walked across the bridge, disappearing through the door on the other side.  

Agatha turned to present day Rio.  

“Time to show me what?”

 

The next door to appear was one they both knew well. It was the door to the home they shared in the woods near the beach for decades.  Agatha didn’t wait for Rio to escort her inside; she ran to the door and flung it open, and stepped into a tidal wave of nostalgia.  

Agatha’s ability to appreciate the surroundings were limited, however.  In this memory, she and Rio were having sex on the living room couch. Agatha straddled Rio’s lap, riding her fingers, tits in Rio’s face, head thrown back.  

“Again?” Agatha asked Rio, exasperated.  “This is just getting awkward.”

Then she stole another look at the pair on the couch.  

“We look good, though,” she said.  

“Yuuup,” Rio said.  

“So, wait.  This is what Lilia wanted us to see?” Agatha asked.  “Fair, but unexpected. If I had known she was into that I might’ve--”

“Agatha, please don’t finish that sentence.” 

“Fine.”  



“I’m close, Rio,” Memory Agatha was panting.  

“Come for me, baby,” Rio said.

“Let me have a taste,” Agatha said.  “It’ll help me.”

Rio moved her arm more vigorously, and Agatha moaned.  

“You don’t need that,” Rio said.

“But it feels so good,” Agatha protested.  “Pleeease.”

Rio leaned her head back against the couch and let a few thin wisps of her black magic rise from her lips.  

Agatha leaned forward and breathed it in.  

“Fuuuuck,” she whispered.  Her eyes glazed over.  She bent to kiss Rio while she climaxed.  

But after her body tensed with pleasure, it suddenly went completely limp.  Agatha fell backwards from Rio’s lap and landed on the floor, unmoving.

 

Present day Agatha chuckled.  “I remember this.  I had a bump on my head for two weeks.  Guess that taste I took was a little too big.”

Rio swallowed, and turned to Agatha with a pained look on her face.  “Agatha, when you kissed me just then, you pulled magic from my lungs. Way more than I ever would have given you on purpose. . .”

As Agatha processed what Rio was telling her, the scene around them changed.  While it was still their old living room, now it was dark, cold.  Instead of Rio sitting on the couch, she was standing next to Agatha in her black, Deathly robes.  White wisps were beginning to rise from Agatha’s motionless form. 

Agatha had now seen Rio attend a number of reapings.  Usually she stood perfectly still waiting for the person’s soul to rise and speak to her, or wordlessly drift across the bridge.  This time, Rio dove to the floor beside Agatha’s body.  She drew up her force field power  -- the one that had, many times both before and after this day -- sent Agatha careening across rooms, crashing into furniture, pinned helplessly against walls -- and directed it at the soul attempting to leave Agatha’s body.  She held one hand a few inches above Agatha’s chest, the other over her face, so that the spirit was blocked from leaving Agatha’s body.

She grimaced with effort.  “No, please,” she murmured.  “Please, please.”

Rio didn’t stop the force field for at least two minutes after the wisps of Agatha’s soul had completely returned to her body.   

 

The scene changed again, still their living room, but again full of color, warmth, and life.  Rio knelt next to Agatha, one hand on her chest, the other looking for a pulse in her neck.  

Memory Agatha’s eyes blinked open.  Memory Rio gathered her into her arms and held her tightly, and the scene faded away. 



Present-day Agatha’s mouth was agape.  

“I DIED?”

Present-day Rio stood with her face in her hands.  

“I thought I just passed out, and that’s why you never let me do it again.”

Rio didn’t move.  Agatha ran her fingers through her hair.  

“Why didn’t you ever tell me?” she asked, her voice incredulous.  

Rio took her hands away from her face.  She shook her head.  Her words came slowly.  

“At first, I was in shock.  Before this happened, I didn’t even know I could do that.  Then later I couldn’t believe I had done it.  I knew why I did, but it’s such an egregious hypocrisy, I felt--”

“You regretted it.”

“No, not then,” Rio said.  “Maybe there were moments when I thought I did, when we were at our worst.  Fighting.”

“When you’d say you wanted me dead.”

“Violation of the Balance hurts me, physically.  Your existence causes me all kinds of pain,” Rio laughed bitterly.  

“So when I died for real, the pain must have stopped.”

“In one way it did.  But, guess what?  After all those years, being without it was worse.  Turns out I missed the scar.”

“I can’t believe you never threw it in my face.  All those times I screamed at you for not saving Nicky. You had already saved me.”

“I didn’t want to acknowledge it.  I was ashamed of it.”

“Of saving a person you loved?”

“Agatha, I know it’s hard for you to understand, but sometimes the two versions of me fucking hate each other.”

Agatha digested this for a moment.  “Thank you for the special treatment,” she said gruffly.  Begrudgingly.   

Rio looked at Agatha in half shock, half confusion..

“Um.  You’re welcome.”

“I need to ask you something.”

“If I could do that for you, why couldn’t I do it for Nicky?”

Agatha nodded.  

“First of all, I did do it for him. I refused him the day he was born, when you asked me to.  And I did it a second time, too, six years later.  But I need you to hear me, Agatha -- really hear me this time when I tell you -- I couldn’t do it any longer.  His body was dying.  Yours was fine once I pulled the magic from your lungs and your body cleared out the chemicals.  You had a place to go back to.  He didn’t.”

“But, I just needed a little more time!  I was amassing the magic to save him, I was working on finding the right spells!”

“Agatha, how much time would have been enough?  How many more witches would you have killed senselessly trying to find the power to do the impossible? Meanwhile, I’m supposed to keep sending our son back to a dying body to become the walking dead?  He was suffering, Agatha.  His body needed to rest.”

Agatha blinked tears down her cheeks.   

“Was he scared?  When it happened?”

“Not at all. I can show you, if--.”

Agatha shook her head vigorously.  “No. I can’t.  Was he mad at me?” Her voice broke.  

“Why on Earth would he be mad at you, Agatha?”

“Because, I failed him.”

“I promise you, he didn’t see it that way.”  

“He was disappointed in me.  He didn’t like the siphoning.”

“He understood more than you think.”

Agatha’s head snapped upwards.  “How do you know that?”

Rio bit her bottom lip, and then pursed her lips.  “Ummm.”

“You talked to him, didn’t you?  You visited him behind my back!”

“Did I do what I had to do in order to have a relationship with my son, Agatha?  Yes, I did.  You didn’t leave me a choice.”

“Who were you to him?”

“I was. . . someone who wasn’t real.”




As the next memory began, they were again transported to the woods where Agatha and Nicky had lived.  

And there was Nicky.  He was swinging gently on a rope that was tied to a sturdy oak tree whose branches arched their way over a ditch.  He was singing to himself.  

Agatha put her hands over her mouth, and her eyes welled up with tears.  

“You okay?” Rio asked gently.

“Oh my god,” Agatha said, and wiped her eyes with the back of her hand.  “I remember that swing.  He made it all by himself.”

“I know,” Rio said, a smile on her lips.  

Nicky hopped down from the rope, and brought it with him as he climbed up one side of the ditch. He stretched it as far back as it could go, took a running start, and sailed over the ditch.  At the apex of the rope’s swing on the other side of the ditch, he jumped and crash landed in the soft grass on the other side. He whooped in delight and raced back to do it again.  

When he reached the other side, a voice spoke from behind Nicky.  

“I bet I can jump farther than you.”

Nicky whirled around, and a child, a boy about his age with thick dark hair and dark eyes, emerged from behind a tree.

“Can I try?”

Nicky startled.  “Uhh, okay,” he said.  He handed the boy the rope.  The boy took it, and stretched it backwards up the hill, just like Nicky had done.  But the boy lost his grip halfway through the swing, and tumbled into the dirt, laughing uproariously.   

"That was fun,” he said, when Nicky approached to make sure he was okay.

Nicky laughed.  “It’s more fun if you make it to the other side.  Look, see the knot down here at the bottom?  That’s where you put your feet.  It holds you up.”

“Can you show me?” the boy asked.

Nicky repeated his successful transit across the ditch, making sure to plant his feet firmly on the knot at the bottom of the rope to show the boy what to do.

“Whoaaaa!!” the boy exclaimed and then raced to catch the rope, which was swinging wildly.  “My turn!”

Nicky’s new friend landed in the dirt a few more times, but eventually made it to the other side. They immediately challenged each other to jump from swings that peaked higher and higher.

When Nicky decided he was going to try it with a big stick in hand and jump off the swing to slay a dragon in midair, the other boy frowned.

“I don’t think that’s a good idea, Nicky,” he said.  “What if you land on your sword?  It could poke your guts right out.”

“I guess you’re right,” Nicky agreed with resignation.  He sat down in the dirt and began digging a hole with a stick.  The other boy joined him.  

“Hey, how did you know my name?” Nicky asked.  

“I’ve seen you around,” the boy said.  “I heard your mom say it.”

“What’s your name?”

“It’s Luc.”

Present-day Agatha shook her head.  “Fuck.  Luke. His ‘imaginary’ friend.” 

“You never suspected?” Rio asked.

“I thought it was his way of coping with always being on the move.  Having a friend who was there no matter where we went.”

“I knew if I showed up as a random adult in his life you’d know immediately.  Plus, this was more fun.  You never told me how fun it is to be a kid.”

“It’s not that fun for everyone,” Agatha muttered.  

“Touche,” Rio replied.  



In the memory scene, the new friends continued their conversation.

“How old are you?” Luc asked Nicky.  

“I’m five.  Do you live here?”

“Not really,” Luc said.  “I travel around a lot.”

“Me too,” Nicky said.  “My mama and I have lived lots of different places.”

“That must be fun,” Luc said.

“Yeah, we know all the Colonies,” Nicky said.  He was boasting for his new friend, but something in his voice sounded sad. Wistful.

“Did you make that swing?” Luc asked.

“Yeah,” Nicky said, his smile returning.  

“Holy cow,” Luc said.  “How did you get the rope way up on that branch?”

“Well,” Nicky said, and he got up and ran to the trees. “See, this tree was strong enough for a swing, but too big for me to climb it.  This one I could climb, but the branch would break from swinging.  So I climbed up the little one and jumped from that branch to that one,” he said, pointing high above their heads.  “With the rope.”

 

“Oh my Jesus Christ,” Agatha said upon hearing this, and ran her fingers through her hair, and turned a full 360 degrees in agitation.  “I should never have let him out of my sight.”

“It was probably the best day of his life, Agatha,” Rio said with a chuckle.  “Look how proud he is.”

Instead Agatha looked at Rio, who was looking on proudly at their son and his antics.  

Rio caught her staring.  “You sure you’re okay? You look a little green.”

"I’m fine,” Agatha snapped.  



“Hey, can you come with me so I can show you something?” Luc was asking Nicky in the memory.

“How far is it?” Nicky asked with concern.  “I’m not allowed to go past the blue pine tree.”

“It’s a little farther than that,” Luc said.  “But I promise your mama won’t mind.”

Agatha glared at Rio.  

“What? I didn’t know about the pine tree rule, God,” Rio said.  



Agatha and Rio followed Nicky and Luc past the remembered pine tree and through some dense brush until they reached a tiny clearing in the trees.  Planted in the small patch of grass, hidden away among the pines, was a garden.  It was clearly abandoned -- tall weeds grew between the vegetables, and the wire fence was rusted and falling over.  

Nicky looked at Luc in unbridled excitement.  Wordlessly, the boys began picking vegetables from the plants and shoveling them into their faces as fast as they could, giggling as they went.  Nicky hungrily gnawed on a tomato with one hand and a fistful of green beans in the other.  

“Oh, Nicky, you have to wash those,” Agatha said with a sigh.  

“Nah, our hands were dirtier than those carrots we’re pulling out of the ground,” Rio said with a chuckle.  

“You planted it,” Agatha said, gesturing at the “abandoned” garden.

“I did.  You know, you were never as good at foraging as you thought you were,” Rio said. 

Agatha stared at Rio.  

“What?  Raspberry bushes, patches of chanterelles, random black walnut trees.  Somehow they were always near your path, weren’t they?” 

Agatha clicked her tongue.  “You rigged the game.”

“The game of keeping my son fed?  Yes.  And that’s a funny way of saying ‘thanks for making sure we had fresh food most of the year’.”

“Are you saying I wasn’t a good provider for my son?”

Rio shrugged.  “No.  I’m saying we both were.”




“Let’s take some home for our mamas,” Luc said to Nicky.  “Here, fill up your pockets!”

Luc stuffed Nicky’s jacket and pants pockets with ears of corn, carrots, and peppers.  

Then Agatha’s voice rang out from a distance.

“I gotta go,” Nicky said.  “Mama’s calling me.”

“Okay,” Luc said.  “Thanks for letting me play on your swing, Nicky.”

“Can you play tomorrow?” Nicky asked.

“I’ll see if I’m allowed,” Luc said.  

Agatha’s voice called out again.

“Coming!” Nicky called back.  “Bye, Luke!”

Memory Agatha met Nicky near his swing.  

“Nicholas, what did we agree on about the pine tree?” she said sternly.

“I know, Mama, I’m sorry, but look!”  He pointed to his overstuffed pockets.  

“Well, what do you have there?” she asked.

“We found this garden, Mama, and it was underneath all these weeds and had a broken fence, but it was full of stuff, look!”

“Wow!  That’s a lot of good food, Nicky, thank you.”  She took some of the vegetables out of Nicky’s pockets to help carry them.   “Did anybody see you take this?”

Nicky shook his head emphatically.  “There was nobody there, Mama.”

“So, who’s we?” Agatha asked as they headed up the hill towards their camp.  

“Oh, me and my new friend Luke!  We played on the swing together.”

“Luke, huh?  Can I meet him, is he still here?” Agatha asked, looking around.  

Nicky turned around and looked towards the pines.  

“I guess he went home,” Nicky said.  “But he said he might come back tomorrow.”

“Ah,” Agatha said, “We have to pack up tomorrow, buddy.  Time to find our next place.”

“Please, Mama, just one more day?”

“Sorry, kid.  Time to go.”

Nicky turned and looked sadly over his shoulder at his rope swing, and the scene faded to black.  



Rio shook her head.  “I was so pissed you guys left the next day.  That garden would have fed you for a week.  He left the rope swing, though.  He could have climbed up and untied it, but he didn’t.”

“You think he left it for you.”

“I do.”

“But you visited him again?”

“Many times.”

“And he just thought you were a regular kid who kept showing up wherever we went?”

“Oh, no,” Rio laughed.  “He was totally on to me.”




A new scene faded in.  Nicky and Luc stood calf-deep in a babbling stream; their shoes had been tossed gleefully into a pile of leaves a few meters away.  They bent at the waist, their hands also plunged into the cold water that cascaded over rocks and fallen branches.  Luc meticulously lifted every flat, heavy rock that lay partially submerged, checked underneath it, and laid it gently back down.  Nicky stood with his hands cupped under the water, ready to capture any small creature that might be shaken loose by Luc’s interventions in its environment -- crayfish, salamanders, minnows, frogs or toads.  The boys had both decided long ago that a turtle would be the ultimate jackpot; they had never yet been quite so fortunate.  

Nicky stood up abruptly as a coughing fit wracked his frail body.  

“Why don’t we take a break?” Luc asked, looking on with concern.  “That rock looks good for sitting,” he said, pointing to a large, flat boulder a short distance downstream.  

Nicky nodded.  The coughing was over, but his chest heaved with effort.  

They sat together on the rock, mostly in silence, tossing pebbles into the rushing water and poking at the moss with scavenged sticks.  

“You’re not really a kid, are you?” Nicky asked.

“What makes you say that?”

“You don’t talk like a kid.  You’re always worried about me and telling me not to do things all the time.  Kind of like my mama, but in a nicer way.”

 

(Present-day Agatha scoffed in indignation while Rio smirked.)

 

Nicky continued, “Plus, you visit me wherever I go.  And I’ve never heard your mother call you.”

“You have your mama’s intuition,” Luc said.

“You know my mama?”

“I used to.”

“Who are you?” Nicky asked.

“I’m. . . someone who wanted to have a chance to get to know you, Nicky.  Is that okay?”

Nicky nodded.  “You’re fun to play with.”

“Thanks.  You too, Nicky.”

“Are you a witch?  My mama says that witches are usually a lot older than they look. I saw you use your green once.”

“You did?”

“You greened a bush in the woods and grew blueberries for us to eat.”

Luc nodded.  “I’m kind of a witch, yes.”

“If you’re a witch, you should stay away from my mama.  She’ll take your green.”

“Oh no,” Luc said.  “Okay, well thank you for warning me.”

Nicky sighed heavily, and even over the gurgling stream, Luc could hear the rattle in his chest.  It could be a trick of the late afternoon light making his lips look tinged with blue, but Luc didn’t think it was.

“Do you know why she does that, Nicky?” Luc asked gently.  “Takes magic from other witches?”

“She says you can’t trust other witches, so you have to take their powers before they can hurt you.  She’s teaching me how to do it so I can protect myself.”

Nicky’s little mouth curled into a frown, and he tried valiantly not to cry.    

“Does that upset you?”

“I don’t want to kill witches!  And I wish my mama didn’t have to, either.  I wish she could just stop and we could live in a house and be happy.”

Luc put his hand on Nicky’s.  “I wish that for you too, Nicky.  I think she’s just trying to keep you safe.”

“I know.  She wants to make me better.  She reads her spell books all day trying to figure it out.”

“Because of your cough?”

Nicky nodded.

“Do you feel sick?”

Nicky nodded again. 

“Maybe you should go home and have some dinner and some rest.”

“You really don’t talk like a kid.”

Nicky stood up unsteadily.  He looked exhausted with the prospect of walking back up the hill towards home. 

“Do you want a piggyback ride?” Luc asked.  

“Won’t I be too heavy?”

“I’m stronger than I look.”

Luc carried Nicky to within a few hundred feet of the temporary shelter Agatha had made for them this month.  He set him down.  

“Nicky,” he said, “You know how I’m not really a kid, right?  Well, the next time you see me, I’m going to look a lot different.”

“Then how will I know it’s you?”

“I’ll have a green light, like this.”  Luc conjured a green flame in his palm.

“Okay,” Nicky said.  “Bye, Luke.”

“Bye, Nicky.”

Nicky walked a few steps and then turned.  “Will I see you soon?”

“Yeah, Nicky,” Luc said.  “I think you will.”

Nicky walked the rest of the way.  Luc brushed tears away from his eyes.   The memory scene faded out. 



Agatha sat down on the flat, mossy rock next to the remembered creek where her son once played.  She drew her knees up to her chest, hugged them, and put down her head.  

“I’ll give you some space,” Rio said, and turned to walk away.

Agatha lifted her head.  

“Stay.”

Rio sat down on the rock next to Agatha, just as she had sat nearly 300 years ago next to their son.  

For a long time, they sat in silence, and watched the water flow past them.

Then, the unthinkable occurred.  Agatha took Rio’s hand.  

As Rio’s fingers intertwined with Agatha’s, the scene around them began to change.

“Wait -- Rio, I’m not ready to leave!” Agatha cried out.

“I’m not either!” Rio exclaimed.  

 

When the new scene around them solidified, they found themselves at a reaping, recognizable to Agatha now by the dark lighting and lifeless feel of the surroundings.  They stood in a small, presumably cozy under other circumstances, bedroom in a modern home.  

“Another one?” Agatha asked.  “Who is it this time?”

Rio’s forehead wrinkled in confusion.  “I don’t know,” she said.  “I don’t remember it, so why would the spell bring us here?”  

She turned in circles, trying to place this apparent memory.  

“Hold on, Agatha -- there’s no memory version of me, do you see?  I haven’t been here before.”

“Yeah. Why not?”

“Because. . .wait, can’t you feel it?  We’re out of the spell.  This isn’t a memory.”

Rio pointed to the bridge that had just appeared in the room.  “Can you see that?” she asked Agatha.

“What, that giant bridge right in front of us?  Of course I can see it.”

“Excuse me,” a voice interrupted them.  They whirled around to see the spirit of an elderly woman.  She was smiling, but confused.  

“Do either of you know which way to the Afterlife?” she asked Rio and Agatha, who glanced at each other in shock.  “I’m certain I’ve passed away.”

“Um.  Put a pin in that, just for a moment?” Rio said to the woman.  She turned back to Agatha.

“Humans aren’t supposed to be able to see that,” she said, gesturing towards the bridge.

“Well, I am a ghost.”

“Not even as ghosts,”  She thought for a moment.  “Unless. . . Agatha?  Did you decide to cross the bridge?”

“Uh, absolutely the fuck not,” Agatha said.  “And what are we doing at a present-day reaping, anyway?  I thought you were on strike.”

“I don’t think I brought us here,” Rio said.  She pointed at Agatha.  “You did.”

“What are you talking about?”

“Before we came here, right before we got yanked away from that memory by the creek -- did something, sort of, call you here?  Did you feel a pull, like something was guiding you here?”

“Yeah,” Agatha said, shrugging, “I thought that was you, taking us to the next memory.”

“No, it wasn’t me,” Rio said, “. . . It was her.”  She gestured toward the old lady.  

Agatha looked at Rio with widened eyes.  “What are you saying?”

“You took my power and lived.”

“Well, not so much the second time.”

“But the first time.  You absorbed it, but I stopped it from killing you.”

“So you’re saying. . . I took your powers?”

“How else would you explain this, Agatha?  We both got pulled into a reaping by a departed soul, you can see the bridge that usually only I can see . . .”

“Wait a minute,” Agatha said, waving her hands in front of her.  “I absorbed your magic hundreds of years ago.  Why wouldn’t this have happened before?”

“I don’t know.  Maybe because you weren’t aware of it before?  Maybe because you absorbed it a second time?  Maybe because you died for real?”

“Maybe it’s the Darkhold.”

“You had that book almost as long as you had my magic in you.”

“But I didn’t use it to unlock the Afterlife dimension until yesterday.”

“Maybe.  But this happened when we held hands.  Is that a coincidence?”

“How would the Afterlife dimension know we held hands?”

“I don’t know.”

“Well,” Agatha said, giving up for now on trying to figure it out.  “However it happened, you’ve really fucked up the Sacred Balance this time,” she said with a delighted grin.

“Oh, no, Agatha,” Rio said, panicking.  “You cannot interfere, please!”

Agatha scoffed.  “Well, if you refuse to do your job, someone has to.”

“I’ll go back,” Rio said.

“I’m sorry - louder, please?”

“I was always going to go back.  I just wanted -- “

“To punish me?”

“No.  Well, maybe a little.  I wanted to be --”

“Appreciated,” Agatha finished for her, correctly this time.  “I won’t interfere.  You think I want your job?  Your job is horrible.”

“It’s not all bad,” Rio said, and nudged Agatha with her elbow.  The elderly woman had found her own way to the bridge, and was standing at its apex, gazing across it with a look of pure wonder on her face.

She turned back and looked at Agatha and Rio.  

“Is my Elizabeth in there?” she asked.  “I think -- I think I can feel her.”

“Yes,” Agatha answered, and then turned to Rio in shock.  “How did I know that??” she whispered.  

“I’ve been waiting four years to see her again,” the woman said.  “Can we go now?”

Rio nodded at the woman, and then looked expectantly at Agatha.

Agatha raised her eyebrows.  “Me?” she asked.  She stepped tentatively onto the bridge.  The woman waited for her to pass; Agatha stepped in front of her.  Then she led her across the threshold of the doorway to the Afterlife.  



Agatha had never had a clear vision of what she expected the Afterlife to be.  Truth be told, if she didn’t know Rio, she probably wouldn’t believe that one existed at all.  The Christian concepts of heaven and hell that she’d been steeped in as a child by virtue of existing in the time and place that she did, she’d always considered them nonsense.  She didn’t expect to see angels frolicking on puffy clouds or endless, sunny fields of flowers, nor a warm, white light emanating from some higher being (or his son).   She didn’t believe, either, that there was a place full of fire and brimstone, though she had made peace with the notion that there might be some sorting process based on one’s actions during life.  (As well as the fact that if that were true, she might not rise to the echelons of the best-behaved.)  She knew she had hopes for what it would not be:  a doorway to reincarnation, for one.  While the idea of another go-round or ten wasn’t entirely unappealing, she didn’t prefer to imagine living as anyone or anything besides herself.  A Buddhist-like nirvana also did not appeal -- that much peace and calm and lack of self sounded a crushingly boring way to spend eternity.  

 

Truthfully, Agatha doubted that any human culture actually had it right.  After all, she had more insight into the workings of Death than anyone in  human history, and still she knew vanishingly little.  She wasn’t allowed to.  The secret of the Afterlife was well-guarded.  But when you personally know a being who is tens of thousands of years old, you were forced to take a wide view, and Agatha had read enough to know that there were as many conceptions of the life after death as there were cultures in human history -- in other words, thousands upon thousands.  

 

The meager clues that she had gleaned from Rio over the years left her moderately confident in just two things:  that, one, something inherent to the individual is preserved, and two, at the very least, entrance bestowed some kind of understanding of. . . something.  Of what or whom, and to what degree, those were still mysteries that even knowing Rio couldn’t resolve.  Now, after having been an involuntary witness to so many of Rio’s past reapings, there was one more facet she was sure of:  reunion.  

 

 Agatha’s first glimpse of the actual Afterlife, the Death dimension that she’d been running from, threatened by, dying to understand, confirmed one thing for sure: she was right that nobody had it right.

 

The first revelation was that the place itself was physical, not simply a realm of the mind or spirit.  The ground was solid, the air was thick around them.  Second, it was dark; the sky and the land were black like the dead of night. In fact, she noticed the sky first. She could remember the view of the stars in the days before light pollution dulled the skies on Earth.  This sky was an order of magnitude more spectacular than that.  Stars of different sizes and luminosities and colors, tinged blue and red and yellow scattered thickly across the sky like glitter.  Nebulae, none of them familiar from Earthly books, exploded across the sky like fireworks.  

 

The land was every bit as dramatic as the sky.  Agatha’s mind searched for a way to comprehend it, searching for comparisons with sights she could remember, but there was nothing on Earth of this magnitude.  Perhaps, she thought, it was the Grand Canyon and the Tibetan Himalayas all at once. The black silhouettes of jagged peaks stretched as far upward as the canyons dropped below them.  And somehow all of it felt as unbounded as the open desert or the middle of the ocean.  

And all of it - from every towering peak, across every flat plateau and as far below the bridge as she could see, was covered with tiny points of light.  Agatha thought that there must be millions of them in every direction that she looked.  

And only when you looked at the ones closest to you could you see that the source of every point of light, was a tiny, flickering flame.  

They came in every color a human mind could imagine.  Smokeless and free of kindling beneath them, but brilliant.  

Besides Agatha, Rio, and the spirit of the woman who had called them here, there were no other people.  And yet Agatha could feel that she was surrounded by life.  

“They’re alive,” she whispered.  She understood: each flame burned with the energy of a human soul.  

The old woman drew them in to one in particular.  It was brilliantly white.

“She’s here,” the woman whispered, and then knelt beside it in the soft, black soil.  She held out her hands to its warmth, closed her eyes  and said, “I’ve missed you.”

Before their eyes, the woman’s soul became translucent and faded away.  But the flame she had whispered to now burned a dazzling sky blue.  

“She joined her?” Agatha murmured, a question she already knew the answer to.  

Rio nodded.

“And they’ll spend eternity together like this?”

“I don’t know,” Rio said.  “I’ve only been around a hundred thousand years.  Eternity is a lot longer than that.”

“Where is he?” Agatha asked.  “Wait -- never mind.”

And now they were high atop a mountain.  Not all the way up, but close.  

“Purple,” Agatha breathed, staring at the small flame at her feet.

“He chose it,” Rio said. 

Agatha knelt beside Nicky’s flame.  She blinked tears down her cheeks.  

“I can feel him,” she said.  “As real as if he was in my arms.”

“Someday you can join him,” Rio said.  “And anyone else you want to join. Your life energy is limitless here.  Some of these flames are families.  Covens, even.  You can join anyone you loved.”

“There isn’t anybody else,” Agatha said.  

She closed her eyes and held her hands out to feel the warmth of the purple flame.  She had longed to feel Nicky’s love again for so, so long.  

“Maybe I should stay,” she said.  “Maybe it’s time.”

“Yeah,” Rio said quietly, “You can stay if you want to.”

Agatha opened her eyes.  “What about you?”

Rio understood Agatha’s question.  “I don’t have a human soul,” she said.

Agatha stood to face Rio.  Looked into her eyes.  “How can that possibly be true?” she asked.

“It’s true,” Rio said.  

“So you can’t. . .join?”

Rio shook her head.

“Not even the part of you that’s not Death -- that’s Rio?”

“I can’t separate the parts of me.”

Agatha turned, and knelt down at Nicky’s flame again.

“I’ll visit you soon, my baby,” she said.  

The flame flickered wildly in response.  

“Be happy, Mama.”  Nicky’s voice wasn’t explicitly audible, but Agatha could perceive it with every cell of her body.  

She smiled even as the tears streamed down her face.  

“I’ll try,” she said.  “I promise.”

She stood again to face Rio, and wiped her tears away with the back of her hands.

“So,” she said, “Now that I’ve got access to the restricted Deathly VIP booths of the world, how about you give me the address of the place in the Void where you conjure up these human bodies you’re always wearing?”

“Are they coming back?”

Billy put his hands on his thighs, extended his arms, and exhaled.  “Okay.  I think I’m ready to update the team,” he said.

“But will they be back?” May asked, after Billy described the memory spell to the team.  “And when?  We’re still dealing with the same crisis, and unfortunately for literally everyone, those two are our only hope.”

Their conversation was interrupted by a voice coming from the basement stairs.

She sent a beam of green streaming at a potted plant sitting on the basement windowsill.  It withered and turned to dust, and a new one immediately grew in its place, bursting with purple flowers.  

“What, Agatha?  How do you have Green Magic now?” Billy asked.

“Oh, that’s right,” Agatha said, “I forgot, I have one more update.”

Suddenly, lightning bolts flashed in the room, accompanied by a long rumble of thunder.  In the bright, unearthly light, Agatha’s face appeared skeletal -- deathly.  She held out her arms and let a conjured wind whip through her hair and her robes.

“I have Death powers now,” she said.

Chapter Summary

Agatha decides what's next for her, with respect to Rio and the general direction of her life. Maybe she even has friends?

 

Westview, 2024

She wiped her eyes, smiling in delight.

The team stared at her.

“What, is that so hard to understand?”

“Sure I’m sure.  I already reaped my first soul.  And yes, I’ve been to the Afterlife, and no, I can’t tell you what it’s like.  It’s not allowed.”

“I can bend some of them,” Agatha agreed.  “And by the way, Rio pretends to be by the book, but she does it, too.  For example, she let your mother stay on Earth as a ghost for years.  Until you came back from the Blip.”

“Maria believed that you and all the other Blipped people would come back.  When you did, she was there.  She saw you get promoted, get powers, and help end Wanda’s little torture town.  When she was at peace, she crossed the bridge.  You’ll see her when you get there.”

Monica pressed her fingers to her lips, her eyes welling with tears.  Agatha turned to May.

“Andrew says he loves you.”

Then, to Daisy.

“Lincoln says ‘no regrets’.”

Then to all of them she said, “And Phil says not to miss him.  He’s in a magical place - for real this time.  Whatever that means.”

 

For the second time in as many minutes, all of the agents found themselves staring aghast at Agatha.

 

“I can’t believe you got all of those names right,” Billy said incredulously.  “So, um, did my -- did Wanda say anything?  Do you have a message for me?”

As Agatha turned to walk back up the stairs, all the agents spoke at once.

“Hold on a minute, we still have so many questions!” Monica said.  

“Yes, and could we have the Darkhold back, please?” Jemma asked. 

“Are you and Rio still fighting?”  Daisy added. 

“Are you going to help her reap souls?” May wanted to know.  

Agatha turned back around to face them, and pointed at each agent as she answered their question.

“No, that book stays with me.  None of your business.  And no.  I’m too human to do her job.  Also, it sucks. Plus, I have other priorities.”

“Respectfully, Ms. Harkness, or Mrs. Death, or whatever title you prefer now,” Monica said, “We think it’s best to keep our operation active for the time being.  We need to be sure this crisis is truly over before we leave the premises.”

“Agreed,” May said.  “We need to ensure Rio doesn’t stop working again.”

“There’s nothing you can do if she does,” Agatha told her with a smirk.  “There never was. I was always the only one who could fix it.”

And with that, Agatha conjured a swirl of black, green, and purple magic, and disappeared.  



The team exchanged looks full of dismay, confusion, and skepticism. 

“Do we trust her?” Monica asked.

“Of course not,” May answered.  “Is this really the best we can do?  Having these two to be in charge of the circle of life?”

“And what about Agatha’s new powers?” Daisy added.  “She can’t directly kill people anymore, but she’s basically an immortal shapeshifter now.”

“And what did she mean by ‘other priorities’?” Jemma asked.

“Right?!” Daisy agreed,  “That was ominous as fuck.”

“I’m sure that’s just Agatha being dramatic,” Billy interjected.

“We can’t just let her go free, can we?” May asked.

“Technically, she didn’t do anything wrong this time,” Monica said.

“I’m sure we’re well within the statute of limitations for a few hundred offenses,” May said.

“She did technically save the world though,” Daisy said. 

“After she broke it,” May argued.

“That was Rio’s choice, not Agatha’s,” Billy said.  

“Well,” Monica said, “If she wants us off her property -- and granted I’m using the term ‘her’ very loosely -- there isn’t much legal basis for us to insist on staying.  Maybe we can move the base to the town outskirts again.”

“Guys,” Daisy said with a sigh, “I’m just going to say it.  We’re talking about a couple of immortal witches who can transport themselves anywhere in the world with a snap of their fingers.  I don’t see how planting a military base anywhere in Westview, New Jersey is going to help us with any of this.”

“She’s right,” May said begrudgingly.  “But the situation is still volatile until proven otherwise.  We don’t know if this mission is over.”

“So what do we do?” Monica asked.  

No one had a ready answer to that question.  They fell silent, everyone deep in thought.

“Oh,” Jemma said suddenly, tapping her chin with her fingertips in excitement.  

“Spill,” Daisy said, well-versed in the signs that Jemma was gearing up for some incoming brilliance.

“I may have an idea that’s win-win for all of us,” she said, staring straight ahead as the wheels turned.  “Yes, I think that could work,” she said, mostly to herself.  Then she lifted her eyes to meet the team’s expectant gaze.    

“Daisy is right.  We can’t possibly keep track of Agatha and Rio when they’re out there in the world.  We don’t know enough about magic to be able to track it, let alone interact with it.  But what if -- what if we gave Agatha a reason to keep coming to us?  To Shield?”

“But Agatha doesn’t go anywhere she doesn’t want to,” Monica said.  “How do you get her to want to spend time at Shield?”

“Simple,” Jemma said, smiling, “We offer her the two things she can never get enough of: knowledge and power.”

By the time twilight descended on Westview, New Jersey, Shield and Sword had packed up their operations at Agatha’s house and moved their small base to the outskirts of town.  The Agents spent most of the evening watching the news --  across the world, a massive humanitarian effort was mobilizing to help manage both the logistics and the repercussions of a sudden twenty-fold increase in the number of human deaths.  

Death herself, for whom human time was only a suggestion, had no cause to hurry.  She would attend to each soul in due time.  After the first few hundred thousand crossings, Death took a break.

Rio knocked on Agatha’s door.

“Well, woman of the hour,” Agatha said.  “I hope you don’t expect me to fawn all over you like the rest of the world.”

“Yes, I have met you, Agatha,” Rio said. “Can I come in?”

Agatha was puzzled for a beat.  “It’s not like you to ask.”

“So, are you planning to stay and live here?” Rio asked.  “If so, you really need to redecorate.”

Agatha squelched the flames in her palms, and the fire in her eyes went out as well.  She slumped back down onto the couch.

“We should have been grieving together,” she said, “Instead of apart.”

Agatha nodded.

“I don’t think I know,” Agatha said.  “I feel like my head isn’t going to be clear for years after all that.”

They looked into each other’s eyes over their accidental four cups of coffee.  For once, the weight in the air between them felt more like possibility than pain.   

“Speaking of things to show you,” Agatha said, and got up from the table.

She retrieved a pile of mail from the living room coffee table, rifled through it, and tossed one large envelope onto the kitchen table in front of Rio.

“From Shield?” Rio asked, puzzled.

“Look inside,” Agatha directed her.

“Scientific Basis of Magical and Interdimensional Phenomena:  A SHIELD Initiative”

A red stamp below the title identified the document as Classified.  

“It’s a job offer,” Agatha said.

“They want you to join Shield??”

“Mmhmm, the Science Division.  They want to figure out how magic works.  Physically.  Biologically.  Including yours -- well, ours.”

“Agatha,” Rio said, eyes wide, “You HAVE to do it.”

“Do I?”

“Yes!  This is what you’ve always wanted!”

“Is it?”

“Agatha, you’ve been amassing knowledge about magic for centuries.  Isn’t this your chance to finally, fully understand it?”

“Ehhh,” Agatha said, “Maybe.”

“Okay, real talk, Agatha -- what else are you doing with your time right now?”

“What am I doing?” Agatha scoffed, “I’m doing plenty.  I’m finding Wanda Fucking Maximoff, for one thing.”

“Agatha, I know you have a score to settle, but don’t dismiss this opportunity so fast.  Who knows, maybe they can even help you.  You can’t be the only one who wants to figure out where the hell the soul of the Scarlet Witch is hiding.”

“I don’t know.  I work better alone.”

“Are you sure about that?  You work better without government resources?  Classified information?  Access to powerful people?”

Agatha pursed her lips.

“Plus, you know,” Rio added hesitantly, “It might be good for you to have a group around you.  It might even be good for us.”

“Hmm,” Agatha said, sipping her coffee.  “Always trying to get me to have a coven.”

“Promise me you’ll think about it,” Rio said.

“All right,” Agatha said, “I’ll think about it.”  



*****

Three days later, Agents May, Johnson, Rambeau, and Dr. Simmons knocked on Agatha’s front door.  A quinjet idled in her driveway.  Again.

“Ready?” Agent May asked when Agatha opened her door, Rio standing behind her.  Never one to miss an opportunity to dress fabulously when the occasion demanded it, Agatha wore a tailored black suit that she had absolutely definitely not stolen from a designer shop in the city yesterday.  

“Almost,” Agatha said, holding tightly onto the packet of paperwork that sealed her employment for SHIELD, even as Agent May reached for it.  “I have a few conditions of my own.”

Agent May’s eyes were covered with dark sunglasses, but somehow it was still clear that she had rolled them.

“Go ahead,” she said.

“First of all, I will not be a guinea pig.  No experiments ON me, only BY me.”

“Oh, not to worry,” Jemma assured her, “Any experiments on your magic will be completely on your terms, Agent Harkness.”

“Agent Harkness,” Agatha repeated to herself.  “Huh.  Second condition:  I will not help you build a witch registry.  As much as I would personally like to know where to find them all, that’s just asking for another set of, shall we say, Trials.”

“Oh, no,” Daisy said, “We don’t do registries.  Uh, anymore.”

“Third,” Agatha continued.  “I will not fight in any wars for Shield.  I don’t care if it's aliens or rogue superheroes or kaiju or whatever.  I’m a pacifist.  Plus, I”ve seen what happens to your female avengers.  First Natasha, then Wanda, then Maria Hill.” She pointed at Monica.  “Her “aunt” Carol wound up a crazy cat lady in the distant corners of space.  No, thank you.”

“Wow, you really know your female superheroes,” Daisy remarked.  

“Powerful women are kind of a special interest.”

“Well, we’re all pretty powerful, and we’re still standing,” Daisy said.

“Haven’t all of you died at least twice?  Or, at least been stuck in a parallel universe or a murder planet?”

“That’s. . . true. . .” Daisy admitted.  “But, now, how many times did you die, again?”

“Enough to last me a while,” Agatha said.  

“Not to worry, Agent Harkness,” Jemma was quick to reassure her.  “Think of yourself like the Peggy Carter of the Magic Initiative.  More than capable of brawn, but mostly focused on being the brains and the leadership.”

Agatha got a faraway look in her eye.  “Oh, Peggy,” she said fondly.  “I haven’t thought of her in decades.” 

Rio glared at her.  

As Jemma and Daisy exchanged a look Agatha said, “I know what you’re thinking, and yes, I did shoot my shot.  She was too hung up on some waitress at the time.  Her loss.”

“Wow,” Daisy said, elbowing Jemma, whose jaw was dropped halfway open, “Seems like maybe you would’ve had a shot after all.”

“You shush,” Jemma whispered fiercely.

“Next time we time travel, I’ll be your wingwoman,” Daisy said.  “Deny it all you want, I know she’s your hall pass.  Oh, but we cannot tell Daniel.  Seriously, he’s not invited.”

“Anywaaay,” May said, drawing their attention back to the matter at hand.  “We agree to all of your conditions.  You have to sign the forms before you can get on the jet.”

Agatha signed, and handed the forms to May.  

“You’ll send a team for all my books?”

“Tomorrow morning,” May confirmed.  “You’ll meet us here?” she asked Rio.

“Yes, ma’am.”  Rio saluted May and ran her tongue across the inside of her bottom lip.  This time it was Agatha’s turn to glare.

 

Before Rio could flirt any further, a blue Subaru screeched to a halt in front of the house.  Billy slammed the car door and came running through Agatha’s front yard.  He stood in front of Agatha, breathing hard.

“What is this?  Don’t you have high school?” Agatha said.

“I wanted to say good bye,” he said, and threw his arms around her.  “And good luck.”

Agatha hugged him back.  Tentatively at first. . . then for real.

“I’m proud of you,” Billy said.  “Just don’t forget to look for Tommy.  You promised.”

“I did,” Agatha agreed.

Billy smiled, and stepped back.

“Teen, wait,” Agatha said.

“What is it?” he asked.

“Don’t tell them,” Agatha said.  “Your parents.  Find another way to explain your powers.”

“Really?”

“Really.  Look, when you took over their son’s body, you spared them the worst pain anybody with kids can imagine: you stopped them from losing a child.  Don’t take him away from them now.”

“Agatha, are you sure?  Isn’t it better that they know the truth?”

“Someday they will,” Agatha said.  “And trust me, by then it won’t matter.  They’ll love you both.”

“Okay,” Billy said, nodding.  “Okay.  I’ll figure something out.”

 

Billy stepped down off the porch.  It was Rio’s turn to say goodbye.  

The kiss, and the accompanying noises, went on an uncomfortably long time for everyone standing there witnessing it.

May cleared her throat, loudly.

Rio winked at her.  “Jealous?” she teased. 

“No,” May said flatly.

Rio blew Agatha a kiss.  “Te veo,” she said, and then disappeared.  

 

As they walked toward the jet, Daisy and Jemma flanked Agatha.

“Sooo, seems like the reunion is going pretty well,” Daisy said, grinning.

“Will we be seeing a lot of Ms. Vidal around the base?” Jemma asked.  

Agatha’s cheeks darkened.  “I -- I don’t know, if she wants to, I guess maybe.”

“Oh, she does,” Daisy said.

“That’s an affirmative,” Jemma agreed.  

 

**

 

Agatha wasn’t sure what she expected when she stepped aboard a Shield vehicle for the first time.

But she definitely wasn’t expecting to see Jennifer Kale sitting there waiting for her.

“What -- “ she said,  and whipped her head around looking for someone to interrogate.  “Why -- Is this a prank?”

“Is there a problem, Agent Harkness?” Monica asked. 

“What is SHE doing here?” Agatha asked, pointing at Jen.

“See, I told you she wouldn’t join if she saw me.  Didn’t I tell you to get the paperwork signed first?” Jen said.  “Always nice to see you, Agatha.”

Agatha scoffed.  “So, what?  Do they just let anyone in Shield now?”

“You’re not the only one with hundreds of years of magical knowledge to contribute, Agatha.”

“So long as no one asks you to make a broom with them, right Gwynnie?”

“Oh my god, that was four HUNDRED years ago!”

“What are you even doing here?” Agatha shouted, “ Shouldn’t you be out maiming and defrauding the public?”

“Shouldn’t YOU?”

Jemma interrupted timidly.  “Agent Kale will actually have her own branch of research within the Initiative.”

Agatha looked expectantly at Jen.  

Jen sighed.  “I want to figure out how binding works so I can learn to undo it.  Not all the bound witches out there know who their binder was, or. . . have access to their hair.”

“Hmm, how noble of you,” Agatha said.   “Just stay out of my way, I have my own priorities.”

“Of course you do,” Jen sighed.  “I might need a bunch of your hair, though.”

“Probably,” Agatha said. 

 

Daisy helped them get buckled in for takeoff.  

“Oh god, how long is this flight?” Agatha complained.  “Do I have to be strapped down next to her for the whole thing?”

Nobody answered Agatha.  The jet lifted off, and they were roughly pushed backwards into their seats as it accelerated dramatically towards their destination.  As their altitude leveled off, May’s voice from the cockpit let them know they were free to move about the aircraft.  Agatha unbuckled, and moved to get up immediately.

“Agatha, wait,” Jen said, and grabbed her forearm.

“What now?” Agatha demanded.

“As much as it pains me to admit it, we’re going to be working together, so.  I owe you an apology, and I’d rather get it overwith.”

Agatha sat down again.  “I’m listening.”

“I wanted to say, I’m sorry for buying into the rumors.  About you and about Nicky.  I guess I just thought because you were so willing to kill witches for power, you were capable of worse.  Billy filled me in on what really happened to him.  I’m sorry.”

“So glad you two are besties now,” Agatha said.  

“He’s a good kid,” Jen said. “He’s the one who put me in touch with Shield Science.”

“That he is,” Agatha agreed.  She closed her eyes and breathed in deeply through her nose, gathering her strength.  

“I suppose,” she said, “That I didn’t give you any reason not to think the worst.  And for whatever it’s worth, I don’t hate you because of the broom thing.  I mean, I do, but that’s not the main reason.”

“Okaaay,” Jen said.  “So, what’s the main reason?  Do enlighten me, I can’t wait to hear it.”

“Rio wanted to come and get you the night Nicky was born.  I wouldn’t let her.  I thought I could handle it myself.”

“Ah.  So, I’m a regret.  That makes sense.  Well, all we can do is move forward, right?  Look at it this way, I’m sure we’ll find brand new reasons to hate each other.”

“Bet on it.”

They fell into an awkward silence.  Every muscle in Agatha’s body was locked and loaded to spring her from this chair and find a dark corner away from any further conversation.  Something kept her seated.

“I’m sorry I bound you,” she said.  “Although let me reiterate, I did not know it was you.”

“Would that have stopped you?” Jen asked. 

“Yes.  You’re a midwife,” Agatha said.  “Maybe.  No -- I did hate you.  Oh well, guess we’ll never know the truth.”

“Okay, well, apology accepted.  I guess.”

Silence descended again.

“I’m pregnant,” Jen said.  Agatha’s eyebrows shot upwards and her eyes widened.

“And,” Jen continued, “Now that you and your situationship have stopped jeopardizing all life on Earth, she can finally start growing in there.”

“Conjured?” Agatha asked.  

“No.  Agatha, there’s modern medicine now.  I used a donor and an IVF clinic.”

“All right, well.  Mazel Tov.”

“I’ve wanted to do it for a long time, but I was in so much legal trouble it wasn’t a real possibility.  Going on the Road and getting my magic back is what made me finally decide to do it.”

Monica, Jemma, and Daisy approached Agatha and Jen.

“Did we overhear?” Jemma asked.  “You shared the good news?”

“I know we haven’t known you very long,” Daisy said to Jen, “But I just want you to know that Jemma and I are excellent aunties.”

“Yes, we’ve had much practice with each other’s little ones.  In a few years, Alya will be old enough to babysit.  Can you believe it?”

"I really can’t,” Daisy said.

The conversation continued next to her.  Agatha stood, walked a few paces away to an empty seat, and took out her phone.  She texted Rio.




A:  Jen’s pregnant

R:  It wasn’t me

 

Agatha sent Rio the green-faced nausea emoji. 

 

A:  Stop

A:  These people talk so much

 

R:  They’re nice.  Even I don’t want to kill them

R:  Maybe you should talk back

 

A:  Ehh.  Idk if i trust them

 

R:  Did you try?

 

A:  No.

 

R:  Follow your gut, Agatha.  I gotta go.  Huge backlog at work.

 

A:  Whose fault is that

 

R:  Shut up.  Let me know what it’s like when you get there

 

A:  K

 

R:  Be good, Agatha

 

A:  We’ll see.

A:  Te veo.  



Agatha put her phone away.  She sighed.  She took a long look at the empty seat next to her.  She had the urge to stretch out her legs, lean back, and spend the flight scheming her infiltration of the highest levels of the halls of power at Shield.

Then she turned her head.  She took another long look, this time at the group chatting on the other side of the plane.  

She stood up.

“All right,” she said, approaching them (and not much caring about whatever conversation she was interrupting).  “Fine, since someone has to do it,  I’ll be the Cool Aunt.  And the Smart one.  And the Hot one.”

Daisy, Jemma, Monica, and even Jen laughed at her.  

She sat down next to them. 

“Just, please God, do NOT invite me to the baby shower.”
